Divine Transcendence
advanced
8 min
Proof That All Bodies Are Equal in Essence
Imam Razi proves that all bodies are equal in essence by logical division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's proof that if God were a body, His essence would be equal to all bodies, thus refuting anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would be identical to every other body.

Imam Razi presents a decisive proof that all spatial beings (bodies) are equal in their complete essence. He uses a logical division argument to show that any distinction would collapse, leading to the conclusion that God cannot be a body. This lesson walks through the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the formal structure of proof by division.
The passage is dense – take your time.
2
Identify the two components
Look for the two aspects: spatiality (mutahayyiziyya) shared by all bodies, and particularity (khussusiyya) unique to each. The argument assumes God as body would have both.
3
Follow the first division
If the essence is spatiality and particularity is an attribute, then God's essence is merely spatiality – making Him identical to all bodies. This yields the desired conclusion.
This is the straight path.
4
Examine the second division
If spatiality is an attribute and particularity is the essence, the particularity must either have a location or not. Both options lead to impossibility or ultimately to the same equality.
Pay attention to the sub-argument about spatiality requiring direction.
5
Conclude the proof
Both divisions force the conclusion: all spatial beings are equal in complete quiddity. Thus, God cannot be spatial.
This is a qati' (decisive) proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لسائرها فى المتحيزية ، ومخالفاً لها فى الخصوصية : كان ما به الاشتراك مغايراً - لا محالة ـ لما به الامتياز. وحينئذ يكون عموم المتحيزية مغايراً لخصوص ذاته المخصوصة . وحينئذ نقول : إما أن تكون الذات هي المتحيزية ، وتكون تلك الخصوصية صفة لتلك الذات ، وإما أن يقال : المتحيزية صفة ، وتلك الخصوصية هي الذات. أما القسم الأول فإنه يقتضى حصول المقصود . لأنه إذا كان مجرد المتحيزية هو الذات ، وثبت أن مجرد المتحيزية أمر مشترك فيه بينه وبين سائر المتحيزات ، فحينئذ يحصل منه أن بتقدير أن يكون تعالى متحيزاً ، تكون ذاته مماثلة لذوات سائر المتحيزات . وليس المطلوب إلا ذلك . وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال : الذات الخصوصية ، والصفة هى المتحيزية . فنقول : هذا محال . وذلك لأن تلك الخصوصية من حيث إنها هي هي ، مع قطع النظر عن المتحيزية . إما أن يكون لها اختصاص بالخبز[؟] ، وإما أن لا يكون لها ذلك (٤) والأول محال . لأن كل ما كان حاصلا في حيز وجهة على سبيل الاستقلال ، كان متحيزاً . فلو كانت تلك الخصوصية التي فرضناها خالية عن التحيز ، حاصلة فى الحيز ، لسكان الخالى عن التحيز متحيزاً . وذلك محال . وأما القسم الثانى وهو أن يقال : إن تلك الخصوصية غير مختصة بشيء من الأحياز والجهات . فنقول : إنه يمتنع أن تكون المتحيزة صفة قائمة بها ، لأن تلك الخصوصية غير مختصة بشيء من الأحياز والجهات ، والمتحيزية أمر لا يعقل إلا أن يكون حاصلا) (ه) في الجهات . والشيء الذي يجب أن يكون حاصلا في الجهات ، يمتنع أن يكون حاصلا في الشيء الذي يمتنع حصوله في الجهة . وإذا لم تكن المتحيزية صفة للشيء ، كانت نفس الذات . وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون الأشياء المتساوية في المتحيزية ، متساوية في (تمام) (۳) الذات . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن المتحيرات يجب أن تكون كلها متساوية فى تمام الماهية ، وهذا برهان قاطع في تقرير هذه المقدمة
For all other things in being spatial (mutahayyiziyya), and differing from them in particularity (khussusiyya): that by which there is sharing is necessarily different from that by which there is distinction. Then the generality of spatiality is different from the particularity of its specific essence. Then we say: either the essence is the spatiality, and that particularity is an attribute of that essence, or it is said: spatiality is an attribute, and that particularity is the essence. As for the first division, it necessitates the attainment of the objective. Because if mere spatiality is the essence, and it is established that mere spatiality is something shared between Him and other spatial beings, then it results from that that assuming that the Exalted is spatial, His essence would be similar to the essences of other spatial beings. And nothing else is required. As for the second division, which is to say: the essence is the particularity, and the attribute is spatiality. We say: this is impossible. That is because that particularity, in itself, apart from consideration of spatiality—either it has a connection to a place (bal-khubz[?]), or it does not have that [connection] (4). The first is impossible, because everything that exists in a spatial location and direction independently is spatial. So if that particularity which we assumed to be free from spatiality existed in a spatial location, then that which is free from spatiality would be spatial—that is impossible. As for the second division, which is to say: that particularity is not connected to any spatial location or direction. We say: it is impossible for spatiality to be an attribute inhering in it, because that particularity is not connected to any spatial location or direction, and spatiality is inconceivable except as existing in directions. And something that must exist in directions is impossible to exist in something that is impossible to exist in a direction. And if spatiality is not an attribute of the thing, it is the essence itself. Then it follows that things equal in spatiality are equal in (complete) essence (3). So it is established by what we have mentioned: that spatial beings must all be equal in complete quiddity (tamam al-mahiyya), and this is a decisive proof in establishing this premise.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a proof by division (qism) aiming to show that all bodies (mutahayyizat) are necessarily equal in their complete essence. The author first sets up a hypothetical: if God were a body, there would be two components in Him—spatiality (mutahayyiziyya) shared with all bodies, and a particularity (khussusiyya) that distinguishes Him. These two must be distinct. Then he divides: either the essence is the spatiality and the particularity is an attribute, or the spatiality is an attribute and the particularity is the essence. The first division directly yields the desired result: if the essence is merely spatiality, then any body's essence is identical to that common spatiality—so God's essence would be like all bodies. The second division is refuted by considering the particularity apart from spatiality. It either has a location (which would itself be spatial) or does not. If it has no location, then spatiality cannot inhere in it (since spatiality requires direction), so spatiality must be the essence itself, again leading to equality. Thus all bodies are equal in complete quiddity. The proof is presented as a decisive (qati') demonstration of the premise that God cannot be a body.

Key Terms
متحيزية (mutahayyiziyya)
خصوصية (khussusiyya)
تمام الماهية (tamam al-mahiyya)
ذات (dhat)
صفة (sifa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that particularity (the distinguishing feature) could be the essence even if it has no location, and spatiality could still be an attribute.
Correction: The proof shows that if particularity has no location, spatiality cannot inhere in it because spatiality requires direction. Thus spatiality must be the essence itself, leading to equality.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi conclude from the division argument about all spatial beings?
A) They are all different in their particularities.
B) They are all equal in complete quiddity.
C) They are all created by God.
D) They are all material substances.

Key Takeaway
All bodies share the same essence of spatiality, so a spatial God would be like any other body—this is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously imagined Allah as being in a specific direction, like above the sky? How does this proof challenge that assumption, and what step will you take to purify your conception of tawhid today?
?
If every body shares the same complete essence, as the proof concludes, then what distinguishes your existence from a mere rock? How does this realization affect your gratitude for the distinct soul and consciousness Allah gave you?
?
The proof shows that any particularity you use to differentiate bodies reduces to spatiality itself. What does this teach you about the danger of attributing any spatial qualities to Allah, even in metaphorical language? What will you change in your speech or thoughts about Allah?

Proof 1: From Necessary Properties of Similar Things
Razi presents the first proof from the principle of al-mutamathilayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that God's essence cannot be similar to bodies, based on the principle that similar things share all necessary properties.

Core Idea
If God were like bodies, either God would be temporal or bodies eternal.

Imam Razi begins by laying down a fundamental rule of kalam: two things that are alike in essence must be alike in all their necessary properties. He then applies this rule to the relationship between God and bodies to demonstrate impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Get a feel for the logical flow.
Notice the concise style of Razi.
2
Understand the rule of al-mutamathilayn
The rule states that similar things share all necessary properties (lawazim). This is a key axiom in kalam.
3
Follow the logical consequence
If God were similar to bodies, their necessary properties would match. This leads to a contradiction: either God becomes temporal or bodies become eternal.
Both options are impossible given known truths.
4
Reflect on the impossibility
The conclusion is that God cannot be similar to bodies in essence. This establishes divine transcendence.
This proof is foundational for understanding tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن تكون ذات الله تعالى مساوية لذوات الأجسام في تمام الماهية لوجوه : الأول : إن من حكم المتماثلين : الاستواء في جميع اللوازم . فيلزم من قدم ذات الله تعالى، قدم سائر الأجسام، أو من حدوث سائر الأجسام، حدوث ذات الله تعالى . وذلك محال . الثاني : إن المثلين يجب استواؤهما في جميع اللوازم . وكما صح على سائر الأجسام خلوها عن صفة العلم والقدرة والحياة ، وجب أن يصح على ذاته الخلو عن هذه الصفات، وحينئذ يكون اتصاف ذاته بحياته وعلمه وقدرته ، من الجائزات . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع كون تلك الذات موصوفاً بالحياة والعلم والقدرة ، إلا بإيجاد موجد ، وتخصيص مخصص. وذلك يقتضى احتياجه إلى الإله . وحينئذ كل ما كان جسماً كان محتاجاً إلى الإله . وهذا يقتضى أن الإله يمتنع أن يكون جسماً . الثالث : إنه لما كان ذاته تعالى مساوية لذوات سائر المتحيزات ، ع في سائر المتحيزات كونها متحركة تارة وساكنة أخرى ، وجب أن تكون ذاته أيضاً كذلك . وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم أن تكون ذاته تعالى قابلة للحركة والسكون . وكل ما كان كذلك ، وجب القول بكونه محدثاً
And we only said: it is impossible that the essence of God Most High be equal to the essences of bodies in complete quiddity, for several reasons: The first: Indeed, from the rule of the two similar things (al-mutamathilayn) is equality in all necessary properties (lawazim). So it would follow from the eternity of the essence of God Most High the eternity of all other bodies, or from the temporality of all other bodies the temporality of the essence of God Most High. And that is impossible. The second: Indeed, the two similar things must be equal in all necessary properties. And just as it is established for all other bodies that they are devoid of the attributes of knowledge, power, and life, it would be necessary that His essence be validly devoid of these attributes. Then the description of His essence with His life, knowledge, and power would be among contingent (ja'izat) matters. And if that is the case, it is impossible for that essence to be described with life, knowledge, and power except by the bringing into existence of an existing thing (mujid) and the specification of a specifier (mukhasis). That necessitates its need for the divine (ilah). Then everything that is a body is in need of the divine. And this necessitates that the divine cannot be a body. The third: Indeed, since His essence Most High is equal to the essences of other spatial beings, and it is established [صح?] for other spatial beings that they are sometimes moving and sometimes at rest, it would be necessary that His essence also be thus. And on this assumption, it follows that His essence Most High is susceptible to motion and rest. And everything that is thus, it is necessary to say that it is temporally originated (muhdath).
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents three independent proofs (wujuh) that God's essence cannot be similar to the essences of bodies. The first proof invokes the kalam principle of al-mutamathilayn (two similar things): if two things are similar in essence, they must share all necessary properties (lawazim). If God's essence were like a body's, then either God's eternity would entail the eternity of bodies (impossible, as bodies are contingent), or bodies' temporality would entail God's temporality (also impossible, as God is eternal). Thus similarity is impossible. The second proof applies the same principle to attributes: if God were like bodies, He could be devoid of knowledge, power, and life just as bodies are; but God's attributes are essential, not contingent, so His possession of them would require an external cause, making Him dependent—contradicting divinity. The third proof argues from motion and rest: bodies are subject to change, so if God were like a body, He would be subject to motion and rest, implying temporality (muhdath). All three proofs aim to establish divine transcendence (tanzih) over bodily attributes.

Key Terms
متماثلان
لوازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think similarity in essence could coexist with difference in some attributes.
Correction: The rule of al-mutamathilayn demands equality in all necessary properties, so even partial similarity is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, if God were similar to bodies, what would be the consequence?
A) Both would be eternal.
B) Both would be temporal.
C) Either God would be temporal or bodies eternal.
D) Both would be contingent.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that similarity in essence would force an impossible consequence, so God cannot be like bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever unconsciously imagine God as having a form or being located somewhere? How does Razi's argument challenge that subtle assumption? What specific step will you take today to purify your concept of God?
?
Razi says that if God were like anything else, then the logical consequences would be impossible. Does your worship reflect a deep conviction that God is utterly unique and beyond comparison? Are there any ways you treat God as if He were like other beings you know? Write one concrete change you will make in how you address or think about God.
?
If God is not similar to anything, then His names and attributes must be understood in a way befitting His majesty. Is there an attribute of God that you tend to think of in human terms (e.g., anger, love, power)? How will you adjust your understanding to affirm that it is not like human attributes?

Proof 2: From Contingent Attributes
Razi presents the second proof from contingency of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that God cannot be like bodies: if He were, His essential attributes would become contingent, requiring a creator.

Core Idea
If God were like bodies, His attributes would be contingent, making Him dependent on another.

The second proof extends the rule of similarity to attributes. Just as bodies lack knowledge, power, and life, so too would God if He were like them. But God possesses these attributes necessarily; if they were contingent, He would need a cause, contradicting His divinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully.
This proof is longer and more detailed.
2
Identify the rule applied to attributes
The same rule of similarity requires that if God and bodies are alike, they must share the possibility of lacking certain attributes.
3
Trace the argument that attributes become contingent
If God could lack life, knowledge, and power, then His actual possession of them becomes contingent (ja'iz), not necessary.
Contingent things require a cause.
4
Understand the conclusion: God cannot be a body
If God's attributes need a cause, then God needs a creator, which contradicts His being the divine (ilah). Therefore, God cannot be a body.
This argument shows that divinity and bodily nature are incompatible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن تكون ذات الله تعالى مساوية لذوات الأجسام في تمام الماهية لوجوه : الأول : إن من حكم المتماثلين : الاستواء في جميع اللوازم . فيلزم من قدم ذات الله تعالى، قدم سائر الأجسام، أو من حدوث سائر الأجسام، حدوث ذات الله تعالى . وذلك محال . الثاني : إن المثلين يجب استواؤهما في جميع اللوازم . وكما صح على سائر الأجسام خلوها عن صفة العلم والقدرة والحياة ، وجب أن يصح على ذاته الخلو عن هذه الصفات، وحينئذ يكون اتصاف ذاته بحياته وعلمه وقدرته ، من الجائزات . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع كون تلك الذات موصوفاً بالحياة والعلم والقدرة ، إلا بإيجاد موجد ، وتخصيص مخصص. وذلك يقتضى احتياجه إلى الإله . وحينئذ كل ما كان جسماً كان محتاجاً إلى الإله . وهذا يقتضى أن الإله يمتنع أن يكون جسماً . الثالث : إنه لما كان ذاته تعالى مساوية لذوات سائر المتحيزات ، ع في سائر المتحيزات كونها متحركة تارة وساكنة أخرى ، وجب أن تكون ذاته أيضاً كذلك . وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم أن تكون ذاته تعالى قابلة للحركة والسكون . وكل ما كان كذلك ، وجب القول بكونه محدثاً
And we only said: it is impossible that the essence of God Most High be equal to the essences of bodies in complete quiddity, for several reasons: The first: Indeed, from the rule of the two similar things (al-mutamathilayn) is equality in all necessary properties (lawazim). So it would follow from the eternity of the essence of God Most High the eternity of all other bodies, or from the temporality of all other bodies the temporality of the essence of God Most High. And that is impossible. The second: Indeed, the two similar things must be equal in all necessary properties. And just as it is established for all other bodies that they are devoid of the attributes of knowledge, power, and life, it would be necessary that His essence be validly devoid of these attributes. Then the description of His essence with His life, knowledge, and power would be among contingent (ja'izat) matters. And if that is the case, it is impossible for that essence to be described with life, knowledge, and power except by the bringing into existence of an existing thing (mujid) and the specification of a specifier (mukhasis). That necessitates its need for the divine (ilah). Then everything that is a body is in need of the divine. And this necessitates that the divine cannot be a body. The third: Indeed, since His essence Most High is equal to the essences of other spatial beings, and it is established [صح?] for other spatial beings that they are sometimes moving and sometimes at rest, it would be necessary that His essence also be thus. And on this assumption, it follows that His essence Most High is susceptible to motion and rest. And everything that is thus, it is necessary to say that it is temporally originated (muhdath).
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents three independent proofs (wujuh) that God's essence cannot be similar to the essences of bodies. The first proof invokes the kalam principle of al-mutamathilayn (two similar things): if two things are similar in essence, they must share all necessary properties (lawazim). If God's essence were like a body's, then either God's eternity would entail the eternity of bodies (impossible, as bodies are contingent), or bodies' temporality would entail God's temporality (also impossible, as God is eternal). Thus similarity is impossible. The second proof applies the same principle to attributes: if God were like bodies, He could be devoid of knowledge, power, and life just as bodies are; but God's attributes are essential, not contingent, so His possession of them would require an external cause, making Him dependent—contradicting divinity. The third proof argues from motion and rest: bodies are subject to change, so if God were like a body, He would be subject to motion and rest, implying temporality (muhdath). All three proofs aim to establish divine transcendence (tanzih) over bodily attributes.

Key Terms
جائزات
إله
موجد
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that contingency of attributes does not imply need for a separate creator.
Correction: Razi shows that if attributes are contingent, they require an external cause (mujid) and specifier (mukhasis), leading to dependence.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, if God were similar to bodies, what would His attributes of life, knowledge, and power become?
A) Necessary.
B) Impossible.
C) Contingent.
D) Eternal.

Key Takeaway
The second proof demonstrates that God's essential attributes cannot be contingent, so He cannot share the nature of bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes were not necessary but contingent, then your worship would be directed to a being dependent on another. How does realizing that God is the only necessary being (wajib al-wujud) transform your certainty in your acts of worship? Will you let this truth anchor you when doubts arise about who you are praying to?
?
Razi's proof shows that a body cannot be the divine because it would need a specifier. Consider your own assumptions: have you ever entertained a mental image of God as having form? Confess it now and repent, asking Allah to purify your tawheed.
?
If you truly believe God is not a body, then your love, fear, and hope must be directed to an unseen necessary being. What in your life still treats God as if He were limited or tangible? What step will you take today to elevate your tawheed beyond imagination?

Proof 3: From Motion and Rest
Razi presents the third proof from motion and rest as properties of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that God cannot be like bodies, because bodies are subject to motion and rest, which would entail temporality in God.

Core Idea
If God were like bodies, He would be changeable, but change implies being created.

The third proof argues from the observable property of bodies: they alternate between motion and rest. If God were similar to bodies in essence, He too would be subject to such changes. But anything subject to change is temporally originated (muhdath), contradicting God's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the brevity of this proof.
The proof is short but powerful.
2
Identify the reasoning from similarity to motion/rest
If God is like bodies, He must share their property of being sometimes moving, sometimes at rest.
3
Follow the implication to temporality
Being susceptible to motion and rest means being subject to change, which is a sign of temporal origination (muhdath).
Anything changeable is not eternal.
4
Conclude the impossibility
Since God is eternal and not originated, the premise of similarity must be false.
This reinforces divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن تكون ذات الله تعالى مساوية لذوات الأجسام في تمام الماهية لوجوه : الأول : إن من حكم المتماثلين : الاستواء في جميع اللوازم . فيلزم من قدم ذات الله تعالى، قدم سائر الأجسام، أو من حدوث سائر الأجسام، حدوث ذات الله تعالى . وذلك محال . الثاني : إن المثلين يجب استواؤهما في جميع اللوازم . وكما صح على سائر الأجسام خلوها عن صفة العلم والقدرة والحياة ، وجب أن يصح على ذاته الخلو عن هذه الصفات، وحينئذ يكون اتصاف ذاته بحياته وعلمه وقدرته ، من الجائزات . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع كون تلك الذات موصوفاً بالحياة والعلم والقدرة ، إلا بإيجاد موجد ، وتخصيص مخصص. وذلك يقتضى احتياجه إلى الإله . وحينئذ كل ما كان جسماً كان محتاجاً إلى الإله . وهذا يقتضى أن الإله يمتنع أن يكون جسماً . الثالث : إنه لما كان ذاته تعالى مساوية لذوات سائر المتحيزات ، ع في سائر المتحيزات كونها متحركة تارة وساكنة أخرى ، وجب أن تكون ذاته أيضاً كذلك . وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم أن تكون ذاته تعالى قابلة للحركة والسكون . وكل ما كان كذلك ، وجب القول بكونه محدثاً
And we only said: it is impossible that the essence of God Most High be equal to the essences of bodies in complete quiddity, for several reasons: The first: Indeed, from the rule of the two similar things (al-mutamathilayn) is equality in all necessary properties (lawazim). So it would follow from the eternity of the essence of God Most High the eternity of all other bodies, or from the temporality of all other bodies the temporality of the essence of God Most High. And that is impossible. The second: Indeed, the two similar things must be equal in all necessary properties. And just as it is established for all other bodies that they are devoid of the attributes of knowledge, power, and life, it would be necessary that His essence be validly devoid of these attributes. Then the description of His essence with His life, knowledge, and power would be among contingent (ja'izat) matters. And if that is the case, it is impossible for that essence to be described with life, knowledge, and power except by the bringing into existence of an existing thing (mujid) and the specification of a specifier (mukhasis). That necessitates its need for the divine (ilah). Then everything that is a body is in need of the divine. And this necessitates that the divine cannot be a body. The third: Indeed, since His essence Most High is equal to the essences of other spatial beings, and it is established [صح?] for other spatial beings that they are sometimes moving and sometimes at rest, it would be necessary that His essence also be thus. And on this assumption, it follows that His essence Most High is susceptible to motion and rest. And everything that is thus, it is necessary to say that it is temporally originated (muhdath).
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents three independent proofs (wujuh) that God's essence cannot be similar to the essences of bodies. The first proof invokes the kalam principle of al-mutamathilayn (two similar things): if two things are similar in essence, they must share all necessary properties (lawazim). If God's essence were like a body's, then either God's eternity would entail the eternity of bodies (impossible, as bodies are contingent), or bodies' temporality would entail God's temporality (also impossible, as God is eternal). Thus similarity is impossible. The second proof applies the same principle to attributes: if God were like bodies, He could be devoid of knowledge, power, and life just as bodies are; but God's attributes are essential, not contingent, so His possession of them would require an external cause, making Him dependent—contradicting divinity. The third proof argues from motion and rest: bodies are subject to change, so if God were like a body, He would be subject to motion and rest, implying temporality (muhdath). All three proofs aim to establish divine transcendence (tanzih) over bodily attributes.

Key Terms
متحيزات
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, if God were like bodies, what would be necessary to say about Him?
A) He is eternal.
B) He is temporally originated.
C) He is all-powerful.
D) He is unique.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that similarity to bodies would require God to be changeable and thus created, which is impossible.

Proof of Equal Essence of Extended Things
Imam Razi proves the equality of all extended things in complete quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that all extended things are equal in complete quiddity (tamam al-mahiyya) and why this is a decisive premise for establishing divine transcendence.

Core Idea
All extended things share the same complete essence, not just an attribute.

This lesson presents a concise but powerful logical proof from Imam Razi. He argues that extension (al-mutahayyiziyya) is not a mere attribute of things but their very essence. Therefore, anything that shares extension shares its essence completely. This proof is a cornerstone for establishing that God cannot be extended, because if He were, He would share essence with other extended things, which is impossible. The passage is short but dense, so we will unpack it step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style; every phrase carries weight.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify and understand the three key terms: متحيز (extended thing), متحيزية (extension or extendedness), and تمام الماهية (complete quiddity or essence).
These terms are central to the proof.
3
Follow the logical flow
The proof has a conditional premise: if extension is not an attribute but the essence itself, then equality in extension implies equality in complete essence. Then the conclusion: all extended things are equal in complete quiddity.
The proof is decisive (برهان قاطع) for establishing this premise.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this proof matters for divine transcendence: if God were extended, He would share essence with other extended things, which contradicts His uniqueness. This proof blocks that possibility.
This is a key step in Razi's larger argument for God's transcendence above being extended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذي يمتنع حصوله في الجهة . وإذا لم تكن المتحيزية صفة للشيء ، كانت نفس الذات . وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون الأشياء المتساوية في المتحيزية ، متساوية في (تمام) (۳) الذات . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن المتحيرات يجب أن تكون كلها متساوية فى تمام الماهية ، وهذا برهان قاطع في تقرير هذه المقدمة
That which is impossible to occur in direction. And if extension (al-mutahayyiziyya) is not an attribute of a thing, then it is the essence itself. And then it follows that things equal in extension are equal in (complete) essence. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that extended things (al-mutahayyizat) must all be equal in complete quiddity (tamam al-mahiyya), and this is a decisive proof in establishing this premise.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a proof. It begins from a premise about extension (al-mutahayyiziyya). If extension is not an attribute of a thing but rather the thing's very essence, then anything that shares extension shares the essence. Therefore all extended things are equal in their complete quiddity (tamam al-mahiyya). This is a decisive proof for establishing this premise, which will be used later to show that God cannot be extended.

Key Terms

تمام الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof conflates equality in extension with equality in essence as if they were automatically the same.
Correction: The proof does not conflate; it explicitly argues that extension is not an attribute but the essence itself. Therefore, equality in extension necessarily entails equality in essence.

Key Takeaway
All extended things are equal in their complete quiddity because extension is not an attribute but the essence itself.

First Reason: Equality in Concomitants Leads to Absurdity
Imam Razi gives the first of four reasons why God cannot equal bodies in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational argument for why God cannot be similar to bodies in essence.

Core Idea
If God were like bodies, they would be eternal or He would be temporal — both impossible.

Imam Razi begins his case for divine transcendence with a powerful logical argument. He shows that assuming any similarity between God and created bodies leads to an unavoidable contradiction. Let's see how this simple yet profound reasoning works.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key concept: mutamathilayn
Two things that are similar in essence are called mutamathilayn. According to rational theologians, they must share all their necessary attributes (lawaazim).
This principle is crucial for the argument.
2
Follow the conditional chain
If God's essence were like bodies, then they would be equal in all concomitant properties. Since God is eternal and bodies are temporal, this forces a choice.
3
Identify the two impossible outcomes
Either bodies become eternal or God becomes temporal. Both are impossible, so the original assumption of similarity must be false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Appreciate the logical force
The argument does not require actual similarity; it shows that if similarity were assumed, absurdity follows. Thus, God is absolutely unique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن من حكم المتماثلين : الاستواء في جميع اللوازم . فيلزم من قدم ذات الله تعالى، قدم سائر الأجسام، أو من حدوث سائر الأجسام، حدوث ذات الله تعالى . وذلك محال
The first: Among the ruling of two similar things (al-mutamathilayn) is equality in all concomitants (al-lawaazim). So it follows from the eternity of the essence of Allah, the eternity of all bodies, or from the temporality of all bodies, the temporality of the essence of Allah. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is the first of four reasons. It argues that if God's essence were equal to the essences of bodies (i.e., God were extended), then they would be similar (mutamathilayn). Similar things must be equal in all their necessary concomitants (lawaazim). A concomitant of God is eternity, and of bodies is temporality. Therefore, either bodies would be eternal (like God) or God would be temporal (like bodies). Both are impossible; thus God cannot be similar to bodies in essence.

Key Terms
متماثلان
لازم (ج لوازم)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument assumes God is actually like bodies and then finds a contradiction.
Correction: Actually, the argument is a reductio: it assumes similarity for the sake of argument and shows that impossible consequences follow, thereby proving similarity cannot hold.

Checkpoint
According to the first reason, what would follow from God's eternity if He were like bodies?
A) Bodies would be temporal.
B) Bodies would be eternal.
C) God would be temporal.
D) Either bodies would be eternal or God would be temporal.

Key Takeaway
The first reason shows that divine similarity to bodies leads to an absurdity, proving God is transcendent and unique in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even unconsciously, imagined Allah to be like a human king—far away, limited, or needing intermediaries? After reading this passage, what specific thought pattern will you actively correct?
?
When you make dua, do you truly feel that Allah is immediately present and hearing you, or do you think of Him as distant? How does the argument that He is not like bodies strengthen your certainty in His closeness?
?
If you catch yourself forming a mental image of Allah, what concrete step will you take to reject that image, knowing that any similarity to creation is impossible? Will you say 'Subhanallah' and renew your tawhid?

Third Reason: Motion and Rest Imply Temporality
Imam Razi argues that if God were extended, He would be subject to motion and rest, hence temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's being subject to motion and rest would imply temporality, and how this refutes the view that God is a body.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would be temporal—which is impossible.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents a third argument against divine corporeality. He builds on a foundational principle in kalam: any entity capable of motion and rest is necessarily temporal. Since God is eternal, He cannot be subject to such accidents, and therefore He cannot be a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The Arabic contains a possible OCR stray character 'ع'—ignore it for meaning.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break the argument into its components: equality premise, observation of bodies, implication for God, application of the temporality rule, and final conclusion.
3
Reflect on the key principle
Think about why 'capable of motion and rest' entails temporality in kalam. Recall that bodies are temporal because they are never free from accidents like motion/rest, and whatever changes is temporal.
This principle is established in the proofs for the temporality of bodies.
4
Consider the conclusion
Since God cannot be temporal, the assumption that He is a body leads to a contradiction. Therefore, He is not a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنه لما كان ذاته تعالى مساوية لذوات سائر المتحيزات ، ع في سائر المتحيزات كونها متحركة تارة وساكنة أخرى ، وجب أن تكون ذاته أيضاً كذلك . وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم أن تكون ذاته تعالى قابلة للحركة والسكون . وكل ما كان كذلك ، وجب القول بكونه محدثاً ما ثبت في تقرير هذه الدلائل في مسألة حدوث الأجسام . ولما كان محدثاً وحدوثه محال - فكونه جسمها : محال
The third: Since His essence is equal to the essences of all extended things, and in all extended things it occurs that they are sometimes moving and sometimes at rest, it must be that His essence is also like that. And on this assumption it follows that His essence is capable of motion and rest. And everything that is such, it is necessary to say that it is temporal, as established in the exposition of these proofs in the question of the temporality of bodies. And since it is temporal and its temporality is impossible, then its being a body is impossible.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is the third reason. It argues that if God's essence were equal to the essences of extended things (bodies), then because bodies are subject to motion and rest, God would also be subject to motion and rest. Anything subject to motion and rest is temporal (hadith), as established in proofs of the temporality of bodies. But God is eternal, so temporality is impossible. Therefore, God cannot be a body. This reason uses a familiar kalam argument that bodies are temporal due to accidents of motion and rest.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that God actually moves and rests.
Correction: The argument only shows that if God were a body, He would be capable of motion and rest; it does not assert actual occurrence. Capability itself is enough to imply temporality.

Checkpoint

C) It is necessary.
D) It is possible.

Key Takeaway
Anything capable of motion and rest is temporal; since God cannot be temporal, He cannot be a body.

Fourth Reason: Composition Would Require a Composer
Imam Razi argues that a bodily God would be composed of parts requiring a composer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument from Asas al-Taqdis that refutes the notion of God being a body by showing it leads to composition and dependence on an external composer.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would need an external composer — impossible.

Imam Razi continues his arguments for divine transcendence by considering the implications of assuming God has a body. In this passage, he demonstrates that a bodily God would be composed of parts, and those parts would require an arranger, contradicting God's self-sufficiency. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's logical flow come through.
2
Identify the conditional assumption
Notice the 'if...then' structure: if God were a body, then He would be composed of parts.
3
Trace the implications
The parts would be identical to each other and similar to parts of other bodies, so composition and separation would be possible, requiring a composer.
This step links bodily composition to dependence.
4
Conclude the impossibility
The need for a composer contradicts God's nature as Creator of all things, so the initial assumption is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنه لو كان جسما لكان مؤتلف الأجزاء . وتلك الأجزاء تكون متماثلة بأعيانها ، وهى أيضا مماثلة الأجزاء سائر الأجسام . وعلى هذا التقدير ، كما صح الاجتماع والافتراق على سائر الاجسام ، وجب أن يصح على تلك الأجزاء. وعلى هذا التقدير لابد له من مركب ومؤلف . .وذلك على إله العالم محال
The fourth: If He were a body, He would be composed of parts. And those parts would be identical in themselves, and they are also similar to the parts of all other bodies. And on this assumption, just as composition and separation are valid for all bodies, it must be valid for those parts. And on this assumption, He must have a composer and arranger. And that is impossible with respect to the God of the world.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is the fourth reason. It argues that if God were a body, He would be composed of parts. These parts would be similar to each other and similar to parts of other bodies. Bodies can be composed and separated, so the parts of God could also be composed and separated. That would mean God requires an external composer (murakkib) and arranger (mu'allif) to bring His parts together. This is impossible for God, as He is the creator of all things. Therefore, God cannot be a body.

Key Terms
جسم
جزء
مؤتلف
مركب
مؤلف

A) He would have no parts.
B) He would need a composer.
C) He would be eternal.
D) He would be simple.

Key Takeaway
God cannot be a body because that would entail composition and dependence on a composer, which is impossible for the Creator.

Second Proof: Extension Implies Finitude and Contingency
Imam Razi enumerates a three-premise syllogism against divine extension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second kalam proof for God's incorporeality, which demonstrates that extension leads to finitude, possibility, and temporality, and therefore cannot apply to Allah.

Core Idea
An extended being is finite, possible, and created — God is none of these.

This passage presents a second rigorous proof against attributing extension or corporeality to Allah. Imam Razi structures his argument as a syllogism with three premises, each supported by rational reasoning. By tracing the logical chain from extension to finitude to possibility to creation, you will see why kalam theologians affirm tanzih (transcendence) as a rational necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the overall argument
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing on details. Then read the translation to get the gist of the proof's flow from extension to impossibility.
Notice the syllogistic structure: if A then B, B then C, C then D, so not A.
2
Examine the first premise
Focus on the claim that an extended thing must be finite. Razi argues that magnitude accepts increase and decrease, which is a sign of finitude. Ask: why does capacity for increase imply finitude?
This premise relies on the intuitive idea that anything extended can be conceived as larger or smaller, hence limited.
3
Examine the second premise
Every finite thing is possible (mumkin). Razi states that we can conceive a finite thing as larger or smaller, so its existence is not necessary in itself. Reflect on the connection between conceivability of alternatives and contingency.
This is a key move: finitude indicates that a thing's magnitude is not necessary, so its existence is contingent.
4
Examine the third premise (partial)
Every possible thing is temporal (muhdath). The argument begins but the text cuts off. Still, the overall logic is clear: what is temporal is created and cannot be God. Try to infer the missing reasoning.
Standard kalam holds that a possible thing requires a preponderating factor to bring it into existence, hence it is temporal.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the consequences of extension (finitude, possibility, temporality) are impossible for God, extension itself is impossible. This proves divine transcendence of spatial attributes.
The proof does not deny all attributes, only those incompatible with necessary existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثانى فى بيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون متحيزاً : هو أنه لو كان متحيزاً ، لكان متناهياً . وكل متناه : ممكن . وكل ممكن : محدث . فلو كان متحيزاً لكان محدثاً . وهذا محال ، فذاك محال أما المقدمة الأولى : وهى بيان أنه تعالى لو كان متحيز ، لكان متناهيا : . فالدليل عليه : أن كل مقدار ، فإنه يقبل الزيادة والنقصان . وكل ما كان كذلك ، فهو متناه . وهذا يدل على أن كل متحيز ، فهو متناه . وشرح هذا الدليل قد قررناه في سائر كتبنا وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهى بيان أن كل متناه ، فهو ممكن : فذلك لأن كل ما كان متناهياً ، فإن فرض كونه أزيد قدراً أو أنقص قدراً : أمر ممكن ، والعلم بثبوت هذا الإمكان ضروري . فثبت : أن كل متناه ، فهو في ذاته ممكن . وأما المقدمة الثالثة : وهى بيان أن كل ممكن محدث : فهو أنه لما كان : الزائد والناقص والمساوى متساوون (۷) في الإمكان ، امتنع رجحان
The second proof in explaining that it is impossible for Him to be extended: He says: If He were extended, He would be finite. And every finite thing is possible. And every possible thing is temporal. So if He were extended, He would be temporal. And this is impossible, so that is impossible. As for the first premise: it is the explanation that if He were extended, He would be finite. The proof of that: every magnitude accepts increase and decrease. And everything that is such is finite. This indicates that every extended thing is finite. And we have established the explanation of this proof in our other books. As for the second premise: it is the explanation that every finite thing is possible. That is because every finite thing, its being conceived as greater or smaller in extent is a possible matter, and knowledge of the existence of this possibility is necessary. So it is established that every finite thing is in itself possible. As for the third premise: it is the explanation that every possible thing is temporal. That is because, since the greater, the lesser, and the equal are equal in possibility, it is impossible for precedence ('رجحان' cut off)
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is the second proof, structured as a syllogism with three premises. The main argument: If God were extended, He would be finite. Every finite thing is possible (mumkin), and every possible thing is temporal (hadith). Therefore, if God were extended, He would be temporal, which is impossible. Hence God cannot be extended. The author then explains each premise. Premise 1: Extended things are finite because magnitude accepts increase and decrease, which is characteristic of finitude. Premise 2: Finite things are possible because we can conceive them as larger or smaller, indicating they are not necessary in themselves. Premise 3: Every possible thing is temporal — the explanation begins but is cut off at the end of the page. The argument is a classic kalam proof for divine incorporeality.

Key Terms
متحيز
متناه
ممكن
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies all attributes of God.
Correction: The proof only denies extension and similar spatial attributes, not all attributes. It uses rational categories to show that extension is incompatible with necessary existence.

Checkpoint
What is the logical consequence of being extended according to the first premise?
A) It is eternal.
B) It is finite.
C) It is necessary.
D) It is immaterial.

Key Takeaway
Extension leads to finitude, possibility, and temporality — all impossible for God, so Allah transcends all spatial and temporal limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were extended, He would be finite. Do you ever unconsciously imagine Allah as being in a particular direction or place? How can you train your heart to worship Him as the One who transcends all space and direction?
?
Every finite thing is possible—it could have been otherwise. Your own body, your lifespan, your abilities—all finite. Does this truth make you feel your utter dependence on the Necessary Being? What would change in your daily life if you truly lived in that awareness?
?
The proof concludes that an extended being would be temporal, brought into existence. Everything you rely on—your health, wealth, relationships—is temporal. Are you clinging to any of these as if they were eternal? How can this proof free you to rely only on the Eternal?

The Fourth Proof: God Cannot Be a Body Due to Composition
Imam Razi presents the fourth argument against God being a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if God were a body, He would be composed of parts and would need a composer, which is impossible for God.

Core Idea
A body requires a composer; God is the Creator, not created.

Imam Razi presents a crisp logical argument against the notion that God could be a body. The argument proceeds step by step, showing that if God were a body, He would have parts, and those parts would be subject to separation and combination, implying that God is a composite requiring a maker. This leads to an impossibility because God is the ultimate Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If He were a body...'
The argument is concise; each phrase builds on the previous.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The argument starts with a hypothetical: 'If He were a body, He would be composed of parts.' Underline this assumption.
This is the starting point of the proof.
3
Trace the consequences
Follow the chain: composition implies parts, parts are identical, parts can separate and combine, so God would need a compositor. Write down each step.
This shows the logical necessity of a composer.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion states that having a composer is impossible for God. Reflect on why this is a contradiction to God's nature as the Necessary Being.
This refutes the idea of God being a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنه لو كان جسما لكان مؤتلف الأجزاء . وتلك الأجزاء تكون متماثلة بأعيانها ، وهى أيضا مماثلة الأجزاء سائر الأجسام . وعلى هذا التقدير ، كما صح الاجتماع والافتراق على سائر الاجسام ، وجب أن يصح على تلك الأجزاء. وعلى هذا التقدير لابد له من مركب ومؤلف . .وذلك على إله العالم محال
The fourth: If He were a body, He would be composed of parts. And those parts would be identical in their essences, and they would also be identical to the parts of other bodies. On this assumption, just as separation and combination are valid for other bodies, they must be valid for those parts. On this assumption, He would necessarily have a compositor and a composer. And that is impossible for the God of the world.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against attributing a body to God. The argument is that a body is composed of parts, and those parts would be similar to each other and to parts of other bodies. Then, as other bodies can undergo separation and combination (i.e., their parts can be taken apart or put together), the same would apply to God's parts. This would imply that God is composed and has a composer, which is absurd because God is the Creator, not created. So the conclusion is that God cannot be a body.

Key Terms
جسم
مؤتلف الأجزاء
اجتماع
افتراق
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God were a body, He could be a simple body without parts, or that being a body does not imply composition.
Correction: The argument shows that any body, by definition, is composed of parts (مؤتلف الأجزاء). Moreover, the parts of a body are inherently capable of separation and combination, which would make God dependent on a composer.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth argument conclude about God being a body?
A) It is possible because bodies can be simple.
B) It is impossible because a body requires a compositor.
C) It is necessary for God's existence.
D) It is unknown.

Key Takeaway
God cannot be a body because any body is composed of parts and requires a composer, which contradicts God's status as the uncreated Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that everything composed needs a composer. You are composed of body and soul, atoms and molecules. How does this truth press you to acknowledge your utter dependence on God for every moment of your existence? Will you consciously affirm today, 'I am totally needful of You, O Creator'?
?
This proof refutes the idea that God could be a body, yet we often treat worldly things as ultimate. Is there anything in your life—money, status, relationships—that you treat as if it were self-sufficient, like a 'god'? How will you break that attachment today in light of the fact that only the Uncomposed Creator is worthy of ultimate reliance?

The Second Proof: God Cannot Be an Occupant of Space
Imam Razi proves that God cannot be an entity in space through a three-premise syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's syllogistic proof that God cannot be an occupant of space because it would lead to finitude, possibility, and origination.

Core Idea
Occupying space would make God finite, possible, and hence originated — impossible for the Eternal.

In this lesson, we examine Razi's second proof for divine transcendence. He constructs a rigorous syllogism that connects being an occupant of space to being originated, which cannot apply to God. The proof relies on three carefully justified premises. Let us walk through each step and see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and the translation once to get the overall flow of the argument. Notice the syllogistic structure.
The proof is presented as a conditional chain.
2
Understand the three premises
Identify the three premises: (1) if an occupant of space, then finite; (2) everything finite is possible; (3) everything possible is originated. The passage elaborates each.
The third premise is the most elaborated.
3
Grasp the reasoning for premise 3
Focus on the argument for premise 3: among possibilities, a preponderator is needed to prefer one magnitude over another. The need cannot be at the time of persistence, so it must be at origination or before, hence every possible is originated.
This is the key step for understanding causality in kalam.
4
Trace the conclusion
Combine the premises: God cannot be originated, so the chain leads back to the impossibility of God being an occupant of space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثانى فى بيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون متحيزاً : هو أنه لو كان متحيزاً ، لكان متناهياً . وكل متناه : ممكن . وكل ممكن : محدث . فلو كان متحيزاً لكان محدثاً . وهذا محال ، فذاك محال . أما المقدمة الأولى : وهى بيان أنه تعالى لو كان متحيز ، لكان متناهيا . فالدليل عليه : أن كل مقدار ، فإنه يقبل الزيادة والنقصان . وكل ما كان كذلك ، فهو متناه . وهذا يدل على أن كل متحيز ، فهو متناه . وشرح هذا الدليل قد قررناه في سائر كتبنا . وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهى بيان أن كل متناه ، فهو ممكن : فذلك لأن كل ما كان متناهياً ، فإن فرض كونه أزيد قدراً أو أنقص قدراً : أمر ممكن ، والعلم بثبوت هذا الإمكان ضروري . فثبت : أن كل متناه ، فهو في ذاته ممكن . وأما المقدمة الثالثة : وهى بيان أن كل ممكن محدث : فهو أنه لما كان : الزائد والناقص والمساوى متساوون في الإمكان ، امتنع رجحان بعضها على بعض ، إلا لمرجح . والافتقار إلى المرجح . إما أن يكون حال وجوده ، أو حال عدمه. فإن كان حال وجوده فإنه يكون إما حال بقائه ، أو حال حدوثه ، ويمتنع أن يفتقر إلى المؤثر حال بقائه . لأن المؤثر ، تأثيره : فى التكوين والتأثير . فلو افتقر حال بقائه إلى المؤثر ، لزم تكوين الكائن ، وتحصيل الحاصل ، وذلك محال . فلم يبق إلا أن يحصل الافتقار . إما حال حدوثه أو حال عدمه. وعلى التقديرين فإنه يلزم أن يكون كل ممكن محدثاً ، فثبت : أن كل جسم متناه ، وكل ممكن محدث، والإله يمتنع أن يكون محدثاً. وبالله التوفيق
The second proof in explaining that it is impossible for Him to be an occupant of space: If He were an occupant of space, He would be finite. Everything finite is possible. Everything possible is originated. So if He were an occupant of space, He would be originated. This is impossible, so that is impossible. As for the first premise: that if He, exalted, were an occupant of space, He would be finite. The evidence for it: every quantity accepts increase and decrease, and whatever is like that is finite. This indicates that every occupant of space is finite. We have elaborated this proof in our other books. As for the second premise: that everything finite is possible: that is because everything finite, supposing it to be greater or lesser in quantity is possible, and knowledge of the existence of this possibility is necessary. So it is established that everything finite is possible in itself. As for the third premise: that everything possible is originated: it is that since the greater, the lesser, and the equal are equal in possibility, it is impossible for one of them to be preferred over another except by a preponderator. The need for a preponderator is either at the time of its existence or at the time of its non-existence. If it is at the time of its existence, then it is either at the time of its persistence or at the time of its occurrence. It is impossible for it to need the effecter at the time of its persistence, because the effecter's effect is in bringing into being and affecting. So if it needed the effecter at the time of its persistence, that would necessitate bringing into being what already is and making what already exists, and that is impossible. So there remains only that the need is either at the time of its occurrence or at the time of its non-existence. In either case, it follows that every possible is originated. So it is established that every body is finite, and every possible is originated, and the God it is impossible that He be originated. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that God cannot be an occupant of space (mutahayyiz). The proof is a syllogism with three premises: (1) If God were an occupant of space, He would be finite. (2) Everything finite is possible (because one can conceive it being larger or smaller). (3) Everything possible is originated. The conclusion is that God would be originated, which is impossible. The bulk of the passage elaborates the third premise: among possible magnitudes (greater, lesser, equal), a preponderator (murajjih) is needed to prefer one over another. This need cannot be at the time of persistence (since that would require effecting an already existing thing, which is absurd), so it must be at the time of origination or before existence. Thus every possible requires an originator and is originated. Therefore, God cannot be an occupant of space, as He would then be originated.

Key Terms
متحيز
متناه
ممكن
محدث
مرجح
تكوين
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that possibility does not necessitate origination; a possible thing could be eternal.
Correction: Razi argues that possible things require a preponderator at the time of origination; without it, they would not come into existence. Therefore every possible must be originated.

Checkpoint
What is the third premise in Razi's second proof?
A) Every occupant of space is finite.
B) Everything finite is possible.
C) Everything possible is originated.
D) God cannot be an occupant of space.

Key Takeaway
The second proof demonstrates that occupying space would entail origination, which is incompatible with the divine nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God cannot be contained in any space, what does that mean for how you imagine Him when you pray? Do you subconsciously picture Him in a certain direction or place? How can you train yourself to worship a God beyond all spatial limitations?
?
The proof shows that everything finite depends on a preponderator for its existence. Where in your life do you still act as if you are the ultimate decider, forgetting that your very being and every choice depend on God's constant origination?
?
Razi argues that if God were in space, He would be originated. Do you ever reduce God to a location—like a mosque, a holy book, or a moment of spiritual feeling—thereby limiting His transcendence? What would change if you truly believed He is not confined to any place?

God Cannot Be an Occupant of Space: The Third Proof (Part 1)
Imam Razi presents the first part of the third proof against God being an occupant of space.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first part of Imam Razi's third proof that God cannot be an occupant of space, specifically the argument that it would force a specification leading to composition.

Core Idea
Spatial existence for God forces a specification that implies composition.

Imam Razi continues his critique of corporealism with a third proof. He argues that if God were an occupant of space (mutahayyiz), He would necessarily require a specification (takhsis) to distinguish Him from other spatial beings. This leads to a dilemma: either the distinction is real (making spatial being a genus) or not (making it a species). The first option is rejected because it implies composition. We will follow the argument as presented in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to familiarize yourself with the flow of the argument.
The passage is the opening of the third proof; it is incomplete but self-contained for our purposes.
2
Understand the implication (mulazama)
Notice that Razi says if God were an occupant of space, He would be equal to others in the concept of spatial being but different in essence. This sets up the need for specification.
Equality in concept does not mean equality in reality; distinction is required.
3
Examine the two alternatives after specification
After specification, either the distinction is real (haqiqi) or not. The first makes 'spatial being' a genus with species; the second makes it a species with individuals.
In each case, the Necessary Existent becomes one of the subordinate categories.
4
See why the first alternative is rejected
Razi rejects the first alternative because it would make God's essence composite. He does not yet reject the second; that will come later.
Composition is impossible for God, so the first alternative is false.
5
Note the incompleteness
The passage ends before the second alternative is refuted and the conclusion drawn. This is a common technique; the full proof would continue.
Keep in mind that the argument is not yet finished.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثالث : لو كان إله العالم متحيزاً ، لكان محتاجاً إلى الخير . وهذا محال ، فكونه متحيزاً ، محال . بيان الملازمة : إنه لو كان متحيزاً، لكان مساوياً لغيره فى المتحيزات ، في مفهوم كونه متحيزاً ، ولكان مخالفاً لها في عينه و تشخصه . ثم نقول : إن بعد حصول الامتياز بالتعين : إما أن يحصل الامتياز فى الحقيقة - وعلى هذا التقدير ، يكون المتحيز جنساً ، تحته أنواع . أحدها : واجب الوجود - وإما أن لا يحصل الامتياز في الحقيقة - وعلى هذا التقدير ، يكون المتحيز نوعاً ، تحته أشخاص . أحدهما : واجب الوجود - فنقول : الأول باطل . لأن على هذا التقدير تكون ذاته مركبة
The third proof: If the God of the world were an occupant of space, He would be in need of specification. This is impossible, so His being an occupant of space is impossible. Explanation of the implication: If He were an occupant of space, He would be equal to others among occupants of space in the concept of being an occupant of space, and He would be different from them in His essence and individuality. Then we say: After the distinction by specification occurs, either the distinction occurs in reality – on this assumption, the occupant of space is a genus under which are species, one of which is the Necessary Existent – or the distinction does not occur in reality – on this assumption, the occupant of space is a species under which are individuals, one of which is the Necessary Existent. We say: The first is false, because on this assumption His essence would be composite.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage begins the third proof that God cannot be an occupant of space (mutahayyiz). The argument is that if God were an occupant of space, He would share the generic concept of 'spatial being' with others, but He would be distinct in His essence (ayn) and individuality (tashakhkhus). This distinction (imtiyaz) forces a choice: either the distinction is real (haqiqi), making 'spatial being' a genus with species, one of which is the Necessary Existent; or the distinction is not real, making 'spatial being' a species with individuals, one of which is the Necessary Existent. The author declares the first alternative false because it would make God's essence composite (murakkaba). The proof is incomplete in the provided text; the refutation of the second alternative and the conclusion are missing.

Key Terms
متحيز
محتاج إلى التخصيص
تمييز
جنس
نوع
مركبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God were spatial, He would be just like any other object without needing any distinction.
Correction: Razi argues that even if God were spatial, He would still be distinct from other spatial beings in His essence, which forces a specification that leads to composition.

Checkpoint
What does the third proof claim about an occupant-of-space God?
A) He would need no specification.
B) He would be a genus or species, leading to composition.
C) He would be identical to all others.
D) He would be simple.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that if God were an occupant of space, His necessary specification would lead to composition, which is impossible for God.

Proof that Every Body is Created and God is Not a Body
Imam Razi proves that every body is created through the need for a determinant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's logical argument that every body is originated and therefore God cannot be a body.

Core Idea
A body's need for a determinant at its origination proves it is created, so God is not a body.

Imam Razi presents a concise logical proof that establishes the contingency of all bodies. By analyzing the need for a determinant, he shows that bodies are originated and thus cannot be attributed to God, who is necessary. This passage is fundamental to understanding the Islamic doctrine of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get a sense of the argument. Note the key terms like مرجح (determinant) and محدث (originated).
The argument is dense but logical; focus on the flow.
2
Identify the core disjunction
Imam Razi splits the need for a determinant into two possibilities: at existence or non-existence. Then further splits existence into continuance or origination.
This disjunction is the backbone of the proof.
3
Understand the impossibility during continuance
Why can't a body need a determinant while it already exists? Because that would mean 'obtaining the already obtained' which is logically absurd.
This is a key move; reflect on why it's impossible.
4
Trace the conclusion
The only remaining possibilities are need at origination or before existence, both implying the body is originated. Hence every body is created, and God, being necessary, cannot be a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعضهم على بعض إلا لمرجح. والافتقار إلى المرجح إما أن يكون حال وجوده أو حال عدمه. فإن كان حال وجوده فإنه يكون إما حال بقائه أو حال حدوثه، ويمتنع أن يفتقر إلى المؤثر حال بقائه. لأن المؤثر تأثيره في التكوين والتأثير. فلو افتقر حال بقائه إلى المؤثر لزم تكوين الكائن وتحصيل الحاصل وذلك محال. فلم يبق إلا أن يحصل الافتقار إما حال حدوثه أو حال عدمه. وعلى التقديرين فإنه يلزم أن يكون كل ممكن محدثاً. فثبت: أن كل جسم متناه وكل ممكن محدث. فثبت: أن كل جسم محدث. والإله يمتنع أن يكون محدثاً. وبالله التوفيق.
Some of them over others except by a determinant. And the need for the determinant is either at the time of its existence or at the time of its non-existence. If it is at the time of its existence, then it is either at the time of its continuance or at the time of its origination, and it is impossible for it to need the effecter at the time of its continuance, because the effect of the effecter is in formation and effecting. So if it required the effecter at the time of its continuance, that would necessitate the formation of the already formed and the obtaining of the already obtained, and that is impossible. So nothing remains except that the need occurs either at the time of its origination or at the time of its non-existence. In both cases, it follows that every possible being is originated. So it is established that every finite body and every possible being is originated. So it is established that every body is originated. And it is impossible for God to be originated. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof that every body is contingent and therefore created, so God cannot be a body. The argument proceeds from the premise that a body's existence depends on a determinant (مرجح) that gives it existence over non-existence. The need for this determinant cannot be at the time of the body's continued existence, because that would be trying to create what already exists (تحصيل الحاصل). Therefore, the need must be either at the time of its origination (حدوث) or before it exists (عدم). In either case, the body is dependent on an external cause for its coming into being, which means it is originated (محدث). The conclusion is that every body is originated, and since God is the necessary existent, He cannot be originated. Hence, God is not a body.

Key Terms
مرجح (determinant)
محدث (originated/created)
جسم (body)
ممكن (possible/contingent)
تكوين (formation)
تحصيل الحاصل (obtaining the already obtained)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that bodies are created without proof.
Correction: The argument does not assume creation; it proves it by showing that bodies require a determinant at origination, which logically entails that they are originated.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's proof, why cannot the need for a determinant be at the time of the body's continued existence?
A) Because the determinant would be unnecessary
B) Because it would require creating what already exists (تحصيل الحاصل)
C) Because the body is eternal
D) Because the determinant is the body itself

Key Takeaway
Every body depends on a cause for its existence and is therefore originated, so God, as the necessary being, cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument proves that your own body is dependent on a cause for its existence. Do you live with the constant awareness that your existence is sustained by God every moment, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? How would your day change if you truly internalized that at every instant you are contingent?
?
If needing a determinant during continuance is absurd (because God does not 'recreate' you each moment), then why do you sometimes behave as if God's care is not continuous? What would it mean to trust that His act of creation is complete and yet His sustenance is ongoing?
?
God cannot be a body because He is the originator of all bodies. Yet, how often do you form mental images of God that reduce Him to something physical or limited? What specific steps can you take to purify your conception of God from any trace of anthropomorphism?
