Third Proof: God Cannot Be a Body
Razi proves God cannot be a body via a reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi argues that if God were a body, He would be dependent on another, which is impossible for the Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
A body must be differentiated by something external, making it dependent.

Imam Razi presents a sophisticated proof that God cannot be a body. He argues that any body requires a differentiator, either at the level of genus or species, leading to dependence on an external cause. Since God is the Necessary Existent, He cannot be dependent. Let's follow Razi's logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a claim, an explanation of the entailment, and a dismantling of two possibilities.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming God is a body leads to a contradiction.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Razi considers two ways God could be a body: either as a species under the genus 'body', or as an individual under the species 'body'. Identify these two branches in the text.
These correspond to the two 'إما' clauses.
3
Understand the first refutation
The first possibility (genus-species) would make God's essence composite (genus + differentia). Composites depend on their parts, so God would depend on another. See why this is impossible.
4
Understand the second refutation
The second possibility (species-individual) would make God's individuation external to His essence. Individuation requires a cause, and that cause would be other than God, making Him dependent.
If individuation were from the essence itself, all members would be identical, but there are multiple bodies.
5
Connect to the conclusion
Both possibilities lead to dependence, which contradicts God being necessary by Himself. Therefore, God cannot be a body.
The conclusion is that the assumption is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثالث: لو كان إله العالم متحيزاً لكان محتاجاً إلى الغير. وهذا محال، فكونه متحيزاً محال. بيان الملازمة: إنه لو كان متحيزاً لكان مساوياً لغيره في المتحيزات في مفهوم كونه متحيزاً، ولكان مخالفاً لها في ماهيته وتشخصه. ثم نقول: إن بعد حصول الامتياز بالتعين: إما أن يحصل الامتياز في الحقيقة – وعلى هذا التقدير يكون المتحيز جنساً تحته أنواع أحدها واجب الوجود – وإما أن لا يحصل الامتياز في الحقيقة – وعلى هذا التقدير يكون المتحيز نوعاً تحته أشخاص أحدها واجب الوجود. فنقول: الأول باطل، لأن على هذا التقدير تكون ذاته مركبة من الجنس والفصل. وكل مركب فهو مفتقر إلى جزئه وجزؤه غيره. وكل مركب فهو مفتقر إلى غيره. فلو كان واجب الوجود متحيزاً لكان مفتقراً إلى غيره. والثاني أيضاً باطل، لأن على هذا التقدير يكون تعينه زائداً على ماهيته النوعية، وذلك التعين لا بد له من مقتضٍ، وليس تلك الماهية، وإلا لكان نوعه منحصراً في شخصه، وقد فرضنا أنه ليس كذلك، فلا بد وأن يكون المقتضى لذلك التعين شيئاً غير تلك الماهية وغير لوازم تلك الماهية، فيكون محتاجاً إلى غيره. فثبت: أنه لو كان متحيزاً لكان محتاجاً إلى غيره. وذلك محال، لأنه واجب الوجود لذاته. وواجب الوجود لذاته لا يكون واجب الوجود لغيره. فثبت: أن كونه متحيزاً محال.
The third proof: If the God of the world were a body (mutaḥayyiz), He would be in need of another. And this is impossible, so His being a body is impossible. Explanation of the entailment: If He were a body, He would be equal to other bodies in the concept of being a body, but would differ from them in His essence (māhiyya) and individuality (tashakhkhuṣ). Then we say: After the distinction by individuation (ta‘ayyun) occurs, either the distinction occurs in reality – on this supposition, the body (mutaḥayyiz) would be a genus under which are species, one of which is the Necessary Existent – or the distinction does not occur in reality – on this supposition, the body would be a species under which are individuals, one of which is the Necessary Existent. We say: The first is false, because on this supposition His essence would be composed of genus and differentia. And every composite is dependent on its part, and its part is other than it. And every composite is dependent on another. So if the Necessary Existent were a body, He would be dependent on another. And the second is also false, because on this supposition His individuation would be additional to His specific essence, and that individuation must have a determinant, and that determinant is not that essence, otherwise His species would be confined to His person, and we have assumed it is not so. So the determinant of that individuation must be something other than that essence and other than its necessary concomitants, so He would be in need of another. So it is established that if He were a body, He would be in need of another. And that is impossible, because He is necessary of existence by Himself. And that which is necessary of existence by Himself cannot be necessary of existence by another. So it is established that His being a body is impossible.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God cannot be a body (mutaḥayyiz). Imam Razi begins by stating the claim: if God were a body, He would need another (الغير), which is impossible, so being a body is impossible. He then explains the entailment (بيان الملازمة): as a body, God would share the concept of being a body with other bodies but differ in His essence and individuality. This difference must be accounted for either at the level of reality (i.e., the body is a genus) or not (i.e., it is a species). In the first case, if God is an instance of the genus 'body', then His essence would be composed of genus and differentia, making Him composite and dependent on His parts, which are other than Him. A dependent being cannot be the Necessary Existent. In the second case, if God is one individual under the species 'body', then His individuation (تعين) would be additional to His specific essence. This individuation requires a cause (مقتضى) other than the essence itself, otherwise all members of the species would be identical. Since there are multiple bodies, the cause of God's individuality must be external, making Him dependent on another. Both possibilities lead to dependence, which contradicts God's being necessary by Himself. Thus, God cannot be a body.

Key Terms
متحيز (body/substance)
محتاج إلى الغير (needy of another)
جنس (genus)
فصل (differentia)
ماهية (essence)
تعين (individuation)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that being a body does not necessarily entail dependence, because God could be a body without needing an external cause.
Correction: The proof shows that any body requires differentiation from others. If the differentiation is by essence (genus-species), the essence is composite and dependent. If by individuation, the individuation requires an external cause. Either way, dependence is unavoidable.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what problem arises if God is considered a species under the genus 'body'?
A) His essence would be simple
B) His essence would be composed of genus and differentia, making Him dependent
C) He would be an atom
D) He would be indivisible

Key Takeaway
God cannot be a body because that would require an external cause for His differentiation, contradicting His self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If God does not need any external cause for His existence or attributes, then what does that mean for your own sense of self-sufficiency? Do you ever act as if you are independent of God? How can you cultivate a constant awareness that you are utterly dependent on Him for every breath and every ability?
?
Razi argues that any composition or individuation would make God dependent. Reflect on your own life: which of your attributes or characteristics are you most proud of? Are they truly yours, or are they gifts from God? How does acknowledging that prevent you from falling into a subtle form of self-worship?
?
The proof shows that God cannot be a body because that would necessitate an external cause. But what are the 'bodies' or material things in your life that you have allowed to become necessities for your happiness? Are you living as if something other than God is necessary for you? What needs to change today to realign your dependences solely towards Allah?

First Argument: Every Body Is Divisible and Composite
First way of the fourth proof: from distinct sides to composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi proves that any body (mutaḥayyiz) must be divisible due to having distinct sides, and therefore composite.

Core Idea
A body's distinct sides force it to be divisible and composite.

Imam Razi's fourth proof against God being a body begins with a fundamental observation: anything that occupies space has sides. Once sides exist, division is possible. This leads directly to composition. Let's examine the first argument in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first argument
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Focus on the logical flow from 'every body has distinct sides' to 'therefore it is divisible and composite'.
This argument assumes the denial of the indivisible atom.
2
Identify the key premise
The premise: every body (mutaḥayyiz) must have one side distinct from the other. Why is this necessarily true? Consider spatial extension.
This premise is taken as self-evident for bodies.
3
Trace the inference to divisibility
From distinct sides, Razi infers divisibility (inqisām). Ask yourself: does having sides necessarily mean the body can be divided?
In classical kalām, a body is defined as that which has length, width, and depth.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since it is divisible, it is composite (murakkab). Therefore, if God were a body, He would be composite, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع: لو كان إله العالم متحيزاً لكان مركبا. وهذا محال، فكونه متحيزاً محال. بيان الملازمة من وجهين: أحدهما: وهو على قول من ينكر الجوهر الفرد، إن كل متحيز فلا بد وأن يتميز أحد جانبيه عن الثاني. وكل ما كان كذلك فهو منقسم. فثبت: أن كل متحيز فهو منقسم ومركب. والثاني: إن كل متحيز فإما أن يكون قابلاً للقسمة أو لا يكون. فإن كان قابلاً للقسمة كان مركبا مؤلفاً، وإن كان غير قابل للقسمة فهو الجوهر الفرد وهو في غاية الصغر والحقارة، وليس في العقلاء أحد يقول هذا القول. فثبت: أنه تعالى لو كان متحيزاً لكان منقسماً.
The fourth proof: If the God of the world were a body (mutaḥayyiz), He would be composite (murakkab). And this is impossible, so His being a body is impossible. Explanation of the entailment in two ways: First – according to the view of one who denies the indivisible atom – every body must have one side distinct from the other. And everything that is like that is divisible. So it is established that every body is divisible and composite. Second: Every body is either divisible or not. If it is divisible, it is composite and compounded. And if it is not divisible, then it is the indivisible atom (jawhar fard), which is extremely small and contemptible, and no rational person holds this view. So it is established that if the Exalted were a body, He would be divisible.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God cannot be a body because every body is composite. Imam Razi offers two lines of reasoning (بيان الملازمة من وجهين). The first argument assumes the denial of the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد): a body necessarily has distinct sides, so it is divisible, and every divisible thing is composite. Thus, any body is composite. The second argument is a disjunction: a body is either divisible or not. If divisible, it is composed of parts, hence composite. If not divisible, it would be an indivisible atom (جوهر فرد), which is tiny and contemptible—a description unsuitable for God, and no rational person would attribute that to God. Therefore, God cannot be a body. The conclusion is that God is not divisible or composite.

Key Terms
متحيز
منقسم
مركب
الجوهر الفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It has distinct sides
C) It is composed of atoms
D) It is eternal

Key Takeaway
The first argument shows that spatial extension necessarily entails divisibility and composition, which cannot apply to God.

Second Argument: The Disjunction of Divisibility
Second way of the fourth proof: disjunctive argument for divisibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that a body is either divisible (hence composite) or an indivisible atom (too base for God), so God cannot be a body.

Core Idea
A body is either divisible or an atom – neither suits God.

Razi's second argument presents a clear disjunction: every body is either divisible or not. If divisible, it is composite. If not divisible, it is an indivisible atom – a tiny, contemptible entity. Neither option is fit for the Creator. Let's unpack this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the disjunction: 'either divisible or not'. This is a logical division that covers all possibilities.
This argument does not assume the denial of the indivisible atom; it entertains its possibility.
2
Examine the first branch
If a body is divisible (qābil al-qisma), then it is composed of parts, hence composite. That is clearly composite, which is impossible for God.
3
Examine the second branch
If it is not divisible, then it is the indivisible atom (jawhar fard). Razi describes it as extremely small and contemptible (fī ghāyat al-ṣughr wa al-ḥiqāra). He argues no rational person would attribute this to God.
This branch is meant to be absurd.
4
Draw the final conclusion
Since both possibilities are unacceptable, the initial assumption that God is a body must be false. So God is not a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع: لو كان إله العالم متحيزاً لكان مركبا. وهذا محال، فكونه متحيزاً محال. بيان الملازمة من وجهين: أحدهما: وهو على قول من ينكر الجوهر الفرد، إن كل متحيز فلا بد وأن يتميز أحد جانبيه عن الثاني. وكل ما كان كذلك فهو منقسم. فثبت: أن كل متحيز فهو منقسم ومركب. والثاني: إن كل متحيز فإما أن يكون قابلاً للقسمة أو لا يكون. فإن كان قابلاً للقسمة كان مركبا مؤلفاً، وإن كان غير قابل للقسمة فهو الجوهر الفرد وهو في غاية الصغر والحقارة، وليس في العقلاء أحد يقول هذا القول. فثبت: أنه تعالى لو كان متحيزاً لكان منقسماً.
The fourth proof: If the God of the world were a body (mutaḥayyiz), He would be composite (murakkab). And this is impossible, so His being a body is impossible. Explanation of the entailment in two ways: First – according to the view of one who denies the indivisible atom – every body must have one side distinct from the other. And everything that is like that is divisible. So it is established that every body is divisible and composite. Second: Every body is either divisible or not. If it is divisible, it is composite and compounded. And if it is not divisible, then it is the indivisible atom (jawhar fard), which is extremely small and contemptible, and no rational person holds this view. So it is established that if the Exalted were a body, He would be divisible.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God cannot be a body because every body is composite. Imam Razi offers two lines of reasoning (بيان الملازمة من وجهين). The first argument assumes the denial of the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد): a body necessarily has distinct sides, so it is divisible, and every divisible thing is composite. Thus, any body is composite. The second argument is a disjunction: a body is either divisible or not. If divisible, it is composed of parts, hence composite. If not divisible, it would be an indivisible atom (جوهر فرد), which is tiny and contemptible—a description unsuitable for God, and no rational person would attribute that to God. Therefore, God cannot be a body. The conclusion is that God is not divisible or composite.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the indivisible atom could be a noble concept suitable for God, perhaps thinking of simplicity.
Correction: Razi explicitly states that the atom is extremely small and contemptible (ḥaqīr) and no rational person holds that God is an atom.

The Fourth Proof: Bodily Being Makes God Composite
Imam Razi presents a two-part proof that God cannot be in a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Existent cannot be in a body, and how the proof from compositeness establishes His transcendence.

Core Idea
If God were in a body, He would be composite, and every composite depends on its parts — making God contingent.

Imam Razi now presents his fourth proof for God’s transcendence, focusing on the impossibility of God being in a body. This argument is foundational in Islamic theology for establishing tawhid and rejecting anthropomorphism. The proof proceeds by showing that any body necessarily entails composition, and composition entails contingency, which contradicts God’s necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its two arguments
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice that the proof has a clear structure: a claim, then two ways to support it.
The proof is concise but packed with logical reasoning.
2
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning: if God were in a body, He would be composite → dependent on parts → possible-in-itself → contradiction with necessary existence.
Each step is built on the previous one.
3
Understand why the indivisible atom option fails
The second argument considers the possibility of an indivisible body, but it is extremely small and base — no rational person would attribute that to God. Thus all bodies are composite.
This shows the universality of composition for any body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع : لو كان إله العالم متحيزا ، لكان مركبا. وهذا محال، فيكون كونه متحيزا محالا . بيان الملازمة من وجهين : أحدهما : - وهو على قول من ينكر الجوهر الفرد - إن كل متحيز فلابد وأن يتميز أحد جانبيه عن الثانى . وكل ما كان كذلك ، فهو منقسم : فثبت : أن كل متحيز فهو منقسم ومركب . والثاني : إن كل متحيز فاما أن يكون قابلا للقسمة ، أو لا يكون . فإن كان قابلا للقسمة ، كان مركبا مؤلفا ، وإن كان غير قابل للقسمة ، فهو الجوهر الفرد ـ وهو في غاية الصغر والحقارة - وليس في العقلاء أحد يقول هذا القول . فثبت : أنه تعالى لو كان متحيزا ، لكان منقسما مؤلفا وذلك محال . لأن كل ما كان كذلك ، فهو مفتقر في حقيقته إلى كل واحد من أجزائه ، وكل واحد من أجزائه : غيره . فكل مركب . فهو مفتقر في الحقيقة إلى غيره . وكل ما كان كذلك ، فهو ممكن لذاته فكل مركب : ممكن لذاته . فيلزم أن يكون الممكن لذاته : واجبا لذاته وذلك محال . فيمتنع أن يكون متحيزا .
The fourth proof: If the God of the world were in a body, He would be composite. This is impossible, so His being in a body is impossible. Exposition of the entailment in two ways: The first—according to the one who denies the indivisible atom—every body must have one of its sides distinguished from the other. Everything that is so is divisible. So it is established that every body is divisible and composite. The second: Every body is either divisible or not. If it is divisible, it is composite and made up of parts. If it is not divisible, it is the indivisible atom—which is extreme smallness and baseness—and no rational person says this. So it is established that the Exalted, if He were in a body, He would be divisible and composite, and that is impossible. Because everything that is so is in its reality dependent on each one of its parts, and each one of its parts is other than it. So every composite is in reality dependent on something other than itself. And everything that is so is possible-in-itself. So every composite is possible-in-itself. Thus it would follow that the possible-in-itself is necessary-in-itself, and that is impossible. Therefore, His being in a body is impossible.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) that the Necessary Existent (God) cannot be in a body (mutahayyiz). Imam al-Razi first states the conclusion: if God were in a body, He would be composite, which is impossible, so His being in a body is impossible. He then explains the entailment in two ways. The first way, assuming the denial of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), argues that any body must have distinguishable sides, which entails divisibility, and therefore it is composite. The second way considers two possibilities: either the body is divisible (making it composite) or it is not divisible (making it an indivisible atom, which is extremely small and ignoble—no rational person would claim God is such). Either way, the body is composite. The proof then argues that every composite depends on its parts, each part being other than the whole, so the composite depends on something other than itself. Anything that depends on another is possible-in-itself (mumkin li-dhatihi). Thus a composite is possible-in-itself. If God were composite, He would be possible-in-itself, contradicting His necessary existence (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi). Hence, God cannot be in a body.

Key Terms
متحيز
مركب
الجوهر الفرد
منقسم
ممكن لذاته
واجب لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could be in a body if that body is not composite, e.g., an indivisible atom.
Correction: The proof shows that any body is necessarily composite, either by divisibility (first way) or by absurdity (second way). There is no body that is not composite.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, what is the status of anything that is composite?
A) It is necessary-in-itself
B) It is possible-in-itself
C) It is eternal
D) It is indivisible

Key Takeaway
Any body is composite, and any composite depends on its parts, so it is contingent — therefore God, being necessary, cannot be in a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If God were dependent on parts like creation, He would not be worthy of absolute reliance. Are you placing your trust in created, composite things—people, wealth, status—rather than in the one simple, independent God? What would it look like to transfer that trust today?

The proof shows that what is composite is contingent. In what areas of your life are you still trying to build your identity and security on composite, created supports instead of anchoring yourself in the Necessary Being? Will you let this truth unsettle your false refuges?
?
Do you truly worship God as the Necessary Existent who needs nothing, or do you subconsciously imagine Him as a powerful being within the world, subject to limitations and needs? How would your prayer change if you fully internalized that He is not in a body, not composite, not contingent?

The Fifth Proof: Parts Lead to Multiple Gods or a Trivial God
Imam Razi argues that if God were in a body, He would be composed of parts, leading to absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's fifth proof against God being in a body, including the logical dilemma and its resolution.

Core Idea
If God had a body, He would be either trivial or multiple — both impossible.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from Imam al-Razi's Asas al-Taqdis. The proof systematically demolishes the notion that Allah could be in a body by showing that it leads to absurd consequences. Imam al-Razi uses a dilemma to force the opponent to choose between a trivial God or multiple gods, both of which are rationally untenable. The proof also addresses an objection based on human composition, demonstrating the uniqueness of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Let the logical flow become clear.
Notice the strong rational tone of kalam arguments.
2
Identify the initial premise
The proof starts by stating that no rational person claims God is as small as an indivisible atom, so if God were in a body, He must be composed of parts.
3
Follow the first dilemma
Either the subject of attributes is a single part (trivial god) or the aggregate (multiple gods). Examine each branch and why it fails.
The second branch splits into two sub-branches.
4
Consider the objection and answer
An objector says the same reasoning would deny human bodily existence. Imam al-Razi replies that we know by necessity that a human is a body.
This highlights the difference between necessary knowledge of creation and rational deduction about the Creator.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Think about how this proof reinforces tanzih (transcendence). Why is it impossible for Allah to have a body?
The proof is not just abstract; it protects the Islamic conception of God's oneness and perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الخامس : إنه لو كان متحيزا ، لكان مركبا من الأجزاء ... إذ ليس فى العقلاء من يقول إنه فى حجم الجوهر الفرد . ولو كان مركبا من الأجزاء . فإما أن يكون الموصوف بالعلم والقدرة والحياة جزءا واحدا من ذلك المجموع ، أو يكون الموصوف بهذه الصفات مجموع تلك الأجزاء ، فإن كان الأول كان إله العالم هو ذلك الجزء الواحد. فيكون إله العالم في غاية الصغر والحقارة . وقد بينا : أنه ليس في العقلاء من يقول بذلك . وإن كان الثاني فإما أن يقال : القائم بمجموع تلك الأجزاء علم واحد وقدرة واحدة أو يقال : القائم بكل واحد من تلك الأجزاء : علم على حدة ، وقدرة على حدة ؟ والأول محال لأنه يقتضى قيام الصفة الواحدة بالمحال الكثيرة . وذلك محال . وإن كان الثاني ، لزم أن يكون كل واحد منها إلها قديما. وذلك يقتضى تكثر الآلهة. وهو محال . فإن قيل : هذا يشكل بالإنسان . فإن ما ذكرتم قائم فيه بعينه . فيلزم أن لا يكون جسما . وهذه مكابرة . لأنا نعلم بالضرورة. أن الإنسان ليس إلا هذه البنية .
The fifth proof: If He were in a body, He would be composed of parts... since no rational person says that He is the size of an indivisible atom. If He were composed of parts, then either the one described with knowledge, power, and life is one part of that aggregate, or the one described with these attributes is the aggregate of those parts. If the first, then the God of the world is that single part, so the God of the world would be extreme smallness and baseness. And we have shown that no rational person says that. If the second, then either it is said that the subsistent with the aggregate of those parts is one knowledge and one power, or it is said that the subsistent with each one of those parts is a separate knowledge and a separate power. The first is impossible because it requires the subsistence of a single attribute in many loci, and that is impossible. If the second, then it would follow that each one of them is an eternal god, and that requires a multiplicity of gods, which is impossible. If it is said: This creates a problem with the human being, for what you have mentioned applies exactly to him, and it would follow that he is not a body. This is stubbornness, for we know by necessity that the human being is nothing but this structure.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is the fifth proof (burhan khamis) against God being in a body. Imam al-Razi begins by stating that no rational person claims God is as small as an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), so if God were in a body, He would be composed of parts. He then presents a dilemma: either (1) the subject of knowledge, power, and life is a single part of the aggregate, or (2) the subject is the whole aggregate. Option (1) would make God that single part, which is extremely small and base—unacceptable. Option (2) splits into two sub-options: (2a) the aggregate as a whole possesses one knowledge and one power, or (2b) each part possesses its own knowledge and power. (2a) is impossible because a single attribute cannot inhere in multiple loci. (2b) would make each part a separate eternal god, implying polytheism. The proof ends with an objection: the same reasoning would apply to humans, implying humans are not bodies. The answer is that this is stubbornness, because we know by immediate intuition (darura) that a human is nothing but this bodily structure (which is composite and dependent, thus not divine).

Key Terms
متحيز
مركب من الأجزاء
الجوهر الفرد
صفة
محل
تكثير الآلهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof would also make humans non-bodily.
Correction: Imam al-Razi answers that humans are known by necessity to be bodies, but God is not.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if each part of God's supposed body had its own knowledge and power?
A) The aggregate would have one knowledge
B) The single part would be God
C) There would be many eternal gods
D) It would be impossible for God to exist

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof shows that attributing a body to Allah leads to logical absurdities, thus affirming His absolute transcendence.

If God cannot be composed of parts, what does that demand of your understanding of His absolute unity? Are there any 'parts' in your life—people, possessions, or ambitions—that you treat as if they share in His exclusive rights of lordship or worship?
?
This proof refutes the possibility of many gods. Where in your heart do you still harbour a subtle belief that something besides Allah can independently cause benefit or harm? What would it take to uproot that belief today?
?
The objection from human composition is dismissed as 'stubbornness'. Is there an area of tawheed where you find yourself making excuses or rationalising a compromise? Will you now submit to the clarity of this proof and correct that tendency?

Why God Cannot Be a Body: The Composition Argument
Razi proves God's non-composition via dependency argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Being cannot be composed or bodily, using the classical kalam argument from composition.

Core Idea
Every composite depends on its parts; thus it is contingent, not necessary.

This passage presents a foundational argument for divine transcendence. Imam al-Razi demonstrates that the concept of a composite being contradicts the nature of the Necessary Existent. Understanding this logic is essential for grasping why God in Islamic theology is not a body, unlike some anthropomorphic conceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow.
Note the key terms: murakkab, mumkin li-dhatihi, wajib li-dhatihi.
2
Identify the premises
The argument has two main premises: (1) every composite depends on its parts, and (2) whatever depends on another is contingent. Confirm these from the text.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: If God were composite, He would be contingent. But God is necessary by definition. This leads to a contradiction.
The conclusion is that God cannot be composite, hence not a body.
4
Address a common misunderstanding
This argument denies physical composition, not the multiplicity of attributes. Distinguish between essential parts and divine attributes.
Review the 'misconception' field for clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مؤلفا وذلك محال . لأن كل ما كان كذلك ، فهو مفتقر في حقيقته إلى كل واحد من أجزائه ، وكل واحد من أجزائه : غيره . فكل مركب . فهو مفتقر في الحقيقة إلى غيره . وكل ما كان كذلك ، فهو ممكن لذاته فكل مركب : ممكن لذاته . فيلزم أن يكون الممكن لذاته : واجبا لذاته وذلك محال . فيمتنع أن يكون متحيزا .
Composed, and that is impossible. Because everything that is such is in its essence dependent on each one of its parts, and each one of its parts is other than it. So every composite is in reality dependent on other than it. And everything that is such is contingent by itself. So every composite is contingent by itself. Then it would follow that the contingent by itself is necessary by itself, and that is impossible. Therefore it is impossible that He be a body (mutahayyiz).
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God cannot be a body. It begins by stating that being composed (mu'allif) is impossible for God. The reasoning is: if something is composed, then its true reality depends on each of its parts, and each part is other than the whole. Therefore, every composite depends on something other than itself. Whatever depends on another is contingent in itself. Hence, every composite is contingent in itself. But God is necessarily existent by Himself (wajib li-dhatihi). If we suppose a composite to be necessary, it would be both contingent and necessary – a contradiction. Therefore, the Necessary Being cannot be composite, and since every body is composite, He cannot be a body (mutahayyiz).

Key Terms
مركب (murakkab)
ممكن لذاته (mumkin li-dhatihi)
واجب لذاته (wajib li-dhatihi)
متحيز (mutahayyiz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God has any attributes or multiplicity.
Correction: The argument only denies physical composition (being made of parts). God can still have multiple attributes as long as they are not parts that compose His essence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that the Necessary Being cannot be a body?
A) Because bodies are temporal
B) Because bodies are composite and composition implies contingency
C) Because bodies are created
D) Because bodies are limited

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being cannot be composite because composition entails dependence and thus contingency, contradicting necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God cannot depend on anything, what does that mean for the ways you rely on people, possessions, or even your own abilities? Are you treating any part of creation as if it were necessary for your existence?
?
Razi proves that God is not a body. When you imagine God, do you still have subtle mental images that limit Him? What do you need to purge from your imagination to truly worship the Transcendent?
?
The proof shows that dependence implies contingency. In your spiritual life, where do you find yourself depending on created things for your sense of meaning? How can you shift that dependence entirely to the Necessary Being?

Two Distinctions between the Witnessed and the Unseen
Razi presents two distinctions between the witnessed and the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key distinctions between the witnessed and the unseen used to refute anthropomorphism, as argued by al-Ash'ari and Ibn al-Rawandi.

Core Idea
What applies to creation cannot be transferred to the Creator.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi discusses two arguments from earlier scholars that demonstrate how reasoning from the witnessed world (al-shahid) cannot be directly applied to the unseen (al-gha'ib). These distinctions protect divine transcendence by showing that human composition and simplicity lead to impossibilities or absurdities when attributed to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice that Razi presents two separate arguments from two scholars.
The passage uses technical terms 'al-shahid' and 'al-gha'ib'.
2
Understand al-Ash'ari's argument from composition
Al-Ash'ari argues that humans are composed of parts, each with its own knowledge and power. This is acceptable for humans but applying it to God would imply multiple gods, which is impossible.
3
Understand Ibn al-Rawandi's argument from simplicity
Ibn al-Rawandi argues that man is a simple indivisible substance, which entails lowliness. This is acceptable for man but applying to God would imply lowliness, which no rational person accepts.
Ibn al-Rawandi was a Mu'tazili, but his argument is used here.
4
Reflect on the principle
Both arguments show that what is true for creatures cannot be transferred to God. The distinctions guard against anthropomorphism.
This principle is central in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما سائر الطوائف والفرق فقد ذكروا الفرق بين الشاهد والغائب من وجهين: أحدهما: قال الأشعري: كل واحد من أجزاء الإنسان موصوف بعلم على حدة وقدرة على حدة وهذا يقتضي أن يكون هذا البدن مركبا من أشياء كثيرة وكل واحد منها عالم قادر حي وهذا ما لا نزاع فيه وأما التزام ذلك في حق الله سبحانه وتعالى فإنه يقتضي تعدد الآلهة وذلك محال فظهر الفرق الثاني: قال ابن الراوندي: الإنسان جزء واحد لا يتجزأ في القلب وهذا يقتضي أن يكون الإنسان في غاية الحقارة وذلك غير ممتنع أما لو قلنا بمثله في حق الله تعالى فإنه يلزم منه كونه في غاية الحقارة وذلك لم يقل به عاقل
And as for the other sects and groups, they mentioned the difference between the witnessed and the unseen in two ways: First: Al-Ash'ari said: Each one of the parts of man is described with knowledge individually and power individually. And this requires that this body be composed of many things, each of which is knowing, powerful, living. And this is indisputable. But applying that to God, may He be glorified and exalted, requires multiplicity of gods, and that is impossible. So the difference appears. Second: Ibn al-Rawandi said: Man is a single part that does not divide in the heart. And this requires that man be in extreme lowliness. And that is not impossible. But if we said the like of it concerning God Almighty, then it would entail His being in extreme lowliness, and no rational person has said that.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents two distinctions between the witnessed (al-shāhid) and the unseen (al-ghā'ib) used to refute anthropomorphism. First, according to al-Ash'ari, human beings are composed of parts, each with its own knowledge and power, making the human body a composite of knowing, powerful, living substances. This is acceptable for humans. But if we say the same about God, it would require multiple gods, which is impossible. Thus the difference is clear. Second, according to Ibn al-Rawandi, man is a single indivisible part (a simple substance) in the heart, which entails extreme lowliness. While that is conceivable for man, applying it to God would imply His lowliness, which no rational person accepts. Both distinctions show that attributes of creatures cannot be transferred to the Creator.

Key Terms
الشاهد
الغائب
الوجهان
أجزاء الإنسان
تعدد الآلهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if humans are composed of parts with knowledge and power, then God could also be composed similarly without contradiction.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that applying composition to God would require multiple gods, which is impossible. The passage also shows that simplicity in humans implies lowliness, which cannot apply to God.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari's distinction, why does applying human composition to God lead to an impossible conclusion?
A) Because man is a simple substance, not composed
B) Because it would require multiple gods
C) Because God is not described with knowledge and power
D) Because it would make God lowly

Key Takeaway
Two distinctions from al-Ash'ari and Ibn al-Rawandi show that what applies to creatures cannot be transferred to the Creator, thereby safeguarding divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you ever unconsciously imagine Him like a powerful human? This passage forces you to stop that. Can you identify one attribute you've mentally pictured in a human way? Commit to replacing that image with the understanding that even the name of the attribute is different in its reality.
?
The second argument uses 'extreme lowliness' for man. Have you ever felt your own lowliness before God? Reflect on a moment when you were reminded of your smallness. How does that feeling actually draw you closer to God when you realize it's a reminder that He is infinitely beyond such lowliness?
?
Both arguments rely on the idea that what is true of creation is not transferable to the Creator. Name one area of your life where you have been comparing God to human standards (e.g., expecting Him to act like a human judge or parent). What step will you take today to realign your thinking with divine transcendence?

Refuting the Third Objection: Specific Knowledge and Power Imply Polytheism
Razi refutes the third objection by showing its polytheistic implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing specific knowledge and power to each part of a composite being leads to affirming multiple gods, contradicting the oneness of Allah.

Core Idea
Specific knowledge and power in each part implies many limited gods, not one all-encompassing God.

Imam Razi continues to refute objections to the doctrine of divine simplicity. The third objection claims that each part of a composite being could have its own specific knowledge and power. Razi shows that this would require each part to be a distinct god, destroying the unity of Allah. This lesson explains his logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: objection, rejection, reason, conclusion.
The text is concise but dense.
2
Identify the objection
What does the opponent claim about each part? Find the phrase: كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء يكون موصوفا بعلم متعلق بعلوم معينة وبقدرة متعلقة بمقدور معين.
This is the 'third question' mentioned earlier in the text.
3
Understand the reasoning
Razi says this is impossible (محال). Why? Because it requires that each part be knowing and powerful over specific things. Trace the logical chain: if each part has its own attributes, then each part is a distinct god.
Note the word 'يقتضي' (requires).
4
Grasp the conclusion
The outcome is affirming many gods (آلهة كثيرة). How does that contradict 'إله واحد'? Reflect on the oneness of God.
This is a classic kalam argument for tawhid.
5
Check your understanding
Try to explain the argument to someone else. Use the quiz to test your comprehension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثالث وهو قولهم كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء يكون موصوفا بعلم متعلق بعلوم معينة وبقدرة متعلقة بمقدور معين فنقول هذا أيضا محال لأنه يقتضي كون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء عالما بمعلومات معينة قادرا على مقدورات معينة فيرجع حاصل الكلام إلى إثبات آلهة كثيرة كل واحد منها مخصوص بمعرفة بعض المعلومات وبالقدرة على بعض المقدورات وذلك يناقض القول بأن إله [المـ؟] موجود واحد والله أعلم
And as for the third question, which is their saying: Each one of those parts is described with knowledge related to specific known things and with power related to a specific object of power. So we say: This is also impossible. Because it requires that each one of those parts be knowing of specific known things, powerful over specific objects of power. So the upshot of the speech returns to affirming many gods, each one of which is specialized in knowing some known things and in power over some objects of power. And that contradicts the statement that the God of [the world?] is one, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage refutes a third objection: each part of a composite being might have distinct knowledge and power over particular things. Razi argues this leads to multiple gods, each with limited knowledge and power, contradicting the oneness of God. The outcome is polytheism, which is absurd. The argument reinforces tawhid by showing that attributing distributive divine attributes to parts results in a multiplicity of limited deities, incompatible with the unique, all-encompassing God.

Sixth Proof: God is Not a Body – Motion Refutation
Razi presents a sixth proof against divine corporeality using a motion dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's sixth proof that God cannot be a body, using the impossibility of motion as a reductio argument.

Core Idea
If God were a body, motion would lead to polytheism; no motion leads to contradiction.

This passage presents Razi's sixth argument against divine corporeality. He uses a dilemma: if God were a body, then motion on Him is either possible or impossible. Both options lead to unacceptable conclusions. The first option would allow any moving body to be a potential god, which is absurd. The second option, that God is a motionless body, is also rejected, though the refutation is left unfinished here.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Razi sets up a conditional: if God were a body, then motion on Him is either possible or impossible. This divides the argument into two cases.
2
Analyze the first division
If motion is possible for God, then any body that can move (like the sun, moon, sphere) could be a god, because they only have three alleged defects: composition, finitude, motion. If those don't disqualify God, they don't disqualify celestial bodies. This leads to polytheism, so the first division is invalid.
Notice how Razi uses the same criteria to show inconsistency.
3
Consider the second division
If motion is impossible for God (though He is a body), Razi asserts this is false in several ways, but the text cuts off. Reflect on why a motionless body would still be a problem.
The argument is incomplete here; the full refutation would continue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السادس إنه تعالى لو كان جسما لكانت الحركة عليه إما أن تكون جائزة أو لا تكون جائزة والقسم الأول باطل لأنه لما لم يمتنع أن يكون الجسم الذي تكون الحركة عليه جائزة إلها فلم لا يجوز أن يكون إله العالم هو الشمس أو القمر أو الفلك وذلك لأن هذه الأجسام ليس فيها عيب يمنع من إلهيتها إلا أمور ثلاثة وهي كونها مركبة من الأجزاء وكونها محدودة متناهية وكونها موصوفة بالحركة والسكون فإذا لم تكن هذه الأشياء مانعة من الإلهية فكيف يمكن الطعن في إلهيتها وذلك عين الكفر والإلحاد وإنكار الصانع تعالى والقسم الثاني وهو أن يقال إنه تعالى جسم ولكن الانتقال والحركة عليه محال فنقول هذا باطل من وجوه
The sixth proof: If God Almighty were a body, then motion upon Him would either be possible or not possible. The first division is invalid, because when it is not impossible that a body on which motion is possible be a god, then why is it not permissible that the God of the world be the sun, or the moon, or the sphere? This is because these bodies have no defect that prevents their divinity except three things: their being composed of parts, their being limited and finite, and their being described with motion and rest. So if these things are not impediments to divinity, how can one impugn their divinity? And that is pure disbelief and atheism and denial of the Creator, exalted be He. And the second division: that He, may He be exalted, is a body, but transition and motion upon Him are impossible. So we say: This is false in several ways:
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents a sixth proof against God being a body. It divides the possibility of motion for a body into two cases. First, if motion is possible for God, then any body with motion (like the sun, moon, or sphere) could be a god, since the usual objections (composition, finitude, motion) are the same. If those don't disqualify God, then they don't disqualify celestial bodies either – which is polytheism. Thus the first case is absurd. Second, if motion is impossible for God, one might say God is a body but motion is impossible. Razi states this is false, but the text cuts off listing the refutation. The argument intends to show that both options lead to unacceptable consequences, hence God is not a body.

Key Terms

الحركة جائزة
الحركة غير جائزة
مركبة من الأجزاء
محدودة متناهية
الحركة والسكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A motionless body could be a valid conception of God.
Correction: Razi argues that even if motion is impossible for a bodily God, that position is false in several ways (though the refutation is cut off).

Checkpoint

A) God would be omnipotent
B) Celestial bodies could also be gods
C) God is not composed
D) Motion is impossible for God

Key Takeaway
Razi's sixth proof shows that if God were a body, the possibility of motion would imply that any moving celestial body could be a god, which is absurd; the impossibility of motion is also rejected, so God cannot be a body.

Refuting Polytheism from Divine Parts
Razi refutes the corporealist objection that each divine part has its own attributes, leading to polytheism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing distinct knowledge and power to supposed parts of God leads to affirming many gods, and how Imam Razi refutes this corporealist argument.

Core Idea
A god composed of parts with unique knowledge and power is many gods, not one.

In this passage, Imam Razi tackles the third objection from those who claim God has parts. The opponents argue that each part has its own specific knowledge and power. Razi shows that this leads to affirming multiple limited gods, which contradicts the core Islamic belief in one all-knowing, all-powerful God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the flow of objection and response become clear.
Notice the 'question' and 'we say' structure.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
The opponents assert that each part of God is described with specific knowledge and power. Underline the part of the passage that states this.
3
Understand Razi's refutation
Razi argues that this necessitates each part being knowing and powerful over specific things, which reduces to affirming many gods. Consider why this is impossible.
The key is that each limited part would be a separate god.
4
Connect to tawhid
Reflect on how this contradicts the oneness of God. Why is it essential that God is one and not composed of parts with independent attributes?
Tawhid demands that God is unique and indivisible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثالث : وهو قولهم : كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء ، يكون موصوفا ، بعلم متعلق بعلوم معين ، وبقدرة متعلقة بمقدور معين . فنقول : هذا أيضا محال . لأنه يقتضى كون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء عالما بمعلومات معينة ، قادرا على مقدورات معينة . فيرجع حاصل الكلام إلى اثبات آلهة كثيرة كل واحد منها مخصوص بمعرفة بعض المعلومات، وبالقدرة على بعض المقدورات . وذلك يناقض القول بأن إله المـ[؟] موجود واحد (والله أعلم).
As for the third question: And it is their statement: 'Each one of those parts is described with knowledge related to specific known things, and with power related to specific things possible.' So we say: This also is impossible. Because it necessitates that each one of those parts be knowing of specific known things, powerful over specific things possible. So the upshot of the discourse reduces to affirming many gods, each one of which is particularized with knowledge of some things known and with power over some things possible. And that contradicts the statement that the God of the [؟] is one God (and God knows best).
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. Imam Razi presents the third objection of those who argue for divine corporeality: they claim that each part of the divine body would have its own specific knowledge and power. Razi responds that this is impossible because it leads to a plurality of gods, each limited in knowledge and power. This contradicts the core Islamic belief in one God who is all-knowing and all-powerful. The phrase 'إله المـ' is uncertain in the OCR; likely means 'the God of the world' or 'the God of everything'. The conclusion is that such a conception negates tawhid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A composite being can still be one god if its parts work in harmony.
Correction: Razi shows that each part would have independent attributes, making it a separate god, thus polytheism.

C) It shows God's power is unlimited.

Key Takeaway
Attributing distinct attributes to supposed parts of God results in polytheism, not monotheism.

Sixth Proof: God Cannot Be a Body
Imam Razi argues that if God were a body, motion would be either possible or impossible, both absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be a body, as demonstrated by Imam Razi's sixth proof through a logical dichotomy.

Core Idea
A body cannot be God because it leads to either polytheism or imperfection.

In this lesson, Imam Razi presents a powerful proof for divine transcendence. He considers the hypothetical that God is a body and shows that both possibilities—motion being possible or impossible—lead to absurd conclusions. This argument refutes anthropomorphism and affirms that God is beyond physical categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical structure: a dichotomy is introduced, and each branch is refuted.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Two Cases
The proof splits the hypothesis 'God is a body' into two cases: either motion is possible for Him, or it is not possible. Recognize which case is discussed first and which second.
These are the two main divisions of the argument.
3
Analyze the First Case
Examine why the first case (motion possible) leads to absurdity: if motion is possible for a bodily God, then celestial bodies like the sun and moon would also qualify as gods, which is polytheism. Understand the three supposed defects (composition, finitude, motion) that are not actually disqualifying.
This refutation cuts off the first branch.
4
Analyze the Second Case
Now explore the second case (motion impossible). Razi gives three sub-arguments: it would make God like a paralyzed person (imperfect), it would cause similarity to all bodies (making motion permissible), and it contradicts anthropomorphists who themselves describe God with motion and rest. This shows the impossibility of the second branch.
Each sub-argument independently refutes this case.
5
Conclude the Proof
The final sentence summarizes the conclusion: God is not a body, not a substance, and not a spatial entity. Reflect on how the proof establishes divine transcendence.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السادس : إنه تعالى لو كان جسما، لكانت الحركة عليه إما أن تكون جائزة أو لا تكون جائزة . والقسم الأول باطل . لأنه لما لم يمتنع أن يكون الجسم الذي تكون الحركة عليه جائزة : إلها. فلم لا يجوز أن يكون إله العالم هو الشمس أو القمر أو الفلك؟ وذلك لأن هذه الأجسام ليس فيها عيب يمنع من إلهيتها إلا أمور ثلاثة . وهى كونها مركبة من الأجزاء ، وكونها محدودة متناهية، وكونها موصوفة بالحركة والسكون . فإذا لم تكن هذه الأشياء مانعة من الإلهية، فكيف يمكن الطعن في الهيتها ؟ وذالك عين الكفر والإلحاد ، و إنكار الصانع - تعالى - والقسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى جسم ، ولكن الانتقال والحركة عليه : محال . فنقول : هذا باطل . من وجوه : الأول : إن هذا يكون كالزمن المقعد ، الذي لا يقدر على الحركة . وهذه صفة نقص ، وهو على الله تعالى : محال . الثاني : إنه تعالى لما كان جسما ، كان مثلا لسائر الأجسام . فكانت الحركة جائزة عليه . الثالث : إن القائلين بكونه جسما مؤلفا من الأجزاء والأبعاض ، لا يمنعون من جواز الحركة عليه . فإنهم يصفونه بالذهاب والمجيء . فتارة يقولون: إنه جالس على العرش ، وقدماه على الكرسي - وهذا هو السكون - وتارة يقولون : إنه ينزل إلى سماء الدنيا . وهذا هو الحركة . فهذا مجموع الدلائل على أنه تعالى ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر . وبالجملة فليس بمتحيز .
The sixth proof: If the Most High were a body, then motion with respect to it would either be possible or not possible. The first division is false. Because since it is not impossible that the body for which motion is possible be a god, why is it not permissible that the god of the world be the sun or the moon or the sphere? That is because these bodies have no defect preventing their divinity except three things: they are composed of parts, they are limited and finite, and they are described with motion and rest. So if these things are not preventers of divinity, how can one object to their divinity? That is pure unbelief and atheism and denial of the Maker – Exalted. And the second division: That it is said: 'He is a body, but transition and motion with respect to Him are impossible.' So we say: This is false. From several aspects: First: This would be like the paralyzed person who cannot move. And this is a description of deficiency, and that with respect to God Most High is impossible. Second: Since He is a body, He would be similar to all other bodies, so motion would be possible with respect to Him. Third: Those who say He is a body composed of parts and members do not prevent the permissibility of motion with respect to Him. For they describe Him with going and coming. Sometimes they say: He is sitting on the Throne, His feet on the Footstool – and this is rest; and sometimes they say: He descends to the heaven of the world – and this is motion. So this is the totality of proofs that the Most High is not a body, not a substance, and in summary, not a spatial entity.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) demonstrating that God cannot be a body. Imam Razi divides the hypothesis into two cases: either motion is possible for God or it is not. 

Case 1: If motion is possible for God, then there is no bar to worshipping celestial bodies like the sun, moon, and spheres, because their only defects are composition, finitude, and motion – but if these do not preclude divinity, then polytheism follows. This is absurd and constitutes disbelief. 

Case 2: If motion is impossible for God, then He would be like a paralyzed invalid, a clear imperfection. Moreover, if He were a body, He would be similar to other bodies, and thus motion would be possible. Thirdly, those who affirm a bodily God (anthropomorphists) themselves attribute movement to Him (e.g., sitting and descending), so they cannot claim motion is impossible. 

The conclusion is that God is neither a body nor a substance, nor extended in space.

Key Terms
جسم
جوهر
متحيز
حركة
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can be a material body that moves and changes location.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that a bodily God leads either to polytheism (if motion possible) or to imperfection (if motion impossible), so God must be immaterial and beyond spatial attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, if motion is possible for God, what consequence follows?
A) God would be imperfect.
B) Celestial bodies could be gods as well.
C) God would be similar to humans.
D) God would not be able to act.

Key Takeaway
If God were a body, either motion is possible (leading to polytheism) or impossible (leading to deficiency); both are absurd, so God is not a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself mentally picturing God as having a shape, location, or movement? How does Razi's proof challenge that mental image, and what concrete step will you take today to purify your conception of God?
?
If you believe that divine attributes like sitting or descending must be taken literally, how do you reconcile that with the absurdities Razi points out? What would it cost you to accept that God is beyond such limitations, and are you willing to trust that His revelation is true without physical imagination?
?
The first branch of the proof shows that accepting a bodily God opens the door to polytheism. In your heart, do you truly believe that only the Creator is worthy of worship, without any physical likeness? What will you do to ensure that no created thing—whether a mental image or a physical object—competes with your devotion to Allah?

The Opponent’s First Doubt: Spatial Separation Implies Corporeality
The opponent enumerates the first doubt with a full syllogism leading to God's corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the syllogistic structure of the first doubt that argues God must be a body because He is separate from the world by direction.

Core Idea
Spatial separation from the world, if true, forces God into either an atom or a composite body.

Imam Razi now presents the first of the opponent’s arguments. The opponent constructs a logical syllogism: since both God and the world exist, they must be related either by interpenetration or spatial separation. After dismissing interpenetration, the opponent concludes that God is extended, which leads to the conclusion that He is either an indivisible atom (absurd) or a composite body. This argument forces the reader to consider whether spatial separation necessarily implies corporeality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text of the first doubt carefully, noting the logical steps.
2
Identify the syllogism
Break down the argument into premises and conclusion. The first premise: every two existents are either interpenetrating or spatially separate. Second premise: God cannot interpenetrate the world. Conclusion: God is spatially separate.
Notice the disjunction.
3
Analyze the consequence
The opponent then deduces that spatial separation implies being extended (mutahayyiz). Then from extension, two possibilities: indivisible atom (unworthy of God) or composite body (the intended).
This is a dilemma: either way leads to corporeality.
4
Reflect on the assumption
Ask yourself: Is the original disjunction exhaustive? Could two existents be related in a non-spatial way? This is where the answer will intervene.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما شبه الخصم . فمن وجوه : الشبهة الأولى : إن العالم موجود ، والبارى تعالى موجود . وكل موجودين فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر ، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة. وكون البارى تعالى ساريا في العالم محال . فلا بد وأن يكون مباينا عنه بالجهة . وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متحيز . ثم إنه إما أن يكون غير منقسم ، فيكون فى الصغر والحقارة كالجوهر الفرد . وهو محال . وإما أن يكون شيئا كبيرا مركبا من الأجزاء والأبعاض . وهو المقصود . الشبهة الثانية : إنا لم نشاهد حيا عالما قادرا ، إلا وهو جسم. وإثبات.
As for the doubts of the opponent, they are from several aspects: The first doubt: The world exists, and the Creator Most High exists. And every two existents must either have one pervading the other, or be separate from each other by direction. And that the Creator Most High pervade the world is impossible. So He must be separate from it by direction. And everything that is like that is a spatial entity. Then it is either indivisible, and then it would be in smallness and lowliness like the indivisible atom, and that is impossible; or it would be something large composed of parts and members, and that is the intended. The second doubt: We have not witnessed a living, knowing, powerful being unless it is a body. And to affirm...
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the first two doubts (shubah) of those who assert that God is a body. The first doubt is a complete syllogism: The world and the Creator both exist; any two existents must either interpenetrate or be spatially separate. Since God cannot dwell within the world, He must be separate by direction. Anything separate by direction is extended (mutahayyiz). An extended thing is either indivisible (like an atom) or composite. Indivisibility leads to extreme smallness (unworthy of God), so the only option is composition into a large body with parts. Thus, the opponent concludes God must be a body. The second doubt is an inductive argument: every observed living, knowing, powerful being is a body; therefore God (who is living, knowing, powerful) must also be a body. This second doubt is incomplete in the text but is clearly a common empiricist argument. Imam Razi does not answer these doubts here; he will refute them later.

مباين

متحيز
جوهر فرد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any two things that exist must be related either by interpenetration or spatial separation, ignoring non-spatial relationships like transcendence.
Correction: Islamic theology denies that spatial separation is the only alternative to interpenetration; God is beyond spatial categories altogether.

Checkpoint
In the first doubt, what conclusion does the opponent draw from the necessity of spatial separation?
A) God must be an indivisible atom.
B) God must be non-existent.
C) God must be a composite body.
D) God must be identical with the world.

Key Takeaway
The opponent’s first doubt uses a forced dichotomy and dilemma to argue that God must be a composite body.

?

?
The opponent's dichotomy is forced and incomplete. What assumptions about God's nature do you hold that might be incomplete or borrowed from worldly experiences? The Opponent’s Second Doubt: The Empirical Argument for God’s Corporeality
The opponent presents an inductive doubt: all observed living agents are bodies, so God must be a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will recognize the inductive empirical argument that since all observed living, knowing, powerful beings are bodies, God must also be a body.

Core Idea
Induction from created beings to the Creator is a flawed basis for denying transcendence.

The opponent presents a second doubt, this time rooted in empirical observation. They note that every living, knowing, powerful being we have encountered is a body. Since God is described as living, knowing, and powerful, they conclude that God must also be a body. This argument relies on inductive generalization and ignores the uniqueness of the Creator. The passage as given is incomplete, but the gist is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage
Read the Arabic for the second doubt. It is only a few words.
2
Identify the argument
The opponent states that we have never seen a living, knowing, powerful being that is not a body. The implied conclusion is that God, who has those attributes, must be a body.
This is an argument from analogy/induction.
3
Consider the flaw
Think about whether this induction is valid. Does the absence of observed counterexamples prove the rule? Is God comparable to created beings?
This argument will be refuted by Imam Razi.

The Argument from Direction for Anthropomorphism
Imam Razi presents the opponent's first argument that God must be spatial because He is distinct from the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and premises of the opponent's first argument that God must be a body, which Imam Razi presents to later refute.

Core Idea
The opponent's argument forces God into spatial categories by assuming existence implies location.

Imam Razi begins refuting anthropomorphism by presenting the first of several sophistical arguments. This argument attempts to prove that God must be a body by starting from the simple fact that both God and the world exist. Understanding this argument is crucial to later appreciating its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'sarin' and 'mutahayyiz'.
2
Identify the premises
List the premises: 1) Both world and God exist. 2) Any two existents must either interpenetrate or be separated by direction. 3) God cannot interpenetrate the world. 4) Therefore, God is separated by direction. 5) Anything separated by direction is spatially extended. 6) A spatially extended thing is either indivisible (like an atom) or divisible into parts.
3
Examine the conclusion
The opponent concludes that God must be a large body composed of parts, because the alternative (indivisible atom) is too insignificant for God. This is their intended claim.
The conclusion is not explicitly stated as 'God is a body', but it follows from the options.
4
Reflect on the hidden assumption
Consider the assumption that 'separation by direction' necessarily implies spatial extension. This is exactly what Imam Razi will reject in his refutation.
The common misunderstanding notes that this assumption is not accepted by those who affirm God's existence without location.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى : إن العالم موجود ، والبارى تعالى موجود . وكل موجودين فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر ، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة . وكون البارى تعالى ساريا في العالم محال . فلا بد وأن يكون مباينا عنه بالجهة . وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متحيز . ثم إنه إما أن يكون غير منقسم ، فيكون في الصغر والحقارة كالجوهر الفرد . وهو محال . وإما أن يكون شيئا كبيرا مركبا من الأجزاء والأبعاض . وهو المقصود .
The first sophistical argument: The world exists, and the Creator Most High exists. And every two existents must either be such that one pervades the other, or is separated from it by direction. And it is impossible for the Creator Most High to pervade the world. So He must be separated from it by direction. And everything that is like that is spatially extended. Then either it is indivisible, and thus in smallness and insignificance like the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) — and that is impossible — or it is something large composed of parts and organs — and that is the intended (point).
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the first sophistical argument (shubha) of the opponents who assert that God must be a body. The argument proceeds as follows: (1) The world exists and God exists. (2) Any two existing things must either interpenetrate (sarin) each other or be separate from each other by direction (jiha). (3) It is impossible for God to interpenetrate the world. (4) Therefore, God must be separate from the world by direction. (5) Anything that is separate by direction is spatially extended (mutahayyiz). (6) A spatially extended thing is either indivisible (like the atom, jawhar fard) which is too insignificant for God, or divisible into parts (which is the intended conclusion that God is a body composed of parts). The opponent concludes that God is a body. Imam Razi presents this to refute it later.

Key Terms
موجود
سارٍ
مباين
جهة
متحيز
جوهر فرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that 'separation by direction' necessarily implies spatial extension.
Correction: Those who affirm God's existence without location reject that separation by direction implies spatial extension. God is not in a direction, nor is He extended.

Checkpoint
In the first shubha, what is the opponent's conclusion about God?
A) God is not a body.
B) God must be a body composed of parts.
C) God is an indivisible atom.
D) God pervades the world.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's argument for God being a body rests on the assumption that distinct existents must be separated by direction, which implies spatial extension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah, do you unconsciously think of Him as 'somewhere' — perhaps above the heavens? Where does that image come from in your heart, and how does it affect your awe of Him?
?
The opponent here insists that Allah must be either like a tiny atom or a large body. Have you ever caught yourself reducing Allah to something you can mentally picture or measure? What is the danger of that habit for your faith?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is not contained by any direction or space, how would that change the way you seek closeness to Him in your daily worship?

The Argument from Observed Beings: All Living Bodies?
Imam Razi presents the opponent's inductive argument from observation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning behind the anthropomorphist argument that God must be a body based on observation, and why this argument is insufficient.

Core Idea
Observation of creatures does not limit the Creator — analogy fails for the unique.

Imam Razi now presents the second objection from those who claim God must be a body. They rely on a simple inductive observation: every living being we have ever seen is a body. But can we extrapolate from the seen to the unseen Creator? This passage sets up the challenge before Razi dismantles it.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the core claim.
2
Identify the inductive premise
Notice the key phrase 'we have not observed any living, knowing, powerful being except it is a body' — this is an inductive generalization from created beings.
3
Consider the conclusion
The opponent concludes that God must be a body. Ask yourself: is this conclusion valid? Why might it be flawed?
4
Prepare for the refutation
This argument will be refuted in subsequent lessons. For now, understand its structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : إنا لم نشاهد حيا عالما قادرا ، إلا وهو جسم . وإثبات شيء على خلاف المشاهدة : لا يقبله العقل ، ولا يقر به القلب . فوجب القول بكونه تعالى جسما .
The second sophistical argument: We have not observed any living, knowing, powerful being except it is a body. And establishing something contrary to observation: the intellect does not accept it, nor does the heart affirm it. So it is necessary to say that He Most High is a body.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (shubha) of the anthropomorphists. They argue by empirical induction: every living, knowing, powerful being we have ever observed is a body. Therefore, if God is living, knowing, and powerful, He must also be a body. They then assert that establishing a reality contrary to observed experience is intellectually unacceptable and not affirmed by the heart. Hence, they conclude that God must be a body. Imam Razi will later refute this by showing that God's attributes are not comparable to created attributes.

Key Terms
مشاهدة
حي
عالم
قادر
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must be a body because all living beings we see are bodies.
Correction: The attributes of created beings are not identical to God's attributes; analogy fails for the unique Creator.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the second shubha?
A) God is invisible because He is not a body.
B) All observed living beings are bodies, so God must be a body.
C) The intellect cannot accept anything beyond observation.
D) The heart only affirms what is seen.

Key Takeaway
The anthropomorphist argument relies on an inductive generalization from creatures to the Creator, which fails because God's attributes are unique and incomparable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Third Objection: God Must Be a Body
Statement of the third objection with three premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-premise structure of the objection that God must be a body, and recognize that this is a challenge needing refutation.

Core Idea
The objection tries to prove God is a body through three interconnected premises.

This passage states the third objection raised by those who argue that God must be a body. The objection is presented as a syllogism: from the premise that God knows physical things, to the conclusion that He is a body. The author will later refute this, but first lays out the argument clearly. Understanding its structure is essential before engaging with the author's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow: from God's knowledge of physical things to the necessity of being a body.
The passage uses the phrase 'فهذه مقدمات ثلاثة' (so these are three premises).
2
Identify the three premises
List the three premises: (1) God knows physical things, (2) the knower must have forms of known things in his essence, (3) one with such forms must be a body.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that God is a body. Note that this conclusion follows only if all three premises are accepted.
This is an objection, not a settled truth.
4
Reflect on the nature of this passage
Recognize that this is a statement of an opponent's view. The author's purpose is to present it clearly before refuting it.
Do not mistake the objection for the author's own position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : إن إله العالم يجب أن يكون عالما بهذه الجسمانيات . والعالم بها يجب أن يحصل فى ذاته صورها ، ومن كان كذلك، يجب أن يكون جسما . فهذه مقدمات ثلاثة، متى ظهرت، لزم القول بأنه جسم
The third objection: Surely the God of the world must be knowledgeable of these physical things. And the one knowledgeable of them must have their forms occur in his essence, and whoever is like that must be a body. So these are three premises; whenever they are established, the conclusion necessarily follows that He is a body.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection (shubha) of those who argue that God must be a body (jism). The objection is structured as a syllogism with three premises: 1) God must know physical things, 2) the knower of physical things must have their images in his essence, and 3) whoever has images of physical things in his essence must be a body. The conclusion is that God is a body. The rest of the text will provide proof for each premise and then refute the objection.

Key Terms

مقدمات

Misconception: Some may think this objection is a valid proof from the author's own theology.
Correction: This is an opponent's objection; the author presents it only to refute it later. It is not an affirmation of God being a body.

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four

Key Takeaway
The third objection attempts to prove God is a body through three premises, but it is presented as a challenge to be refuted, not as accepted doctrine.
