Proof of Premise 3: Images in Essence Imply Bodiliness
Proof that knowing physical forms implies bodiliness via the winged square analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that knowing physical forms necessitates having a divisible body, and how this argument uses the example of a winged square.

Core Idea
Knowledge of physical objects requires bodily distinction.

This lesson presents a key objection raised by some theologians: that if God knows physical things, He must be a body. Imam Razi explains the reasoning behind this claim using an analogy of two equal squares. Follow the argument closely to see how it leads to a problematic conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow.
Notice that the passage begins by stating the premise and then provides a proof.
2
Identify the core premise
The passage is proving that anyone who has forms of physical things in his essence must be a body. Locate this statement.
3
Analyze the winged square example
The proof uses the example of a winged square known through two equal squares. Understand why this example is used: it involves two similar images that must be distinguished.
The two squares are identical in essence.
4
Trace the distinction argument
Since the squares are identical in essence, distinction must be by accidents. If the two images were in the same locus, any accident would apply to both, preventing distinction. Therefore they must be in different loci.
Different loci imply that each locus is a divisible body, so the knower must be a body.
5
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that the Creator of the world, who knows physical things, would be a body. Note that this is a dialectical argument that will be refuted later.
Imam Razi does not accept this conclusion; he presents it as an objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثالثة: وهي في بيان أن من يحصل في ذاته صور الجسمانيات، يجب أن يكون جسما. فالدليل عليه: أن من علم مربعا مجنحا بمربعين متساويين، وجب أن يحصل هذا العلم في ذات ذلك العالم. وذلك العالم لا بد وأن يميز بين ذينك المربعين المتطرفين. وذلك الامتياز لما لم يكن في الماهية ولا في لوازمها - لأنهما متماثلان في الماهية - فلابد وأن يكون بالعوارض. ولو كان محلهما واحدا لامتنع امتياز أحدهما عن الآخر بشيء من العوارض. لأن المثلين إذا حصلا في محل واحد، فكل عارض يعرض لأحدهما، فهو بعينه عارض للآخر. وذلك يمنع من حصول الامتياز. ولما بطل هذا، وجب أن يكون صورة أحد المربعين مغاير المحل للمربع الآخر، حتى يكون امتياز أحد المحلين عن الثاني، سببا لامتياز إحدى الصورتين عن الأخرى. وامتياز أحد المحلين عن الثاني، لا يحصل إلا إذا كان ذلك المحل جسما منقسما. فثبت: أن خالق العالم مدرك للجسمانيات، وثبت: أن كل من كان كذلك، فهو جسم. فيلزم أن يكون إله العالم جسما.
As for the third premise — which is in explanation that whoever has forms of physical things occur in his essence must be a body. The proof for it: that whoever knows a winged square by two equal squares, this knowledge must occur in the essence of that knower. And that knower must distinguish between those two equal squares. That distinction, since it is not in the essence nor in its necessary concomitants — because the two are identical in essence — must be by accidents. And if their locus were one, it would be impossible to distinguish one from the other by any accident. Because if two similars occur in one locus, every accident that occurs to one of them is precisely the accident of the other. And that prevents distinction. When this is invalidated, the image of one of the squares must have a locus different from the locus of the other square, so that the distinction of one locus from the second becomes the cause of the distinction of one image from the other. And distinction of one locus from the second occurs only if that locus is a divisible body. So it is established that the Creator of the world is an apperceiver of physical things, and it is established that whoever is like that is a body. Therefore it necessarily follows that the God of the world is a body.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage proves the third premise using an analogy about curved squares (murabba' mujannah). The argument: If someone knows a winged square by means of two equal squares, then the knowledge of these two squares exists in his essence. He must distinguish between the two squares. This distinction cannot be due to their essence or necessary attributes because they are identical in essence; therefore it must be due to accidents (a'rad). If both images were in the same locus (mahall), then accidents applying to one would apply to the other, preventing distinction. Hence the images must be in different loci. For loci to be distinct, each locus must be divisible, meaning it must be a body. Therefore, the knower of physical things must be a body. This completes the objection's syllogism, concluding that God is a body.

Key Terms
مربع مجنح
ماهية
عارض
محل
جسم منقسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key reason the two square images must be in different loci?
A) Because they have different sizes
B) Because they are identical in essence and cannot be distinguished by accidents if in the same locus
C) Because God cannot know two things at once
D) Because squares are physical objects

Key Takeaway
The argument concludes that knowledge of physical forms necessitates a divisible body, implying that if God knows physical things, He would be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before this lesson, when you thought about God's knowledge of the world, did you subconsciously picture it like your own mental images—complete with forms and distinctions? How does this argument expose that as a form of anthropomorphism? Will you commit to purifying your conception of God's knowledge from any trace of physical analogy?
?
The opponent uses a logical proof to argue that God must be a body if He knows physical things. Have you ever felt that making God 'relatable' by imagining Him in human terms is harmless? How does this lesson challenge you to reject even subtle tendencies toward tashbih? What specific thought pattern will you change today?
?
The argument hinges on the need for distinct loci to distinguish two identical images. In your own life, do you often assume that whatever is 'in your mind' must have a physical basis? How does this lesson force you to reconsider the nature of abstract knowledge and its relationship to the Creator?

Answer to the First Objection: On Self-Evidence
Razi refutes the first objection by denying its premise's self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first objection that posits a false dichotomy between two existents is invalid because its premise is not self-evident.

Core Idea
Not all premises are self-evident; some need proof.

The first objection argued that any two existents must be either in each other or separate by direction, implying God must be in a direction. Razi points out that this premise is not self-evident. It requires a separate proof to be established, so the objection cannot stand on its own.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic answer to the first objection and its translation. Notice the key term 'بديهية' (self-evident).
2
Identify the objection's premise
The objection claims two existents are either co-located or separated by direction. Recognize that this is the premise being challenged.
3
Understand the refutation
Razi argues the premise is not self-evident; it requires a separate proof. Therefore the objection collapses unless that proof is provided.
The term 'برهان منفصل' means a separate, independent proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى: إذا بينا أن قولهم: كل موجودين فإما أن يكون أحدهما حالا في الآخر أو مباينا عنه بالجهة: مقدمة غير بديهية، بل مقدمة محتاجة في النفى والإثبات، إلى برهان منفصل. فسقط الكلام. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية: ما بيناه: أنه لا يلزم من عدم النظير للشيء، عدم ذلك الشيء. فسقطت هذه الشبهة.
And the answer to the first objection: Since we have shown that their statement 'Every two existents are either one of them present in the other or separate from it by direction' is a premise that is not self-evident, rather a premise that in negation and affirmation needs a separate proof. So the argument collapses. And the answer to the second objection: What we have shown, that it does not necessarily follow from the absence of a peer for a thing that the thing itself is non-existent. So this objection collapses.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage provides concise refutations of the first two objections (shubha) that likely argued for God's bodiliness. The first objection claimed that any two existents must be either in each other or separate by direction. The author refutes this by stating that this premise is not intuitively evident (badihi) and requires a separate proof. Therefore, the objection fails. The second objection argued that because God has no peer, He must be non-existent? Or that lack of a similar object implies the object's non-existence. The author refutes it by stating that absence of a peer does not entail absence of the thing itself. Both objections are thus dismissed.

Key Terms
بديهية
برهان منفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every statement that sounds logical is self-evident and needs no proof.
Correction: Not all premises are self-evident; many require demonstration. A premise is self-evident only if its truth is immediately grasped without reasoning.

Checkpoint
Why does the first objection's argument collapse according to the author?
A) Because its premise is false
B) Because its premise is not self-evident and needs separate proof
C) Because it contradicts the Quran
D) Because no one accepts it

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because its core premise is not self-evident and requires proof not provided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you accepted a claim simply because it seemed obvious, without demanding proof? How might that have led you to a mistaken belief? Will you commit to examining the premises of arguments you hear today?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly self-evident statement can be false. Is there an assumption about God or creation that you have never questioned because it seemed intuitive? Are you willing to test it against the Quran and sound reason?
?
Razi's refutation collapses the entire objection by challenging one premise. What 'obvious' belief about your own life might be built on an unexamined premise? Will you take time this week to identify one such belief and seek evidence for it?

Answer to the Second Objection: On the Absence of a Peer
Razi refutes the second objection by denying the logical consequence from lack of a peer to non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second objection that God's lack of a peer implies His non-existence is invalid because absence of a peer does not entail non-existence.

Core Idea
Lack of a peer does not imply non-existence.

The second objection argued that because God has no peer or similar being, He must be non-existent. Razi refutes this by stating a logical principle: the absence of a peer for a thing does not necessitate that the thing itself does not exist. This simple yet powerful point defeats the objection.

Read the passage

2

3
Understand the refutation
Razi asserts that the absence of a peer does not logically entail the absence of the thing itself. The objection fails because it makes an unwarranted leap.
This refutation is concise and relies on a basic logical insight.

Source Text

The first objection claimed that any two existents must be either in each other or separate by direction. The author refutes this by stating that this premise is not intuitively evident (badihi) and requires a separate proof. Therefore, the objection fails. The second objection argued that because God has no peer, He must be non-existent? Or that lack of a similar object implies the object's non-existence. The author refutes it by stating that absence of a peer does not entail absence of the thing itself. Both objections are thus dismissed.

Key Terms
نظير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: If something is unique and has no equal, it cannot exist in reality.
Correction: Uniqueness does not contradict existence. Many things are unique (e.g., the number one, the first cause) yet exist. The absence of a peer does not entail non-existence.

The Argument for a Corporeal Creator from Perception
Argument that the Creator must be a body because He perceives corporeal things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the argument that the Creator must be a body due to His perception of corporeal things, and recognize that this is a rejected view in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Perception of distinct objects requires a divisible locus—so the argument concludes a body.

This passage presents a theological argument that the Creator must be a body because He perceives corporeal things. It is a classical proof used by those who held anthropomorphic views, and Imam Razi later refutes it. Understanding its logical steps is crucial for grasping the doctrine of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the claim: 'Corporeal things, it is necessary that [the Knower] be a body.'
This is the opponent's argument, not Imam Razi's view.
2
Identify the initial premise
The proof starts with the example of knowing two equal squares. The knower must distinguish between them. Why can't the distinction be in the essence or its concomitants?
3
Follow the argument about accidents and loci
Since the two squares are identical in essence, distinction must be through accidents. But if the images were in the same locus, the accidents would be identical, preventing distinction. Therefore the images must have different loci.
4
Understand the final conclusion
Different loci require the knower's essence to be divisible, which is a property of bodies. Hence the Creator must be a body. Recognize that this conclusion is rejected in orthodox Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجسمانيات يجب أن يكون جسما. فالدليل عليه: أن من علم مربعا [؟] مجنها بمر بعين متساويين، وجب أن يحصل هذا العلم في ذات ذلك العالم. وذلك العالم لا بد وأن يميز بين ذينك المربعين المتطرفين، وذلك الامتياز لما لم يكن في الماهية ولا في لوازمها - لأنهما متماثلان في الماهية - فلابد وأن يكون بالعوارض. ولو كان محلاهما واحدا لامتنع امتياز أحدهما عن الآخر بشيء من العوارض. لأن المثلين إذا حصلا في محل واحد، فكل عارض يعرض لأحدهما، فهو بعينه عارض للآخر. وذلك يمنع من حصول الامتياز. ولما بطل هذا، وجب أن يكون صورة أحد المربعين مغاير المحمل للمربع الآخر، حتى يكون امتياز أحد المحلين عن الثاني، سببا لامتياز إحدى الصورتين عن الأخرى: وامتياز أحد المحلين عن الثاني، لا يحصل إلا إذا كان ذلك المحل جسما منقسما. فثبت: أن خالق العالم مدرك للجسمانيات، وثبت: أن كل من كان كذلك، فهو جسم. فيلزم أن يكون إله العالم جسما.
Corporeal things, it is necessary that [the Knower] be a body. The proof for it is: that one who knows a square [?] ... two equal sides, then it is necessary that this knowledge occurs in the essence of that knower. And that knower must distinguish between those two equal squares. That distinction, since it is not in the essence nor in its necessary concomitants – because they are similar in essence – then it must be through accidents. And if their loci were one, it would be impossible for one of them to be distinguished from the other by any accident. Because if two similars occur in one locus, then every accident that occurs to one of them is exactly the same accident for the other. And that prevents distinction. Since this is invalid, it is necessary that the image of one of the squares be different in locus from the other square, so that the distinction of one locus from the second becomes a cause for the distinction of one image from the other. And the distinction of one locus from the second does not occur unless that locus is a divisible body. So it is established that the Creator of the world is a perceiver of corporeal things, and it is established that everyone who is like that is a body. Thus it follows that the God of the world is a body.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a proof—likely attributed to opponents of tanzih—that the Creator must be a body because He perceives corporeal things. The argument proceeds as follows: If the Creator knows two distinct but similar objects (like two equal squares), that knowledge must inhere in His essence. He must distinguish between them. Since the objects are identical in essence, the distinction cannot be due to essence or its necessary concomitants; it must be due to accidents. If the two images were in the same locus (i.e., the same part of the knower), then any accident affecting one would affect the other equally, making distinction impossible. Therefore the images must have different loci, meaning the knower's essence must be divisible to accommodate multiple loci. Divisibility is a property of bodies. Hence the knower (the Creator) must be a body. The conclusion is explicit: 'So it is established that the Creator of the world is a perceiver of corporeal things, and it is established that everyone who is like that is a body. Thus it follows that the God of the world is a body.' This is the argument that Imam Razi will refute.

Key Terms
جسم
علم
ماهية
عوارض
محل
امتياز
جسمانيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage presents Imam Razi's own view that the Creator is a body.
Correction: It is actually the opponent's argument, which Imam Razi is about to refute. The passage is setting up the proof that will be rejected.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the proof presented in this passage?
A) The Creator must be a body because He perceives corporeal things.
B) The Creator is not a body.
C) Two squares can be distinguished without a body.
D) Knowledge does not require a body.

Key Takeaway
This argument concludes that the Creator must be a body, but this conclusion is rejected in mainstream Islamic theology, as Imam Razi will demonstrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This argument assumes that God's knowledge is like human knowledge—accidental and dependent on a divisible subject. Are you willing to submit to a God whose knowledge is utterly beyond your own, even if you cannot conceptualize it?
?
If someone today told you that God must be a body because He sees you, how would you respond—not just with your mind, but with the conviction of your heart? What does that reveal about your trust in divine revelation over human reasoning?

Answer to the First Doubt: The Premise is Not Self-Evident
Razi refutes a doubt by showing its premise is not self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes a theological doubt by showing that its core premise is not self-evident and needs separate proof.

Core Idea
A doubt crumbles when its hidden assumption is shown to be unproven.

In this passage, Imam Razi provides a concise answer to an initial doubt. The doubt likely argued that if God is not a body, then He must be either spatially contained or separated by direction—both impossible for God, so He must be a body. Razi counters by attacking the very premise: the idea that every two existents are either inside each other or apart by direction is not self-evident. He asserts that this premise requires its own proof, and without it, the entire argument collapses. Let's examine how this simple but powerful refutation works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise structure: Razi presents the condition (if we show the premise is not self-evident) and its result (the argument falls).
2
Identify the challenged premise
The premise under attack is: 'Every two existents are either one is in the other or separate by direction.' Recognize that this is not an obvious truth—it needs justification.
This premise is often assumed in discussions about God's nature.
3
Understand the force of the refutation
Razi says the premise is 'non-self-evident' and needs a 'separate proof.' This means the doubter cannot rely on it without proof. Since the doubt's logic depends on this premise, removing its self-evident status makes the doubt invalid.
The key move is to demand proof for an unproven assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى: إذا بينا أن قولهم: كل موجودين فإما أن يكون أحدهما حالا في الآخر أو مباينا عنه بالجهة: مقدمة غير بديهية، بل مقدمة محتاجة في النفي والإثبات، إلى برهان منفصل. فسقط الكلام.
And the answer to the first doubt: If we show that their statement: 'Every two existents are either one is in the other or separate by direction' is a non-self-evident premise, but a premise in need, in negation and affirmation, of a separate proof. So the argument falls.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the first doubt. The doubt (not explicitly stated) likely argues that if God is not a body, then He must be either in something or separate by direction—both impossible for God, so He must be a body. The answer refutes this by challenging the premise that 'every two existents are either one in the other or separate by direction.' Imam Razi states that this premise is not self-evident (badihiyya); rather, it requires a separate proof (burhan munfasil) to establish its truth or falsehood. Therefore, the argument based on that premise is invalid, and the doubt falls away.

Correction: Razi shows that this premise is not self-evident; it requires proof. Thus, it cannot be assumed in an argument.

Key Takeaway
A doubt loses its force if its key premise is not self-evident and must be proven separately.

Answer to the Second Doubt: Uniqueness Does Not Imply Non-Existence
Razi refutes a doubt about divine uniqueness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the absence of a similar (nazir) to something does not logically entail the non-existence of that thing, and thus the doubt is invalid.

Core Idea
Uniqueness does not negate existence.

Imam Razi now addresses the second doubt of his opponents, who argued that if Allah is unlike anything, then He would have no similar, and therefore His existence would be impossible. Razi counters this with a simple logical point: the lack of a similar does not imply the lack of the thing itself. Let's examine this concise rebuttal and see how it dismantles the doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the direct and conclusive tone.
2
Identify the logical claim
The passage states a logical relationship: 'the absence of a similar does not imply the absence of the thing.' Identify the terms 'nazir' (similar) and 'adam' (absence/non-existence).
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the doubt is invalid: uniqueness (having no similar) does not disprove existence. Many unique things exist (e.g., Allah, the soul, the intellect).
This corrects a common misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية: ما بيناه: أنه لا يلزم من عدم النظير للشيء، عدم ذلك الشيء. فسقطت هذه الشبهة.
And the answer to the second doubt: What we have shown: that the absence of a similar to a thing does not imply the absence of that thing. So this doubt falls.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage answers the second doubt. The doubt likely argued that if God is not a body, then there would be no similar to Him, so He would not exist or His existence would be impossible. Imam Razi responds: the absence of a similar (nazir) to a thing does not logically entail the absence of that thing itself. In other words, uniqueness does not negate existence. Therefore the doubt is invalid and dismissed.

Misconception: Some argue that if something has no similar, it cannot exist because existence requires comparability or likeness.
Correction: The absence of a similar does not imply non-existence. Many unique entities (e.g., the First Cause) exist without any similar.

Answering the Third Doubt: Images in a Non-Body
Imam Razi refutes a doubt by showing that images of corporeal things can be impressed in a non-bodily substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the doubt that perception of corporeal things requires a bodily perceiver, using the example of imagining large objects in a finite mind.

Core Idea
Images can be received in a non-bodily substance.

This lesson examines Imam Razi's concise refutation of a doubt about perception. The doubt claims that only a body can receive images of physical things. Razi uses a simple example—imagining trees and horses—to show that this claim leads to a contradiction. As you read, pay attention to the logical structure of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the key terms.
2
Understand the doubt being refuted
The third doubt likely argued that perception of corporeal things requires the perceiver to be a body. Razi refutes it.
The doubt is not explicitly stated here, but its nature is inferred from the response.
3
Follow the two possibilities
Razi presents two possibilities for the self: it is either this body or a non-bodily substance. He then shows that the first is impossible.
The impossibility is due to size: large images cannot fit in a small body.
4
See how the second possibility refutes the doubt
If the self is non-bodily, then images are impressed in a non-body, which is exactly what the opponent denied. Thus the doubt collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشبهة الثالثة: ساقطة أيضا. والدليل عليه: أنه يمكننا تخيل صورة الشجر والخيل. وهذه الصورة لو كانت منتقشة في ذاتنا، لكانت ذاتنا، إما أن تكون هي هذا الجسم وإما أن تكون جوهرا مجردا. والأول محال. لأنا نعلم بالضرورة: أن الأشياء العظيمة لا يمكن انطباعها في المحل الصغير، وأما الثاني فإنه اعتراف بأن صور المحسوسات يمكن انطباعها فيما لا يكون جسما وذلك يوجب سقوط هذه الشبهة. وبالله التوفيق.
And the third doubt: is also refuted. And the proof for it: that we can imagine the image of trees and horses. If this image were impressed in our essence, then our essence would either be this body or be a non-bodily substance. The first is impossible, for we know necessarily that large things cannot be impressed in a small locus. As for the second, it is an admission that images of sensible things can be impressed in what is not a body, and that necessitates the refutation of this doubt. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage answers the third doubt. The doubt likely claimed that perception of corporeal things requires the perceiver to be a body; otherwise, how can an image be received? Imam Razi responds with an example: we can imagine the images of large objects like trees and horses. If these images were impressed in our essence (our mind), then our essence must be either a body or a non-bodily substance (jawhar mujarrad). It cannot be a body because we know necessarily that large images cannot fit in a small bodily locus (the brain or heart is small). If our essence is a non-body, then that concedes that images of sensible things can be impressed in a non-bodily substance—which is exactly what the opponent denies. Thus the doubt is self-refuting. Imam Razi concludes with 'And with Allah is success.'

Correction: The argument concerns mental images, not physical ones. The point is that mental reception does not require a physical body.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Razi's Refutation of the Third Doubt on Mental Images
Razi refutes the third doubt by showing images cannot be in a physical body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the doubt that mental images are physical imprints, thereby establishing that the soul is a non-physical substance.

Core Idea
Mental images cannot fit in a physical body, so they must be in a non-physical substance.

Razi addresses a doubt that might arise from our ability to form mental images of large objects. If images were physical imprints, they would need to fit in the body, which is impossible for large images. This forces us to conclude that the soul is non-physical. In this passage, Razi presents a concise proof to dismantle this doubt completely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the concise style of Razi's reasoning.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Razi says if images were imprinted, our essence would be either a body or a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad). Find where he states these.
These are the two logical options.
3
Understand why the body option fails
Razi argues that large images cannot be impressed in a small body. Reflect on why this is a necessary truth.
Size constraint is the key.
4
See how the second option refutes the doubt
If the self is a simple substance, then images can be impressed in non-physical matter. This admission undermines the doubt's premise.
The doubt likely assumed physicality; accepting non-physicality defeats it.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument supports the doctrine of divine transcendence by affirming a non-physical soul capable of receiving forms.
This connects to broader theological themes.

The first option is impossible because a large image cannot fit in a small physical space (the body). Therefore the doubt is invalid.

The First Proof: God Cannot Be in a Place
Razi presents the first proof that God cannot be spatially located using a disjunctive argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof that God cannot be spatially located because spatial occupation entails either divisibility or extreme smallness, both of which are impossible for the Creator.

Core Idea
If God were in a place, He would be divisible—impossible for the Creator.

Imam Razi opens his fourth chapter by establishing that God is not confined to any place or direction. Before presenting multiple proofs, he offers the first argument: a logical disjunction showing that spatial occupation entails either divisibility into parts or extreme smallness—both absurd for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation once without analysis. Let the flow of the proof settle.
Notice the concise style of kalam arguments.
2
Understand the disjunction
The proof begins with a forced choice: if God is in a place, He must be either divisible or indivisible. Identify these two branches.
3
Analyze each branch
If divisible, then composite—already proved impossible. If indivisible, then like an indivisible atom—extremely small, also impossible. Both are unacceptable.
The indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza') was a debated concept.
4
Consider the additional argument
Razi adds: anything perceptible as 'here' or 'there' has distinct sides, implying divisibility. So spatial indication leads to divisibility.
This second argument appeals to those who deny atoms entirely.
5
Conclude the proof
Both arguments converge: spatial location is impossible for God. Reflect on how this reinforces God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في اقامة البراهين على أنه تعالى ليس مختصا بحيز وجهة . بمعنى : أنه يصح أن يشار اليه بالحس بأنه ههنا أو هناك ويدل عليه وجوه : البرهان الأول : وذلك أنه لم يخل إما أن يكون منقسما أو غير منقسم . فإن كان منقسما كان مركبا - وقد تقدم إبطاله - وإن لم يكن منقسما كان في الصغر والحقارة ، كالجزء الذى لا يتجزأ . وذلك باطل باتفاق العقلاء . وأيضا : فلان من ينفى الجوهر الفرد يقول : إن كل ما كان مشارا إليه بحسب الحس بأنه ههنا أو هناك . فإنه لا بد وأن يتميز أحد جانبيه عن الآخر . وذلك يوجب كونه منقسما . فثبت : أن القول بأنه مشار
Chapter Four: Establishing proofs that He, exalted, is not confined to a specific place or direction, meaning that it is correct to point to Him by sense as being here or there. And there are aspects that indicate this. The first proof: He, exalted, is not free from either being divisible or not divisible. If He is divisible, then He is composite, and its invalidity has preceded. If He is not divisible, then He would be in smallness and insignificance, like the indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'), and that is invalid by consensus of all rational people. Also, those who deny the atom (al-jawhar al-fard) say that everything pointed to by sense as being here or there must have one of its sides distinguished from the other, and that necessitates it being divisible. So it is established that the statement that He is pointed to (by sense) is (invalid).
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter Four, which aims to prove that God is not confined to a specific place or direction (hayyiz wa jiha). The author presents the first proof (burhan). The argument proceeds by disjunction: God must be either divisible (munqasim) or not divisible. If divisible, He would be composite (murakkab), which has already been refuted as impossible for God. If not divisible, He would be like the indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'), i.e., extremely small and insignificant, which is absurd for God and rejected by all rational people. Additionally, the author invokes an argument from those who deny the atom altogether (e.g., philosophers): they hold that anything perceptible as being 'here' or 'there' must have distinct sides, which implies divisibility. Thus, applying this to God, if He were pointed to in a place, He would be divisible, but divisibility is impossible. Therefore, the claim that God can be spatially indicated is false.

Key Terms
حيز
جهة
منقسم
جزء لا يتجزأ
جوهر فرد
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God can be in a place like a spirit or light without being divisible.
Correction: Razi's proof shows that any spatial location, even for non-physical entities (if they are in place), requires divisibility or atomic smallness, both impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is the consequence if God were indivisible?
A) He would be composite.
B) He would be extremely small and insignificant like an atom.
C) He would be impossible to perceive.
D) He would be in all places at once.

Key Takeaway
The first proof demonstrates that spatial location necessarily entails divisibility or atomic insignificance, both incompatible with the divine nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever unconsciously limit Allah by associating His presence only with certain places, like the mosque or your prayer rug? How does this proof challenge you to seek His closeness everywhere, especially in areas you avoid?
?
If Allah cannot be confined to any location, then He is equally present to you in obedience and in sin. How does this truth confront your attempts to hide wrong actions in private? What will you do today to live with this awareness?
?
This argument shows that thinking of Allah as 'distant' or 'near' in a physical sense diminishes His perfection. Have you ever felt Allah is far when your prayers seem unanswered? How can you repent from such a spatial conception and truly believe He is beyond all places, yet aware of you every moment?

5 min
First proof against spatial location for Allah
Razi presents the first proof from division against Allah being in a place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof that Allah cannot be in a place, based on the impossibility of division and composition.

Core Idea
Allah is neither divisible nor indivisible like an atom.

This passage presents the first of several proofs that Allah transcends spatial location. Razi uses a simple dichotomy: either Allah is divisible or not. Both options lead to absurdities. Let's follow his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a dichotomy (divisible/indivisible) and two supporting arguments.
Pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the dichotomy
The proof starts with 'it is not free from either being divisible or indivisible'. See how each branch leads to a false conclusion.
3
Understand the second line of argument
Razi adds an argument even for those who deny atoms: anything pointed to has sides, hence divisible.
This shows the proof does not depend on accepting atoms.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Both branches lead to falsehood, so the original claim (Allah in a place) is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الأول (۲) ) : وذلك أنه لم يخل إما أن يكون منقسما أو غير منقسم . فإن كان منقسما كان مركبا - وقد تقدم إبطاله - وإن لم يكن منقسما كان في الصغر والحقارة ، كالجزء الذى لا يتجزأ . وذلك باطل باتفاق (كل (۳)) العقلاء . وأيضا : فلان من ينفى الجوهر الفرد يقول : إن كل ما كان مشارا إليه بحسب الحس بأنه ههنا أو هناك . فإنه لا بد وأن يتميز أحد جانبيه عن الآخر . وذلك يوجب كونه منقسمها (٤) . فثبت : أن القول بأنه مشار إليه بحسب الحس ، يفضي إلى هذين القسمين الباطلين . فوجب أن يكون القول به باطلا .
The first proof: That is, it is not free from either being divisible or indivisible. If it is divisible, then it is composite—and its refutation has already preceded—and if it is not divisible, then it would be in smallness and insignificance, like the indivisible atom. And that is false by agreement of all rational people. And also: Because the one who denies the indivisible atom says: Everything that is pointed to by sense as being here or there, it is necessary that one of its sides be distinguished from the other, and that necessitates its being divisible. So it is established that the statement that He is pointed to by sense leads to these two false divisions. Therefore, the statement must be false.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that Allah is not confined to place or direction. The argument proceeds by a dichotomy: either Allah is divisible or indivisible. If divisible, He would be composite, which has already been refuted (presumably earlier in the work). If indivisible, He would be like the smallest atom, which is insignificant—something impossible for Allah, and all rational people agree this is false. The proof then adds a second line: those who deny the existence of the indivisible atom still hold that anything pointed to by sense must have two sides, thus being divisible. Therefore, claiming that Allah can be pointed to leads to either of two false alternatives, so the claim itself is false.

مركب
جوهر فرد
مشار إليه بحسب الحس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof depends on accepting the indivisible atom.
Correction: Razi includes a second argument that works even if one denies atoms, because any sensible object has sides and is divisible.

Checkpoint

A) That Allah is either eternal or temporal
B) That Allah is either divisible (hence composite) or indivisible (hence like an atom)
C) That Allah is either in the heavens or on earth
D) That Allah is either one or many

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof shows that claiming Allah is in a place leads to either composition or atom-like insignificance, both impossible.

Objection: Can Allah Be One and Great?
Razi presents an objection and begins his answer with a decisive proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that Allah could be great yet indivisible, and Razi's initial response using a decisive proof based on an indivisible point.

Core Idea
Greatness implies divisibility – not by analogy, but by a decisive logical proof.

Razi has argued that Allah must be one and indivisible because greatness implies composition. Here an objector challenges that: why can't Allah be both one and great? Razi's response distinguishes between analogical reasoning and a decisive proof based on the nature of points.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and responses
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: objection, then two initial counterarguments, then the start of the actual answer.
The answer is incomplete; focus on the method Razi uses.
2
Understand the two counterarguments
Razi first concedes that in the witnessed world greatness implies divisibility, but questions the analogy. Then he raises a further objection: why can't Allah be extremely small?
These are not Razi's final answer; they are dialectical moves.
3
Grasp the start of the decisive proof
Razi says the proof is not analogical but based on a decisive argument involving an indivisible point. The text cuts off, but the direction is clear.
This proof is foundational for kalam arguments about composition.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إه تعالى واحد منزه عن التأليف والتركيب ، ومع كونه كذلك ، فإنه يكون عظيما ؟ قوله : « العظيم يجب أن يكون مركبا منقسما، وذلك ينا في كونه أحدا ، قلنا : سلمنا أن يجب أن يكون منقسما في الشاهد . فلم قلتم : إنه يجب أن يكون في الغائب كذلك ؟ فإن قياس الغائب على الشاهد من غير جامع : باطل . وأيضاً : فلم لا يجوز أن يكون غير منقسم ، ويكون في غاية الصغر ؟ قوله : « إنه حقير وذلك على الله تعالى محال ، قلنا : الذي لا يمكن أن يشار إليه البتة ولا يمكن أن يحس به ، يكون كالعدم فيكون أشد حقارة . وإذا جاز هذا ، فلم لا يجوز ذلك ؟ والجواب على الأول : أن نقول: إنه إذا كان عظيما فلابد وأن يكون منقسما . وليس هذا من باب قياس الغائب على الشاهد ، بل هذا بناء على البرهان القطعى (٥) وذلك لأنا إذا أشرنا إلى نقطة لا تنقسم . فإما أن يحصل فوقها شيء آخر ، أو لا يحصل . فإن حصل فوقها شيء آخر ، كان ...
For it is said: Why is it not permissible to say: He, exalted, is one, transcendent over composition and combination, and despite that, He is great? His statement: 'The great must be composite and divisible, and that conflicts with His being one,' we say: We grant that He must be divisible in the witnessed world. But why do you say that He must be so in the absent (divine) realm? For analogizing the absent to the witnessed without a common point is invalid. Also: Why is it not permissible that He be indivisible and be extremely small? His statement: 'That would be insignificant, and that is impossible for God,' we say: That which cannot be pointed to at all and cannot be perceived by sense is like non-existence and is therefore more insignificant. If this is permissible, then why not that? The answer to the first: We say: If He is great, He must be divisible. And this is not a matter of analogizing the absent to the witnessed; rather, it is based on a decisive proof. That is because if we point to an indivisible point, either there is something above it or not. If there is something above it, then it would be...
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous proof and the beginning of Razi's response. The objector says: why can't Allah be one, non-composite, yet great? The objector quotes the view 'the great must be composite and divisible, which contradicts His oneness'. Razi's initial response is to concede that in the witnessed world (shahid) greatness implies divisibility, but questions why this must hold for the unseen (gha'ib) without a common ground. He also raises a counter: why can't Allah be indivisible and extremely small? If one says that would be insignificant, Razi retorts that something utterly imperceptible is even more insignificant. Then Razi gives the actual answer to the first objection: greatness does necessitate divisibility, but not by analogical reasoning; rather, by a decisive proof (burhan qati') involving pointing to an indivisible point. If you point to a point, either there is something above it or not. If there is, then the point is not the limit, leading to divisibility. The text breaks off here, so the full argument is incomplete.

برهان قطعي

Correction: Razi clarifies that the decisive proof is not analogical at all; it is a logical argument about indivisible points, independent of empirical analogy.

Refuting the Objection of Divine Greatness without Composition
Razi refutes the objection that God can be great without composition by challenging analogical reasoning and the smallness alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why analogical reasoning from the physical world is insufficient to prove that God must be composite, and how Razi counters the alternative claim of extreme smallness.

Core Idea
Greatness in the physical world implies divisibility, but God is not analogous to physical objects.

In this passage, Razi addresses an objection: why can't God be both one and immensely great without being composed of parts? He first shows that the argument from physical greatness relies on a flawed analogy (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid). Then he tackles a second objection that if God is indivisible, He would be extremely small, which is also problematic. Razi's responses clear the ground for a more rigorous proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the passage without analysing; notice the two objections raised by the opponent and Razi's answers.
2
Identify the structure
The passage has two parts: first response about analogy, second response about smallness. See how Razi counters each.
3
Consider the role of analogy
Why does Razi reject the analogy from the physical world? What is 'qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid'?
This is a key concept in Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the smallness objection
Why does Razi say imperceptibility is more contemptible? How does that refute the objection?
Note the logical move: turning the tables.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: الله تعالى واحد منزه عن التأليف والتركيب، ومع كونه كذلك، فإنه يكون عظيما؟ قوله: «العظيم يجب أن يكون مركبا منقسما، وذلك ينافي كونه أحدا، قلنا: سلمنا أن يجب أن يكون منقسما في الشاهد. فلم قلتم: إنه يجب أن يكون في الغائب كذلك؟ فإن قياس الغائب على الشاهد من غير جامع: باطل. وأيضاً: فلم لا يجوز أن يكون غير منقسم، ويكون في غاية الصغر؟ قوله: «إنه حقير وذلك على الله تعالى محال، قلنا: الذي لا يمكن أن يشار إليه البتة ولا يمكن أن يحس به، يكون كالعدم فيكون أشد حقارة. وإذا جاز هذا، فلم لا يجوز ذلك؟ والجواب على الأول: أن نقول: إنه إذا كان عظيما فلابد وأن يكون منقسما. وليس هذا من باب قياس الغائب على الشاهد، بل هذا بناء على البرهان القطعي (٥) وذلك لأنا إذا أشرنا إلى نقطة لا تنقسم. فإما أن يحصل فوقها شيء آخر، أو لا يحصل. فإن حصل فوقها شيء آخر، كان ذلك الفوقاني مغايرا له. إذ لو جاز أن يقال: إن هذا المشار إليه عينه لاغيره، جاز أن يقال: هذا الجزء عين (٦) ذلك الجزء. فيفضي إلى تجويز أن الجبل شيء واحد، وجزء لا يتجزأ، مع كونه جبلا. وذلك شكل (٧) في البديهات. فثبت: أنه لا بد من التزام التركيب والانقسام. وإما أن لا تحصل فوقها شيء آخر ولا على يمينها ولا على يسارها ولا من تحتها، فحينئذ تكون نقطة غير منقسمة وجزءاً لا يتجزأ. وذلك باتفاق العقلاء باطل. فثبت: أن هذا ليس من باب قياس الشاهد على الغائب، بل هو مبنى على التقسيم الدائر بين النفي والإثبات.
If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say: Allah is one, free from composition and combination, and despite that, He is great? His statement: 'The great must be composed and divisible, and that contradicts His being one.' We say: We grant that it must be divisible in the witnessed. But why do you say it must be likewise in the absent? For analogy of the absent to the witnessed without a common ground is invalid. Also: Why is it not permissible that He be indivisible and be extremely small? His statement: 'That is contemptible, and that is impossible for Allah.' We say: That which cannot be pointed to at all nor perceived by sense is like non-existence, thus more contemptible. If this is permissible, why is that not permissible? The answer to the first is that we say: If it is great, it must be divisible. This is not from the category of analogy of the absent to the witnessed; rather it is based on demonstrative proof. That is because if we point to an indivisible point, then either there is something above it or not. If there is something above it, that above is other than it. For if it were permissible to say that this pointed-to is identical to that, it would be permissible to say: this part is identical to that part, leading to the possibility that a mountain is one thing and an indivisible part, while being a mountain. That is a doubt in self-evident truths. So it is established that composition and division must be affirmed. And if nothing is above it, nor to its right, nor to its left, nor below it, then it becomes an indivisible point and an atom. That is unanimously impossible. So it is established that this is not from analogy of the witnessed to the absent, but is based on the division between affirmation and negation.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector suggests that God can be both one and great without composition. Razi first refutes the analogy from the physical world, showing that 'greatness' in the physical sense implies divisibility. He then addresses the second objection about smallness, arguing that imperceptibility implies greater contemptibility. The core of the answer is a demonstrative proof: any point either has something above it (implying composition) or not (implying atomism, which is impossible). Thus the objector's claim is not mere analogy but a rational division. Razi follows the objector's sequence, first dealing with the greatness argument, then the smallness objection, then the main proof.

Key Terms
العظيم
التركيب
الانقسام
قياس الغائب على الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessity of divisibility for greatness in the physical world necessarily applies to God.
Correction: Razi points out that this is an invalid analogy from the witnessed to the absent without a common ground.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say about the claim that 'the great must be composed'?
A) It is always true.
B) It is true only in the physical world but not necessarily for God.
C) It is false.
D) It proves God is composite.

Key Takeaway
The analogy from the physical world is insufficient to prove that God must be composite; a more rigorous rational proof is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi notes that imperceptibility is like non-existence, yet Muslims affirm God exists without being seen. How does this tension affect your certainty in God's existence? Are you willing to embrace that God is beyond your senses, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
If the objection that God would be contemptible if indivisible crossed your mind, do you realize it was based on a false analogy? This passage calls you to purge your mind of such analogies. What habits of thought about God do you need to actively reject starting today?

The Indivisible Point Argument: A Demonstrative Proof for Divine Transcendence
Razi presents a demonstrative proof using the indivisible point to show that greatness necessarily implies composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's demonstrative proof that any greatness necessitates composition, based on the logical division of an indivisible point.

Core Idea
An indivisible point either has something above it (composition) or not (impossible atomism), so true greatness implies division.

Razi now presents the core of his refutation: a rational argument that does not rely on analogy. He uses the concept of an indivisible point (جزء لا يتجزأ) and shows that if such a point exists, it must either have something above it—implying composition—or be isolated, which leads to atomism, considered impossible by all rational thinkers. Thus, any extended object must be composite.

Guided Path
1
Read the demonstrative proof
Read Razi's proof once to get the overall structure.
2
Identify the logical division
The proof is based on a dichotomy: either something above the point or not. Write down the two branches.
This is called 'taqseem' in Islamic logic.
3
Understand the first branch
If something is above, composition follows. Why does Razi reject the possibility of identity?
He shows it leads to absurdity.
4
Understand the second branch
If nothing around, what happens? Why is atomism impossible?
He says it's unanimously rejected by rational people.
5
Conclude
Razi concludes that greatness implies divisibility, and this is a rational proof, not analogy.
This is the key takeaway.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say: Allah is one, free from composition and combination, and despite that, He is great? Razi's Critique of the Karramiyya: Contradiction in Divine Attributes
Razi compares Hanabila and Karramiyya, criticizing the latter's contradictory view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi considers the Karramiyya position contradictory and how he refutes their objection that a non-spatial God is contemptible.

Core Idea
Claiming God is both spatial and indivisible contradicts rational intuition.

In this passage, Razi engages in a comparative critique of two anthropomorphic groups. While both the Hanabila and Karramiyya compromise divine transcendence, Razi argues that the Karramiyya's position is especially incoherent because it combines contradictory claims about God's spatiality and indivisibility. This lesson unpacks Razi's argument and his response to their counter-objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Let Razi's own words frame the argument.
2
Identify the two groups and their positions
Note what each group (Hanabila and Karramiyya) affirms about God. Razi says the Hanabila admit composition, while the Karramiyya claim spatial pointedness, infinity, and indivisibility.
This sets up the comparison.
3
Locate the contradiction in the Karramiyya's claims
Find the three claims Razi lists for the Karramiyya and ask how they can all be true. The key is that something infinite and indivisible cannot also be spatially pointed.
Razi says this is 'contrary to the intellect's intuition'.
4
Analyse Razi's response to the 'contemptibility' objection
Read the last part where Razi answers the Karramiyya's objection that a non-spatial God is more contemptible than an atom. Understand why he says the charge only applies to spatial beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أن الحنابلة القائلين بالتركيب والتأليف، أسعد حالا من هؤلاء الكرامية، وذلك لأنهم اعترفوا بكونه مركبا من الأجزاء والأبعاض. أما هؤلاء الكرامية فإنهم زعموا أنه مشار إليه بحسب الحس، وزعموا: أنه غير متناه، ثم زعموا مع ذلك: أنه واحد، لا يقبل القسمة. فلاجرم صار قولهم قولا على خلاف بديهة العقل. أما قولهم: «الذي لا يحس به ولا يشار إليه: أشد حقارة من الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، قلنا: كونه موصوفا بالحقارة، إنما يلزم لو كان ذا حيز ومقدار، حتى يقال: إنه أصغر من غيره. أما إذا كان منزها عن الحيز والمقدار ولم يحصل بينه وبين غيره مناسبة في الحيز والمقدار. فلم يلزم وصفه بالحقارة؟
Know that the Hanabila who affirm composition and combination are better off than these Karramiyya, because they admit that He is composed of parts and limbs. As for these Karramiyya, they claim that He is sensorily pointed-to, and they claim that He is infinite, and they also claim that He is one, indivisible. Therefore their statement becomes a statement contrary to the intellect's intuition. As for their statement: 'That which is not sensed nor pointed to is more contemptible than the indivisible part,' we say: His being described with contemptibility would only follow if He had location and magnitude, so that it is said He is smaller than others. But if He is transcendent of location and magnitude, and there is no similarity between Him and others in location and magnitude, then why should the description of contemptibility follow?
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a comparative critique. Razi first notes that the Hanabila, who explicitly affirm that God is composed of parts, are less problematic than the Karramiyya, who claim contradictory attributes: that God is spatially pointed-to, infinite, yet indivisible. This combination violates basic rational intuition. Then he addresses the Karramiyya's counter-argument that a non-spatial, imperceptible being would be more contemptible than an atom. Razi responds that contemptibility only applies to beings with location and magnitude; since God is transcendent of both, the charge does not apply.

Correction: Razi only says the Hanabila are 'better off' because their position is less contradictory; he does not accept their view. He is using comparative criticism to highlight the greater incoherence of the Karramiyya.

Razi shows that the Karramiyya's simultaneous affirmation of God's spatial pointedness, infinity, and indivisibility is a logical contradiction, and that the charge of contemptibility only applies to spatial beings.

Second Proof: God Cannot Be in a Place or Direction
Razi presents a concise proof that if God were in a place, He would be dependent on it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God’s being in a place or direction is impossible through a simple deductive argument.

Core Idea
Place implies dependence; God is independent, so He cannot be in a place.

Razi now offers a very tight proof. He argues that if God were confined to a location or direction, He would need that location to exist — but the Necessary Existent cannot need anything. This short argument is a cornerstone of Islamic theology affirming God’s transcendence over physical categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the logical structure: if-then, then a conclusion.
The passage ends with 'بيان الملازمة' (explanation of the implication) which is not included here.
2
Identify the conditional and conclusion
The proof has a conditional (if God is in a place, He is dependent) and a conclusion (so He cannot be in a place). See how the impossibility of the consequent leads to the impossibility of the antecedent.
This is a simple modus tollens argument.
3
Reflect on the key term 'need'
Razi uses 'محتاج' (in need). Why is need incompatible with God? Because God is the Necessary Existent — He cannot depend on anything else for His existence.
This ties back to the concept of wujūb al-wujūd.
4
Consider the common misunderstanding
Some think 'place' is neutral, but Razi shows it entails neediness. Think about why even being in 'all places' would still imply dependence.
The doctrine of transcendence (tanzīh) is at stake.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second proof in demonstrating that it is impossible for Him to be characterized by location and direction: If He were characterized by location and direction, He would be in need in His existence of that location and that direction. This is impossible, so His being in location and direction is impossible. Explanation of the implication:

Commentary
This passage is a short deductive proof. It states that if God were in a place or direction, He would be dependent on that place for His existence, which is impossible for the Necessary Existent. Therefore, God cannot be in a place or direction. The phrase 'بيان الملازمة' indicates that a detailed explanation of the logical connection follows, but it is not present in the available pages.

Misconception: Being in a place is a neutral attribute that does not imply imperfection.

God cannot be in a place because that would imply dependence, contradicting His absolute independence.

Refuting Divine Composition and Spatial Pointability
Razi refutes the Karramiyya on divine composition and spatial pointability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the Karramiyya claim that God is spatially pointed to yet indivisible, by exposing its logical contradictions and defending divine transcendence from the objection of contemptibility.

Core Idea
Claiming God is both spatially pointable and indivisible is a logical contradiction.

In this passage, Imam Razi takes on the Karramiyya sect, who held an incoherent view of God: they said He is spatially pointable (musharun ilayh) by the senses, infinite, yet indivisible. Razi shows that this position leads to absurdities and is actually worse than the openly anthropomorphist Hanbalites. He then rebuts their objection that a non-spatial God would be more contemptible than an atom, affirming Allah's transcendence above space and magnitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the flow from the identity-of-parts dilemma to the conclusion about the Karramiyya's inconsistency.
The passage uses a reductio ad absurdum argument.
2
Identify the core contradiction
Focus on the first part where Razi argues: if two pointed-to parts are identical, absurdities follow (e.g., a mountain becomes an indivisible atom). This forces the opponent to either admit composition or accept an indivisible point, both unreasonable.
The key is that spatial pointability entails divisibility.
3
Compare with the Hanbalites
Razi points out that the Hanbalites, though wrong, are at least consistent because they openly affirm composition. The Karramiyya, however, claim both pointability and indivisibility, which is a direct contradiction.
Consistency is better than self-contradiction.
4
Examine the rebuttal of contemptibility
The Karramiyya object that a non-spatial God is more contemptible than an indivisible atom. Razi answers that contemptibility requires spatial relations, which God transcends. Thus, the objection fails.
This shows the importance of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك الفوقانى مغايرا له. إذ لو جاز أن يقال : إن هذا المشار إليه عينه لاغيره ، جاز أن يقال : هذا الجزء عين ذلك الجزء . فيفضي إلى تجويز أن الجيل شيء واحد، وجزء لا يتجزأ ، مع كونه جبلا . وذلك شك في البديهات . فثبت : أنه لا بد من التزام التركيب والانقسام . وإما أن لا تحصل فوقها شيء آخر ولا على يمينها ولا على يسارها ولا من تحتها ، فحينئذ يكون نقطة غير منقسمة وجزءاً لا يتجزأ . وذلك باتفاق العقلاء باطل . فثبت : أن هذا ليس من باب قياس الشاهد على الغائب ، بل هو مبنى على التقسيم الدائر بين النفى والإثبات . واعلم : أن الحنابلة القائلين بالتركيب والتأليف، أسعد حالا من هؤلاء الكرامية ، وذلك لأنهم اعترفوا بكونه مركبا من الأجزاء والأبعاض . أما هؤلاء الكرامية فإنهم زعموا أنه مشار إليه بحسب الحس ، وزعموا : أنه غير متناه ، ثم زعموا مع ذلك : أنه واحد ، لا يقبل القسمة. فلاجرم صار قولهم قولا على خلاف بديهة العقل . أما قولهم : « الذى لا يحس به ولا يشار إليه : أشد حقارة من الجزء الذى لا يتجزأ، قلنا : كونه موصوفا بالحقارة، إنما يلزم لو كان ذا حيز ومقدار ، حتى يقال : إنه أصغر من غيره . أما إذا كان منزهاً عن الحيز والمقدار ولم يحصل بينه وبين غيره مناسبة في الحيز والمقدار . فلم يلزم وصفه بالحقارة ؟
That upper [part] is other than it. For if it were permissible to say that this pointed-to [thing] is identical to it, not other, then it would be permissible to say that this part is identical to that part. That leads to allowing that the [?] is one thing, and an indivisible part, while being a mountain. That is doubt in self-evident truths. So it is established: it is necessary to affirm composition and division. Or [if] nothing else is above it, nor to its right, nor to its left, nor below it, then it would be an indivisible point and an indivisible part. That is, by agreement of the rational people, false. So it is established: this is not from the type of analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the absent, but rather it is based on the division that runs between negation and affirmation. And know: the Hanbalites who affirm composition and structure are in a better state than these Karramiyya, because they acknowledged that He is composed of parts and limbs. As for these Karramiyya, they claimed that He is pointed to by sense, and claimed that He is infinite, then claimed along with that that He is one, not admitting division. Hence their statement became a statement contrary to the primary intuition of reason. As for their statement: 'That which is not sensed nor pointed to is more contemptible than the indivisible part,' we say: His being described as contemptible only follows if He had space and magnitude, such that it could be said He is smaller than others. But if He is transcendent of space and magnitude and there is no relation between Him and others in space and magnitude, then why would the description of contemptibility follow?
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Karramiyya sect's position on divine corporeality. Razi begins by arguing that if one claims two things (like an upper and lower part) are identical, absurdities follow — like a mountain being an indivisible point. This forces the opponent to either admit composition (which is against divine transcendence) or fall into contradiction. He then compares the Hanbalites, who openly affirm composition (and thus are at least consistent), to the Karramiyya, who absurdly claim that God is spatially pointable by sense (musharun ilayh hissan) yet simultaneously indivisible and infinite — a contradiction. Finally, Razi rebuts the Karramiyya's objection that a non-spatial being is more contemptible than an indivisible atom: he argues that contemptibility implies relation in space and magnitude, but God is transcendent above both, so the objection does not apply.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can be spatially pointed to by the senses while being indivisible and infinite.
Correction: Razi proves that spatial pointability necessarily implies composition and division, which contradicts indivisibility and infinity.

Checkpoint

A) They admit God is composed of parts, which is at least consistent.
B) They deny spatial direction for God.
C) They agree with Razi on divine transcendence.
D) They reject any composition in God.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the Karramiyya by showing that spatial pointability entails composition, and that a non-spatial God is not contemptible because transcendence removes the basis for comparison.

How does Razi's argument free you from that feeling, and what practical step can you take to strengthen your conviction of His absolute transcendence?

Second Proof: God Cannot Be in Space or Direction
Razi presents three arguments proving that space and direction are existent entities, making God's dependence on them impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's three arguments proving that space and direction are real existents, and why this leads to the conclusion that God cannot depend on them.

Core Idea
Space and direction are real; God, being self-sufficient, cannot depend on them.

Razi now presents a rigorous rational proof. He begins by establishing that space and direction are not mere concepts but actual entities. Once this is proven, the impossibility of God occupying space becomes clear, because dependence on anything contradicts His necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice that Razi states the main claim, then explains the entailment by proving space and direction are real.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the main argument structure
The argument has two parts: (1) If God were in space, He would depend on space. (2) Space/direction are real existents, so dependence would be on something real, which is impossible for God. Focus on the second part: the three sub-proofs.
3
Analyze the first sub-proof
The first proof argues that upper and lower spaces differ in essence because they cannot be exchanged (God must be above, not below). This difference implies they are real, not pure non-existence.
Reflect on why the distinction must entail existence.
4
Analyze the second sub-proof
The second proof uses sensory indication: we can point up and down distinctly. Pure non-existence cannot be distinguished by indication, so directions must be real entities.
Consider the role of sensory experience in rational proof.
5
Analyze the third sub-proof and conclusion
The third proof notes that when a substance moves, the space it leaves and the space it enters are distinct. Change implies distinct real spaces. Then conclude: space is independent, but that which occupies space is dependent. Hence, God cannot occupy space.
End with the logical conclusion: God is independent, so cannot be in space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثاني في بيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون مختصا بالحيز والجهة : إنه لو كان مختصاً بالحيز والجهة ، لكان محتاجا في وجوده إلى ذلك الحيز وتلك الجهة . وهذا محال ، فكونه في الحيز والجهة محالى . بيان الملازمة : أن الحيز والجهة أمر موجود . والدليل عليه من وجوه : الأول : هو أن الأحياز الفوقانية مخالفة في الحقيقة والماهية ، للأحياز التحتانية ، بدليل : أنهم قالوا يجب أن يكون الله تعالى مختصاً بجهة فوق ، ويمتنع حصوله فى سائر الجهات والأحياز – أعنى التحت واليمين واليسار - لولا كونها مختلفة فى الحقائق و الماهيات ، لامتنع القول بأنه يجب حصوله تعالى في جهة فوق ، ويمتنع حصوله في سائر الجهات. وإذا ثبت أن هذه الأحياز مختلفة في الماهية ، وجب كونها أمورا موجودة . لأن العدم المحض ، يمتنع كونه كذلك . الثاني : هو أن الجهات مختلفة بحسب الإشارات . فإن جهة الفوق متميزة عن جهة التحت في الإشارة . والعدم المحض والنفى الصرف، يمتنع تمييز بعضه عن بعض في الإشارة الحسية . الثالث : إن الجوهر إذا انتقل من حيز إلى حيز ، فالمتروك مغاير لا محالة للمطلوب ، والمنتقل عنه مغاير للمنتقل إليه . فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن الحيز والجهة أمر موجود . ثم إن المسمى بالحيز والجهة ، أمر مستغن في وجوده عما يتمكن ويستقر فيه . وأما الذي يكون مختصاً بالحيز والجهة ، فإنه يكون مفتقرا إلى الحيز والجهة.. فإن الشيء الذي يمكن حصوله فى الحيز ، مستحيل عقلا حصوله لا مختصا بالجهة .. فثبت : أنه تعالى لو كان مختصا بالحيز والجهة ، لكان مفتقرا في وجوده إلى الغير.
The second proof in demonstrating that it is impossible for Him to be particularized by space and direction: If He were particularized by space and direction, He would be in need in His existence to that space and that direction. That is impossible, so His being in space and direction is impossible. Explanation of the entailment: Space and direction are an existing thing. The proof of that is from several aspects: The first: Upper spaces are different in reality and essence from lower spaces; the evidence is that they said God must be particularized by the direction of above, and it is impossible for Him to exist in the rest of the directions and spaces – that is, below, right, and left – were it not that they are different in realities and essences, then it would be impossible to say that He must exist in the direction of above and cannot exist in the rest of the directions. And if it is established that these spaces are different in essence, then they must be existing things. Because pure non-existence cannot be such. The second: Directions are different according to indications. For the direction of above is distinguished from the direction of below in indication. And pure non-existence and sheer negation cannot have some parts distinguished from others by sensory indication. The third: If a substance moves from one space to another, then the abandoned is necessarily other than the sought, and that from which one moves is other than that to which one moves. So by these three aspects it is established that space and direction are an existing thing. Then what is called space and direction is independent in its existence from what settles and rests in it. As for that which is particularized by space and direction, it is dependent upon space and direction. For that which can occur in space is rationally impossible to occur not particularized by direction. So it is established: if God were particularized by space and direction, He would be dependent in His existence upon another.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) that God cannot be in space or direction. Razi states the main argument: if God were in space, He would depend on space; that is impossible for the Necessary Being. To prove the entailment (that space exists and is independent), he gives three sub-proofs: (1) Upper and lower spaces differ in essence, because opponents claim God must be above and cannot be below; this difference implies they are real entities, not mere nothing. (2) Directions are distinguished by sensory indication (e.g., pointing up vs. down); pure non-existence cannot be so distinguished. (3) When a substance moves, the space left and the space entered are distinct; change implies real distinct spaces. Therefore, space and direction are existent, independent things. Then Razi argues: that which occupies space depends on space, but God cannot depend on anything. So God cannot be in space. Thus divine transcendence is established from the impossibility of dependence.

Key Terms
حيز
جهة
برهان
إشارة
عدم محض
جوهر
مفتقر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the difference between directions is merely conventional, not real.
Correction: Razi insists that the distinctions (like above vs. below) entail real existence because pure non-existence cannot have such real differences.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's second proof, why does the distinction between directions (above, below) prove that space and direction are existing things?
A) Because the Quran mentions them.
B) Because they can be distinguished by sensory indication, which is impossible for pure non-existence.
C) Because people commonly believe in them.
D) Because the body moves between them.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that divine transcendence requires denying any spatial or directional particularity, as that would imply dependence on an external existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you catch yourself imagining God as being 'up there' in a certain direction? What does this lesson reveal about that habit, and how will you train your mind to think of Him without spatial limitations?
?
If God does not occupy any space, then where is He relative to you right now? How does this truth affect your awareness of His closeness (ma'iyya) in this very moment?
?
The lesson shows that space and direction are created entities. If God is not contained by them, then what does it mean for your personal 'search for God'? Are you trying to find Him in a specific place or direction? How can you let go of that and seek Him directly without spatial intermediary?
