The Seventh Proof: Directionality Implies Composition and Change
Razi presents the seventh proof against spatial direction for God, refuting it via composition absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's reductio argument showing that attributing a spatial direction to God leads to the impossible consequence of composition and change in the divine essence.

Core Idea
Direction implies parts, and parts imply change — impossible for God.

Razi's seventh proof tackles the assumption that God could be located in a direction. This argument uses pure logical analysis to show that directionality necessarily entails composition and potential change, both of which are incompatible with the nature of the Necessary Being. The reasoning unfolds through two branches, each leading to an absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the two-part structure: similar parts vs. different parts.
2
Follow the first branch: similar parts
Understand why similar parts would allow distant and contiguous parts to exchange positions, implying change in God.
The key point is that similars have the same properties, so what applies to one applies to the other.
3
Follow the second branch: different parts
See how the analysis leads to simple parts that must be symmetrical, and then to the possibility of exchange, again implying change.
The argument relies on the impossibility of composition in God.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Both branches lead to the same absurdity: conjunction and separation in God. Therefore directionality is impossible.
The proof is a classic reductio ad absurdum in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تلك الأجزاء إما أن تكون متماثلة الماهية ، أو مختلفة الماهية. والأول : محال . لأن على هذا التقدير يكون بعض تلك الاجزاء المتماثلة متباعدة ، وبعضها متلاقية ، والمثلان يصح على كل واحد منهما ما يصح على الآخر . فعلى هذا يلزم القطع بأنه يصح على المتلاقيين أن يصيرا متباعدين ، وعلى المتباعدين أن يصيرا متلاقيين : وذلك يقتضى جواز الاجتماع والافتراق على ذات الله تعالى ، وهو محال . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إن تلك الأجزاء مختلفة في الماهية . فلابد وأن ينتهى تحليل تركيبه إلى أجزاء ، يكون كل واحد منها مبرأ عن التركيب ، لأن التركيب عبارة عن اجتماع الوحدات . ولولا حصول الوحدات لما عقل اجتماعه ما . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء البسيطة ، لا بد وأن يماس كل واحد منها بيمينه شيئا ، وبيساره شيئا آخر. لكن يمينه مثل يساره . وإلا لكان فى نفسه مركبا . وقد فرضناه غير مركب . هذا خلف . وإذا ثبت أن يمينه مثل يساره ، وثبت أن المتلين لابد وأن يشتركا في جميع اللوازم لزم القطع بأن ممسوس يمينه ، يصح أن يصير ممسوس يساره ، وبالعكس. ومتى صح ذلك ، فقد صح التفرق والانحلال على تلك الأجزاء . وحينئذ يعود الأمر إلى جواز الاجتماع والافتراق على ذات الله تعالى. وهو محال . فثبت : أن القول بكونه في جهة من الجهات يفضي إلى هذه المحالات ، فيكون القول به محالا . وبالله التوفيق
Then those parts are either similar in essence or different in essence. The first is impossible, because on this supposition some of those similar parts would be distant and some contiguous, and similars are such that whatever is valid for one is valid for the other. Hence it necessarily follows that it is valid for the contiguous ones to become distant, and for the distant ones to become contiguous. This necessitates the possibility of conjunction and separation in the essence of God Almighty, which is impossible. As for the second division, that is that those parts are different in essence, then the analysis of its composition must lead to parts each of which is free from composition, because composition is the aggregation of units. Were there no units, its aggregation would not be conceivable. Once this is established, we say: each of those simple parts must have each of them touching something with its right side and another thing with its left side. But its right side is like its left side; otherwise it would itself be composite, but we have assumed it non-composite. This is a contradiction. And if it is established that its right side is like its left side, and it is established that two similars must share all concomitants, then it necessarily follows that what is touched by its right side can become what is touched by its left side, and vice versa. When that is valid, then separation and dissolution become valid for those parts. Then the matter returns to the possibility of conjunction and separation in the essence of God Almighty, which is impossible. So it is established: the statement that He is in a direction leads to these absurdities, therefore the statement is impossible. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof (المسألة السابعة) against attributing spatial direction to God. It is a reductio ad absurdum argument that analyzes the notion of parts if God were in a direction. The author first assumes that if God occupies a direction, He must have parts (أجزاء) due to extension. He then examines two possibilities: either these parts are identical in essence (متماثلة) or different (مختلفة). The first case is impossible because then the identical parts would be some distant and some contiguous, but similars can exchange states, implying change in God. The second case leads to analysis of composition into simple parts that touch neighbors; symmetry demands each simple part be the same on each side, but then the things it touches could swap places, again implying division and recomposition. Both paths lead to the conclusion that God would be subject to conjunction and separation (الاجتماع والافتراق), which is impossible for the Necessary Being. Hence the original assumption that God is in a direction is false. The proof is a classic kalam argument based on the impossibility of composition in the divine essence.

Key Terms
أجزاء (parts)
متماثلة (similar)
مختلفة (different)
تركيب (composition)
محال (impossible)
اجتماع وافتراق (conjunction and separation)
جهة (direction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Directionality is a simple attribute that does not entail any complexity in God.
Correction: Razi shows that directionality necessarily implies extension, parts, and potential change, all of which are impossible for the simple, immutable God.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's seventh proof, what is the consequence of assuming that God's parts are similar in essence?
A) They would be eternally fixed.
B) They could exchange positions, implying change.
C) They would be non-composite.
D) They would be infinite.

Key Takeaway
Directionality implies composition, which implies change; since God is immutable, directionality cannot be attributed to Him.

?
If directionality leads to absurdities, then what does that mean for the way you orient your worship? How can you fill the gap in your heart when you're not leaning on a spatial concept of God?
?
This argument shows that God cannot be subject to change or composition. In which specific areas of your life do you find yourself acting as if God is changeable or limited? What concrete step can you take today to align your belief with this truth?

The Eighth Proof: Spatial Elevation Implies Deficiency
Razi's eighth proof against spatial elevation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why spatial elevation for God would imply that God is deficient and dependent on location, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Spatial elevation makes God dependent on a direction, implying deficiency.

Razi's eighth proof targets the claim that God's elevation is spatial. He argues that if God's elevation were by direction and space, then that direction itself would possess a more fundamental elevation. This would make God's attribute accidental, implying deficiency. This passage shows the logical consequence of spatial language for God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise
Read the first line: 'If the elevation of the Creator over the world were by space and direction...' This is the conditional assumption the proof examines.
2
Understand the scenario
Consider the example: if God were on the right or left of the world, He wouldn't be elevated. But the direction 'above' is inherently elevated.
This shows that the direction itself is more fundamentally elevated.
3
Grasp the consequence
If God's elevation is derived from being in a direction, then He is deficient in Himself and perfected by another. This is impossible for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثامن : لو كان علو البارى تعالى على العالم بالحيز والجهة لمكان على تلك الجهة أكمل من علو البارى تعالى . وذلك لان بتقدير أن تحصل ذات الله تعالى في يمين العالم أو يساره ، لم يكن مرصو[؟] بالعلو على العالم. أما تلك الجهة التى فى جهة العلو ، فلا يمكن فرض وجودها خالية عن هذا العلو . فثبت : أن تلك الجهة عالية من العالم الذاتها ، وثبت : أن الحاصل فى تلك الجهة يكون عاليا . لا لذاته ، لكن تبعا لكونه حاصلا في تلك الجهة العالية على العالم . وإذا كان كذلك ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون البارى تعالى ناقصا لذاته مستكملا بغيره . وذلك محال . فثبت : أنه يمتنع أن يكون علوه على العالم بالجهة ، والحيز وذلك هو المطلوب
The eighth proof: If the elevation of the Creator, exalted be He, over the world were by space and direction, then that direction would have a more perfect elevation than the elevation of the Creator. That is because, supposing that the essence of God Almighty occurs on the right or left of the world, it would not be described[؟] with elevation over the world. As for that direction which is in the direction of elevation, it cannot be supposed to exist devoid of this elevation. So it is established that that direction is inherently elevated over the world, and it is established that what occurs in that direction is elevated, not by itself, but because it occurs in that direction which is elevated over the world. If this is so, then it follows necessarily that the Creator, exalted be He, is deficient in Himself and perfected by another. That is impossible. So it is established that His elevation over the world by direction and space is impossible. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof (البرهان الثامن) against spatial elevation for God. The argument is a simple syllogism: If God's elevation over the world is spatial (by direction and space), then the direction itself (e.g., 'above') possesses a more perfect elevation than God. Why? Because if God were placed on the right or left of the world, He would not be described as elevated over the world at all. But the direction 'above' cannot be conceived without being elevated. Therefore, the direction is inherently elevated (عالية لذاتها). Anything placed in that direction becomes elevated derivatively. Thus, God's elevation would be accidental and dependent on the direction, implying that God is deficient in Himself (ناقص لذاته) and perfected by something else (مستكمل بغيره). This is impossible for the Necessary Being. Hence, His elevation cannot be spatial. The proof is concise and relies on the principle that attributes of perfection must be essential, not derived from an external factor.

Key Terms
علو (elevation)
حيز (space)
جهة (direction)
ناقص (deficient)
مستكمل (perfected by another)
محال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'above' is a relational term that does not entail ontological dependence.
Correction: The proof shows that if 'above' is spatial, the location itself is more fundamental, making God's attribute dependent.

Checkpoint
What does Razi's eighth proof conclude about God's elevation if it were spatial?
A) It would be perfect.
B) It would be eternal.
C) It would imply God is deficient and perfected by another.
D) It would prove His existence.

Key Takeaway
Spatial elevation for God is impossible because it would make His attribute dependent on a direction, implying deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Why God's Highness Is Not Spatial
Imam Razi proves that God's highness cannot be by direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's attribute of highness (ʿuluw) cannot be interpreted as spatial direction or location, based on Imam Razi's logical proof.

Core Idea
God's highness is intrinsic, not by location.

Imam Razi presents a concise logical argument to show that if God's highness were by direction, it would imply deficiency in God. This proof is essential for understanding divine transcendence (tanzih) in Sunni theology. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow without analyzing deeply.
Notice the word 'مرصو' in Arabic; it may be a scribal error, but the meaning is clear.
2
Understand the hypothetical scenario
The argument supposes God's essence could be on a specific spatial direction (e.g., right or left). Understand that this is a supposition for refutation.
3
Trace the logical consequence
If God were in a direction, He would not be high by Himself; the direction itself would be high. God would derive highness from that direction, implying need and deficiency.
This is the core of the proof: a contingent attribute cannot belong to the Necessary Being.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since deficiency in God is impossible, the initial supposition is false. Therefore, God's highness is not by direction or place.
The phrase 'وذلك هو المطلوب' signals the conclusion.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this proof protects God's transcendence. It shows that attributing spatial qualities to God leads to absurdity.
This aligns with the Sunni creed of tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لمكان على تلك الجهة أكمل من علو البارى تعالى . وذلك لان بتقدير أن تحصل ذات الله تعالى في يمين العالم أو يساره ، لم يكن مرصو بالعلو على العالم. أما تلك الجهة التى فى جهة العلو ، فلا يمكن فرض وجودها خالية عن هذا العلو . فثبت : أن تلك الجهة عالية من العالم الذاتها ، وثبت : أن الحاصل فى تلك الجهة يكون عاليا . لا لذاته ، لكن تبعا لكونه حاصلا في تلك الجهة العالية على العالم . وإذا كان كذلك ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون البارى تعالى ناقصا لذاته مستكملا بغيره . وذلك محال . فثبت : أنه يمتنع أن يكون علوه على العالم بالجهة ، والحيز وذلك هو المطلوب
Because that direction is more complete than the highness of the Creator the Exalted. For if we suppose that the essence of Allah the Exalted were on the right side of the world or its left, He would not be designated by highness over the world. As for that direction which is in the direction of highness, it cannot be conceived to exist devoid of this highness. So it is established that that direction is high from the world by its essence, and it is established that what occurs in that direction is high, not by itself, but by virtue of its occurring in that direction which is high over the world. If that is so, then it would necessarily follow that the Creator the Exalted is deficient in His essence and perfected by another. That is impossible. So it is established that it is impossible for His highness over the world to be by direction and place, and that is what was required.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a proof demonstrating that God's highness cannot be in terms of direction or spatial location. The argument proceeds as follows: Suppose God's essence were located at a specific spatial direction relative to the world (e.g., right or left). Then He would not inherently possess highness over the world; rather, highness would belong to that direction itself, and God would only be considered high because He occupies that direction. This would imply that God is deficient in Himself (lacking highness by His own essence) and becomes perfect only through something else (the direction). Since deficiency in God is impossible, it follows that His highness is not directional or spatial. The conclusion is that God's attribute of highness (ʿuluw) is not by way of direction or place.

Key Terms
علو (ʿuluw)
جهة (jiha)
حيز (ḥayyiz)
نقص (naqṣ)
استكمال (istikmāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's highness is spatial, like being above a physical throne.
Correction: This proof shows that interpreting highness spatially would imply God's perfection depends on a direction, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.

Checkpoint

A) God would be superior to all directions.
B) God would be deficient in His essence and perfected by another.
C) The world would be in the same direction as God.
D) God's highness would be self-evident.

Key Takeaway
God's highness is an intrinsic attribute of His essence, not a matter of spatial direction or place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The First Doubt: God Must Be in a Direction
Imam Razi states the first rational doubt about God's direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational doubt that claims God must be spatially confined and the logical structure of the opponent's argument.

Core Idea
The doubt forces God into a direction by applying a rule about all existents.

Imam Razi begins by presenting the first rational objection to divine transcendence. The opponents argue from the coexistence of God and the world to the necessity of spatial relationship. This lesson lays out their concise argument before any refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt statement
Read the Arabic passage carefully and then the translation. Notice the three-step logical flow.
The argument is very compact—each sentence is a premise or conclusion.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the two premises: (1) any two existents are either adjacent or separated, (2) God is not adjacent to the world. See how they lead to the conclusion.
The conclusion is that God must be separated and thus confined to an upward direction.
3
Reflect on the force of the argument
Why does this argument seem compelling? Consider that it claims to be based on a universal rule about existents.
The next lessons will show how the author dismantles this reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس
في حكاية الشبه العقلية في كونه تعالى مختصاً بالحيز والجهة
الشبهة الأولى : إنهم قالوا : العالم موجود ، والبارى موجود . وكل موجودين فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما محايثاً للآخر ، أو مباينا عنه بجهة من الجهات الست . ولما لم يكن البارى تعالى محايثاً للعالم . وجب كونه تعالى مباينا عن العالم بجهة من الجهات الست وإذا ثبت ذلك، وجب كونه تعالى مختصاً بجهة فوق .
أما قولهم : إن كل موجودين فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما محايثا للآخر، أو مباينا عنه بجهة . فلهم فيه طريقان :
الأول : ادعاء الضرورة فيه . إلا أنه سبق الكلام على هذه الطريقة في أول الكتاب . والطريق الثاني : إنهم يستدلون عليه . وهو الطريق الذي اختاره ابن الهيثم في المناظرة التي حكاها عن نفسه مع ابن فورك ( قال الشيخ - رحمه الله تعالى - وأنا أذكر محصل تلك الكلمات على الترتيب الصحيح ، وعلى أحسن وجه يمكن تقرير الشبهة به (وهو) أن يقال: لاشك أن كل موجودين في الشاهد، فأحدهما لابد وأن يكون محايثاً للآخر ، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة وكون كل موجودين في الشاهد كذلك ، إما أن يكون الخصوص كونه جوهراً ، أو الخصوص كونه عرضا أو لأمر مشترك بين الجوهر والعرض . وذلك المشترك إما الحدوث. أو الوجود . والكل باطل سوى الوجود، فوجب أن تكون العلة لهذا الحكم هي الوجود، والبارى تعالى موجود، فوجب الجزم بأنه تعالى إما أن يكون محايثا للعالم . أو مباينا عنه بالجهة ..
واعلم : أن هذا الكلام لا يتم إلا بتقرير مقدمات . نحن نذكرها .
و نذكر الوجوه التي يمكن ذكرها في تقرير تلك المقدمات.
أما المقدمة الأولى : ( فهى أن نقول : قولنا : إن القول بأن كل موجودين في الشاهد ، لا بد وأن يكون أحدهما محايثا للآخر أو مباينا عنه بجهة : حكم لابد له من علة ويحتاج إلى دليل . والدليل عليه : هو أن المعدومات لا يصح فيها هذا الحكم ، وهذه الموجودات يصح فيها هذا الحكم ، فلو لا امتياز ما صح فيه هذا الحكم، عما لا يصح فيه هذا الحكم بأمر من الأمور ( وإلا ) لما كان هذا الامتياز واقعا .
وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهي في بيان أن هذا الحكم لا يمكن تعليله بخصوص كونه جوهراً ، ولا بخصوص كونه عرضا
فالدليل عليه . أن المقتضى لهذا الحكم . لو كان هو كونه جوهراً لصدق على الجوهر أنه منقسم إلى ما يكون محايثا لغيره، وإلى ما ينكون[؟]
Chapter Five: On narrating the rational doubts regarding His being confined to space and direction.
The first doubt: They said: The world exists, and the Creator exists. And every two existents, it is necessary that one of them be adjacent to the other or separated from it by one of the six directions. And since the Creator, exalted, is not adjacent to the world, it is necessary that He, exalted, be separated from the world by one of the six directions. And when that is established, it is necessary that He, exalted, be confined to an upward direction.
As for their statement: Every two existents must be such that one is adjacent to the other or separated from it by a direction. For them there are two ways:
The first: claiming self-evidence for it. But discussion of this way has preceded at the beginning of the book. The second way: that they infer it. And it is the way chosen by Ibn al-Haytham in the debate he narrated about himself with Ibn Furak. (The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: And I will mention the gist of those words in proper order and in the best manner possible for presenting the doubt.) And it is that it be said: There is no doubt that every two existents in the witnessed [world], one of them must be adjacent to the other or separated from it by direction. And that every two existents in the witnessed [world] are likewise; either the specification is that it is a substance, or the specification is that it is an accident, or due to something common between substance and accident. And that common thing is either temporality or existence. All are invalid except existence. So it follows that the cause of this judgment is existence. And the Creator, exalted, exists. So it is necessary to be certain that He, exalted, is either adjacent to the world or separated from it by direction.
And know: This statement is not complete except by establishing premises. We will mention them. And we will mention the ways that can be mentioned in establishing those premises.
As for the first premise: It is that we say: Our statement: The statement that every two existents in the witnessed [world] must have one adjacent to the other or separated from it by direction: a judgment that must have a cause and needs a proof. And the proof for it is that non-existents are not valid for this judgment, and these existents are valid for this judgment. So were it not for a distinction of what this judgment is valid for from what it is not valid for by some matter, that distinction would not occur.
As for the second premise: It is in explaining that this judgment cannot be caused by the specification of being a substance, nor by the specification of being an accident. So the proof for it is that if the requirer of this judgment were its being a substance, then it would be true of the substance that it is divisible into what is adjacent to something else and into what is [incomplete].
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the first rational doubt (shubha) that aims to prove God must be confined to a spatial direction. The author then begins to refute it by analyzing its premises. 

The opponents argue: (1) The world exists and the Creator exists. (2) Any two existents must either be mutually adjacent (muhayith) or separated by one of the six directions. (3) Since God is not adjacent to the world (He is not spatially intermixed with it), He must be separated from it by a direction. (4) Therefore, God is confined to an upward direction (jiha fawq). 

Imam Razi notes that the opponents use two methods to support the premise that any two existents must be either adjacent or separated. The first method claims it is self-evident (darura), but he already discussed this in the beginning of the book. The second method is the inferential approach taken by Ibn al-Haytham in his debate with Ibn Furak. 

The essence of this second method is as follows: In the witnessed world, all pairs of existents are either adjacent or directionally separated. Now, what is the cause (‘illa) of this universal rule? The cause cannot be that they are substances or accidents, because the rule applies to both. Nor can it be temporality, because temporality is not universal. So the only remaining common factor is existence (wujud). Since existence is the cause, and God exists, the rule must apply to Him — He must be either adjacent or separated from the world.

The author warns that this argument cannot stand without establishing certain premises (muqaddimat). He will list them. The first premise: The judgment that every two existents in the witnessed world are either adjacent or separated is a judgment that requires a cause (illa) and proof (dalil). The proof is that non-existents do not fall under this judgment, so the fact that existents do indicates a distinguishing cause. The second premise: This judgment cannot be caused by the specific nature of being a substance or an accident. The proof is that if the cause were substantiality, then every substance would have to be divisible into what is adjacent and what is not — here the text breaks off, but the overall argument is to show that the cause must be existence itself.

This passage is a careful deconstruction of the doubt: the author first states the objection, then outlines the opponents' reasoning, and finally begins the systematic refutation by demanding explicit premises and demonstrating their weaknesses.

A) Because the Creator is not adjacent to the world, and any two existents must either be adjacent or separated.
B) Because the Creator is a substance and substances occupy space.
C) Because the world is in the upward direction and the Creator must be above it.

Two Methods to Prove the Key Premise
Imam Razi presents the two methods of proving the key premise and details the inferential method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two methods the opponents use to support the premise that any two existents must be adjacent or separated, and the inferential method in detail.

Core Idea
The opponents rely either on self-evidence or on causal inference to justify their key premise.

After stating the doubt, Imam Razi explains that the opponents have two approaches to prove the universal rule. The first is claiming self-evidence, which he dismisses as already refuted. The second is a sophisticated inference used by Ibn al-Haytham, which attempts to derive the rule from the nature of existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
Read the passage and note that the first method is 'self-evidence' and the second is 'inference'. See how the author dismisses the first and elaborates the second.
The author had already refuted the self-evidence claim earlier in the book.
2
Follow the inferential argument
The inferential method argues that the rule applies to all existents in the witnessed world. It then analyzes what causes that rule—substance, accident, temporality, or existence. Follow this reasoning step by step.
The argument uses elimination to conclude that existence itself is the cause.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since existence is the cause, and God exists, the rule must apply to God—He must be either adjacent or separated.
This conclusion sets up the need to examine the premises more carefully.

Source Text

الفصل الخامس
في حكاية الشبه العقلية في كونه تعالى مختصاً بالحيز والجهة
الشبهة الأولى : إنهم قالوا : العالم موجود ، والبارى موجود . وكل موجودين فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما محايثاً للآخر ، أو مباينا عنه بجهة من الجهات الست . ولما لم يكن البارى تعالى محايثاً للعالم . وجب كونه تعالى مباينا عن العالم بجهة من الجهات الست وإذا ثبت ذلك، وجب كونه تعالى مختصاً بجهة فوق .
أما قولهم : إن كل موجودين فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما محايثا للآخر، أو مباينا عنه بجهة . فلهم فيه طريقان :
الأول : ادعاء الضرورة فيه . إلا أنه سبق الكلام على هذه الطريقة في أول الكتاب . والطريق الثاني : إنهم يستدلون عليه . وهو الطريق الذي اختاره ابن الهيثم في المناظرة التي حكاها عن نفسه مع ابن فورك ( قال الشيخ - رحمه الله تعالى - وأنا أذكر محصل تلك الكلمات على الترتيب الصحيح ، وعلى أحسن وجه يمكن تقرير الشبهة به (وهو) أن يقال: لاشك أن كل موجودين في الشاهد، فأحدهما لابد وأن يكون محايثاً للآخر ، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة وكون كل موجودين في الشاهد كذلك ، إما أن يكون الخصوص كونه جوهراً ، أو الخصوص كونه عرضا أو لأمر مشترك بين الجوهر والعرض . وذلك المشترك إما الحدوث. أو الوجود . والكل باطل سوى الوجود، فوجب أن تكون العلة لهذا الحكم هي الوجود، والبارى تعالى موجود، فوجب الجزم بأنه تعالى إما أن يكون محايثا للعالم . أو مباينا عنه بالجهة ..
واعلم : أن هذا الكلام لا يتم إلا بتقرير مقدمات . نحن نذكرها .
و نذكر الوجوه التي يمكن ذكرها في تقرير تلك المقدمات.
أما المقدمة الأولى : ( فهى أن نقول : قولنا : إن القول بأن كل موجودين في الشاهد ، لا بد وأن يكون أحدهما محايثا للآخر أو مباينا عنه بجهة : حكم لابد له من علة ويحتاج إلى دليل . والدليل عليه : هو أن المعدومات لا يصح فيها هذا الحكم ، وهذه الموجودات يصح فيها هذا الحكم ، فلو لا امتياز ما صح فيه هذا الحكم، عما لا يصح فيه هذا الحكم بأمر من الأمور ( وإلا ) لما كان هذا الامتياز واقعا .
وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهي في بيان أن هذا الحكم لا يمكن تعليله بخصوص كونه جوهراً ، ولا بخصوص كونه عرضا
فالدليل عليه . أن المقتضى لهذا الحكم . لو كان هو كونه جوهراً لصدق على الجوهر أنه منقسم إلى ما يكون محايثا لغيره، وإلى ما ينكون[؟]
Chapter Five: On narrating the rational doubts regarding His being confined to space and direction.
The first doubt: They said: The world exists, and the Creator exists. And every two existents, it is necessary that one of them be adjacent to the other or separated from it by one of the six directions. And since the Creator, exalted, is not adjacent to the world, it is necessary that He, exalted, be separated from the world by one of the six directions. And when that is established, it is necessary that He, exalted, be confined to an upward direction.
As for their statement: Every two existents must be such that one is adjacent to the other or separated from it by a direction. For them there are two ways:
The first: claiming self-evidence for it. But discussion of this way has preceded at the beginning of the book. The second way: that they infer it. And it is the way chosen by Ibn al-Haytham in the debate he narrated about himself with Ibn Furak. (The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: And I will mention the gist of those words in proper order and in the best manner possible for presenting the doubt.) And it is that it be said: There is no doubt that every two existents in the witnessed [world], one of them must be adjacent to the other or separated from it by direction. And that every two existents in the witnessed [world] are likewise; either the specification is that it is a substance, or the specification is that it is an accident, or due to something common between substance and accident. And that common thing is either temporality or existence. All are invalid except existence. So it follows that the cause of this judgment is existence. And the Creator, exalted, exists. So it is necessary to be certain that He, exalted, is either adjacent to the world or separated from it by direction.
And know: This statement is not complete except by establishing premises. We will mention them. And we will mention the ways that can be mentioned in establishing those premises.
As for the first premise: It is that we say: Our statement: The statement that every two existents in the witnessed [world] must have one adjacent to the other or separated from it by direction: a judgment that must have a cause and needs a proof. And the proof for it is that non-existents are not valid for this judgment, and these existents are valid for this judgment. So were it not for a distinction of what this judgment is valid for from what it is not valid for by some matter, that distinction would not occur.
As for the second premise: It is in explaining that this judgment cannot be caused by the specification of being a substance, nor by the specification of being an accident. So the proof for it is that if the requirer of this judgment were its being a substance, then it would be true of the substance that it is divisible into what is adjacent to something else and into what is [incomplete].
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the first rational doubt (shubha) that aims to prove God must be confined to a spatial direction. The author then begins to refute it by analyzing its premises. 

The opponents argue: (1) The world exists and the Creator exists. (2) Any two existents must either be mutually adjacent (muhayith) or separated by one of the six directions. (3) Since God is not adjacent to the world (He is not spatially intermixed with it), He must be separated from it by a direction. (4) Therefore, God is confined to an upward direction (jiha fawq). 

Imam Razi notes that the opponents use two methods to support the premise that any two existents must be either adjacent or separated. The first method claims it is self-evident (darura), but he already discussed this in the beginning of the book. The second method is the inferential approach taken by Ibn al-Haytham in his debate with Ibn Furak. 

The essence of this second method is as follows: In the witnessed world, all pairs of existents are either adjacent or directionally separated. Now, what is the cause (‘illa) of this universal rule? The cause cannot be that they are substances or accidents, because the rule applies to both. Nor can it be temporality, because temporality is not universal. So the only remaining common factor is existence (wujud). Since existence is the cause, and God exists, the rule must apply to Him — He must be either adjacent or separated from the world.

The author warns that this argument cannot stand without establishing certain premises (muqaddimat). He will list them. The first premise: The judgment that every two existents in the witnessed world are either adjacent or separated is a judgment that requires a cause (illa) and proof (dalil). The proof is that non-existents do not fall under this judgment, so the fact that existents do indicates a distinguishing cause. The second premise: This judgment cannot be caused by the specific nature of being a substance or an accident. The proof is that if the cause were substantiality, then every substance would have to be divisible into what is adjacent and what is not — here the text breaks off, but the overall argument is to show that the cause must be existence itself.

This passage is a careful deconstruction of the doubt: the author first states the objection, then outlines the opponents' reasoning, and finally begins the systematic refutation by demanding explicit premises and demonstrating their weaknesses.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The inferential argument is sound and forces God into spatial categories.
Correction: The author will show that the argument depends on premises that are not necessarily true, such as the assumption that the witnessed world provides a universal rule for all existents.

Checkpoint
According to the inferential method, what is the cause of the rule that two existents must be adjacent or separated?
A) Substantiality
B) Accidentality
C) Temporality
D) Existence

Key Takeaway
The opponents' inferential method attempts to prove that existence itself necessitates spatial relation, but this conclusion depends on premises that require scrutiny.

Analyzing the Premises: The Need for a Cause
Imam Razi sets up the first two premises needed to refute the opponents' inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two premises that Imam Razi sets up to refute the inferential method, and how he argues that the judgment requires a cause and that cause cannot be substantiality or accidentality.

Core Idea
The opponent's argument collapses once we demand that the rule 'two existents are adjacent or separated' must have a cause, and that cause cannot be found in the specific nature of substances or accidents.

Imam Razi now begins his refutation by stating that the inferential argument is incomplete without establishing certain premises. He introduces the first premise: that the judgment about existents in the witnessed world must have a cause. Then he introduces the second premise: that this judgment cannot be caused by the specific characteristics of being a substance or an accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the announcement of premises
The author says the argument 'is not complete except by establishing premises'. This signals that he is about to deconstruct the inferential method.
He will then list the premises one by one.
2
Understand the first premise
The first premise is that the judgment about two existents in the witnessed world requires a cause. The proof is that non-existents do not have this judgment, so there must be a distinguishing factor.
This is a general principle of causality in judgments.
3
Understand the second premise
The second premise is that the cause cannot be substantiality or accidentality. The proof is that if it were substantiality, then every substance would have to be divisible into adjacent and non-adjacent, which is not necessarily true.
The text cuts off here, but the argument aims to show that neither substance nor accident can be the cause.

Divine Transcendence
intermediate

The Third Premise: Judgment Not Caused by Temporality
Razi gives two proofs that huduth is not the cause of the immanence/transcendence judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the judgment of immanence or transcendence is not caused by temporality, and how two proofs—one conceptual and one based on the materialist's belief—establish this.

Core Idea
Temporality cannot cause the immanence/transcendence judgment because non-existence is not a cause.

Imam Razi now addresses a potential objection: perhaps the judgment that a thing is either immanent or transcendent depends on its being created (hadith). To refute this, he presents two compelling proofs. The first is a simple logical analysis of the definition of temporality. The second is a powerful reductio argument involving the materialist who believes in eternal heavens yet still affirms the judgment. This lesson unpacks these proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its English translation. Pay attention to the two proofs Razi presents.
2
Understand the first proof
The first proof is conceptual: temporality (huduth) involves non-existence, and non-existence cannot be a cause. Therefore, only existence remains, but existence is common to both eternal and temporal things.
This shows that the judgment cannot be limited to temporal things.
3
Understand the second proof
The second proof is a reductio: if huduth were the cause, then ignorance of huduth would prevent the judgment. But materialists who believe the heavens are eternal still affirm the judgment, so huduth cannot be the cause.
Razi strengthens this with analogies involving existence and color.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this premise is important for divine transcendence: it shows that the immanence/transcendence distinction applies to all existents, regardless of temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة وهي في بيان أن هذا الحكم غير معلل بالحدوث ويدل عليه وجوه الوجه الأول إن الحدوث عبارة عن وجود سبقه العدم والعدم غير داخل في العلة وإذا سقط العدم عن درجة الاعتبار لم يبق إلا الوجود والوجه الثاني وهو الذي عول عليه ابن الهيصم في المناظرة التي زعم أنها دارت بينه وبين ابن فورك رحمهما الله تعالى لو كان هذا الحكم معللا بالحدوث لكان الجاهل بكون السماء محدثة يجب أن يكون جاهلا بأن السماء بالنسبة إلى سائر الموجودات التي في هذا العالم إما أن تكون محايثة لها أو مباينة عنها بالجهة لأن المقتضى للحكم إذا كان أمرا معينا فالجاهل بذلك المقتضى يجب أن يكون جاهلا بذلك الحكم ألا ترى أن الوجود لما كان هو المستدعى للتقسيم إلى القديم والمحدث لاجرم كان اعتقاد أنه غير موجود مانعا من التقسيم بالقدم والحدوث ولما كان التقسيم إلى الأسود والأبيض معلقا بكونه ملونا كان اعتقاد أن الشيء غير ملون مانعا من اعتقاد التقسيم إلى الأسود والأبيض ولما رأينا أن الدهري الذي يعتقد قدم السموات والأرضين لا يمنعه ذلك من اعتقاد أن السموات والأرضين إما أن تكون محايثة وإما أن تكون مباينة بالجهة علمنا أن هذا الحكم غير معلل بالحدوث.
The third premise: it is in explaining that this judgment is not caused by temporality. Two aspects indicate it: The first aspect: temporality is existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence is not included in the cause. And when non-existence falls from the degree of consideration, nothing remains except existence. The second aspect—which Ibn al-Haysam relied on in the debate that he claims took place between him and Ibn Furak, may God have mercy on them both: if this judgment were caused by temporality, then one ignorant of the heaven being created would necessarily be ignorant that the heaven, in relation to the other existents in this world, is either immanent in them or separate from them by direction, because the efficient cause of the judgment, if it is something specific, then one ignorant of that efficient cause must be ignorant of that judgment. Do you not see that when existence is what calls for the division into eternal and originated, inevitably the belief that it is non-existent prevents the division into eternal and originated? And when the division into black and white is dependent on it being colored, the belief that a thing is not colored prevents belief in its division into black and white. And when we see that the materialist who believes in the eternity of the heavens and the earth is not prevented by that from believing that the heavens and the earth are either immanent or separate by direction, we know that this judgment is not caused by temporality.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the third premise: that the judgment of immanence or transcendence is not caused by temporality (huduth). Razi provides two proofs. The first proof is conceptual: temporality is defined as existence preceded by non-existence. Since non-existence cannot be a cause, it drops out of consideration, leaving only existence. But existence is common to both eternal and temporal things, so the judgment cannot be limited to temporal things. The second proof, attributed to Ibn al-Haysam in a debate with Ibn Furak, is a reductio: if the judgment were caused by huduth, then someone who is ignorant of a thing's huduth would also be ignorant of its immanence or transcendence relative to other existents. But we observe that materialists (dahriyya) who believe the heavens and earth are eternal still affirm the judgment of immanence or transcendence regarding them. Therefore, huduth cannot be the cause. Razi reinforces this by analogy: just as ignorance of existence prevents the division into eternal and originated, and ignorance of color prevents the division into black and white, similarly ignorance of temporality should prevent the division into immanent and separate if temporality were the cause. Since it does not, the judgment is independent of temporality.

Key Terms
حدوث
وجود
عدم
علة
دهري
قديم
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only created things have spatial relations with others, so the immanence/transcendence judgment depends on temporality.
Correction: Razi shows that even eternal things (as per materialists) are subject to the immanence/transcendence distinction, so the judgment is independent of temporality.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that huduth is not the cause of the immanence/transcendence judgment?
A) Temporality is preceded by non-existence, and non-existence cannot be a cause.
B) Materialists affirm the judgment.
C) Substances cannot be immanent.
D) The judgment applies only to created things.

Key Takeaway
The judgment of immanence or transcendence is independent of temporality; it applies to all existents, whether eternal or created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that non-existence cannot cause the immanence/transcendence judgment. In what ways might you be allowing your own sense of non-existence—your past or future death—to limit how you perceive God's relationship to you? Will you actively remind yourself that God's existence is not conditioned by your own temporality?
?
The materialist in the passage still affirms that the heavens are either immanent or separate, despite denying creation. Do you ever act as if God's immanence or transcendence depends on your own createdness? What would change in your worship if you truly internalized that God's relationship to creation is independent of time?
?
Razi uses the analogy of color: ignorance of color prevents the division into black and white. What 'color'—what essential property—are you ignorant of that might be preventing you from correctly judging your own position relative to God? Commit to identifying one such ignorance today.

Third Proof: Determinant is Not Temporal Origination
Razi presents the third proof that the determinant is not ḥudūth, citing Ibn al-Haytham.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal origination cannot be the rational determinant for the necessary judgment of immanence or separation.

Core Idea
Inference cannot ground necessity.

Razi presents a third proof, attributed to Ibn al-Haytham, to show that the determinant of the ruling that an existent must be either immanent or separate is not its temporal origination. The argument hinges on the different epistemic statuses of the two claims: one is known by inference, the other by necessity. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall argument.
2
Identify the two types of knowledge
Notice how Razi distinguishes between knowledge by inference (istidlāl) and knowledge by necessity (ḍarūra). The property of being temporally originated is known by inference, while the judgment of immanence or separation is known necessarily.
3
Understand the epistemic gap
Reflect on why a property known by inference cannot serve as the basis for a judgment known by necessity. Inference yields probable or mediated knowledge, while necessity yields immediate and certain knowledge.
4
Draw the conclusion
Conclude that temporal origination (ḥudūth) is not the determinant (muqtaḍā) of the ruling. This completes the third proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث في بيان أن المقتضى هذا الحكم ليس هو الحدوث : وقد ذكره ابن الهيصم أيضا في تلك المناظرة - وتقريره : أن كونه محدثا : وصف يعلم بالاستدلال ، وكونه بحيث يجب أن يكون إما محايثا ، وأما مباينا بالجهة : حكم معلوم بالضرورة، والوصف المعلوم الثبوت بالاستدلال : لا يجوز أن يكون أصلا للحكم الذي يعلم ثبوته بالضرورة . مثبت بهذه الوجوه : أن المقتضى لهذا الحكم : ليس الحدوث .
The third point in explaining that the determinant of this ruling is not temporal origination: And Ibn al-Haytham also mentioned it in that debate. Its formulation: That it being temporally originated is a property known by inference, and that it is such that it must be either immanent or separate in direction is a judgment known by necessity. The property whose existence is known by inference cannot be a basis for the judgment whose existence is known by necessity. It is established by these aspects that the determinant of this ruling is not temporal origination.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a proof. Razi states the third point (al-wajh al-thālith) that the determinant (muqtaḍā) of the ruling that an existent must be either immanent or separate with respect to direction is not temporal origination (ḥudūth). He attributes this argument to Ibn al-Haytham. The reasoning: the property of being temporally originated (ḥādith) is known through inference (istidlāl), while the ruling that it must be either immanent or separate is known necessarily (bi-l-ḍarūra). A property established by inference cannot serve as the basis for a judgment known necessarily. Therefore, the determinant cannot be ḥudūth. The conclusion is restated: 'It is established by these aspects that the determinant of this ruling is not temporal origination.'

Key Terms
المقتضى
الحدوث
الاستدلال
الضرورة
المحايثة
المباينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any property of an existent can ground a necessary judgment.
Correction: Razi shows that a property known only through inference cannot serve as the basis for a judgment known necessarily, due to the difference in epistemic certainty.

Checkpoint
Why can't temporal origination (ḥudūth) be the determinant for the necessary judgment that an existent is either immanent or separate?
A) Because temporal origination is not a real property.
B) Because what is known through inference cannot ground what is known necessarily.
C) Because the judgment itself is not necessary.
D) Because Ibn al-Haytham said so.

Key Takeaway
The determinant of a necessary judgment cannot be a property known only through inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a theological belief you hold with certainty. Is that certainty based on necessary, self-evident knowledge, or on inferential reasoning? If it is based on inference, this lesson demands that you question whether that belief truly deserves the same level of certainty as what is known necessarily. How will you re-evaluate that belief today?
?
The proof shows that temporal origination (a property of all creation) cannot ground a necessary judgment about Allah. Have you ever mistakenly relied on created, temporal things to ground your trust in Allah's attributes? This lesson calls you to anchor your certainty only in what is necessary and revealed, not in your own reasoning. What specific created thing or personal reasoning will you consciously release as a basis for your faith?

First Objection: Philosophers' Non-Spatial Entities
First objection: philosophers' non-spatial entities as counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection that non-spatial entities like intellects and hyle challenge the exhaustive dichotomy of immanence and transcendence.

Core Idea
Not all existents fit the immanent/transcendent dichotomy; philosophers' non-spatial entities are counterexamples.

Imam Razi begins his response to the claim that every two existents must be either immanent or transcendent. He presents the first objection from the philosophers, who affirm entities that are neither spatial nor inhering in space. These include intellects, celestial souls, rational souls, and hyle. Understanding this objection is crucial for testing the validity of the dichotomy.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi presents the philosophers' view as a challenge.
2
Identify the entities listed
List the entities the philosophers affirm: intellects, celestial souls, rational souls, hyle. Notice they are described as non-spatial.
3
Understand the challenge
The philosophers claim these entities are neither immanent nor transcendent with respect to direction. Understand why this challenges the opponent's claim.
The opponent must refute this to maintain the dichotomy.
4
Reflect on the argument's structure
Notice the argument: premise (entities exist), description (non-spatial), conclusion (not covered by dichotomy), then challenge (opponent must disprove).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن جمهور الفلاسفة يثبتون موجودات غير محايثة لهذا العالم الجسماني ولا مباينة عنه بالجهة . وذلك لأنهم يثبتون العقول ، والنفوس الفلكية، والنفوس الناطقة ، ويثبتون الهيولى . ويزعمون : أن هذه الأشياء موجودات غير متحيزة ولا حالة فى المتحيز ، ولا يصدق عليها أنها محايثة لهذا العالم ولا مباينة عنه بالجهة . ومالم تبطلوا هذا المذهب بالدليل ، لا يصح القول بأن كل موجودين في الشاهد، فأما أن يكون أحدهما محايثاً للآخر أو مبايناً عنه .
الثاني : إن جمهور المعتزلة يثبتون إرادات وكراهات موجودة لا في محل . ويثبتون فناء لا في محل . وتلك الأشياء لا يصدق عليها أنها محايثة للعالم، أو مباينة عن العالم بالجهة فما لم تبطلوا ذلك، لا تتم دعواكم.
The first: The majority of philosophers affirm existents that are neither immanent in this bodily world nor transcendent from it with respect to direction. That is because they affirm the intellects, the celestial souls, the rational souls, and they affirm hyle. And they claim that these things are existents that are not located in space nor inhering in a spatial entity, and it cannot be said of them that they are immanent in this world nor transcendent from it with respect to direction. And as long as you do not refute this doctrine by proof, it is not valid to say that for every two existents in the witnessed world, either one of them is immanent in the other or transcendent from it.
The second: The majority of the Mu'tazila affirm volitions and aversions that exist not in a locus. And they affirm annihilation not in a locus. And those things cannot be said to be immanent in the world, nor transcendent from the world with respect to direction. So as long as you do not refute that, your claim is not established.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the opponent's claim that in the witnessed world, every two existents must be either immanent (muhayath) in each other or transcendent (mubayin) from each other with respect to direction (jiha). The first objection cites the philosophers (falasifa) who affirm entities such as the intellects ('uqul), celestial souls (nufus falakiyya), rational souls (nufus natiqa), and hyle (hayula). These are said to be non-spatial (ghayr mutahayyiza) and not inhering in a spatial entity. Therefore, they cannot be described as immanent or transcendent in the required sense. The opponent must first refute this doctrine to maintain their claim. The second objection cites the Mu'tazila, who affirm volitions (iradat) and aversions (karahat) existing not in a locus (la fi mahall), as well as annihilation (fana') not in a locus. These too escape the dichotomy of immanence and transcendence. Unless these counterexamples are refuted, the opponent's claim remains unsubstantiated.

Key Terms
محايثة
مباينة
جهة
فلاسفة
هيولى
عقول
نفوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dichotomy of immanence and transcendence is exhaustive for all existents.
Correction: The philosophers present counterexamples that are neither, showing the dichotomy is not exhaustive without further proof.

Checkpoint
What do the philosophers affirm that challenges the dichotomy?
A) Spatial entities
B) Non-spatial entities like intellects and hyle
C) Material bodies
D) Accidents

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' non-spatial entities serve as counterexamples that the dichotomy of immanence and transcendence must address.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers' objection reveals that our mental categories of 'inside' and 'outside' may fail when applied to spiritual realities. Think of one area in your life where you have quickly judged something as either 'from God' or 'not from God'. How might this lesson challenge you to hold that judgment with more humility? Commit this week to revisiting that judgment, recognising that some truths transcend your categories.
?
Razi warns that unless we refute this objection, the dichotomy of immanence and transcendence is not valid. Consider a belief about God that you have never seriously questioned. What would it take for you to genuinely test that belief against a challenge like this? Are you willing to do that? Decide now to spend 10 minutes this week reading a source that questions a comfortable belief of yours.

Second Objection: Mu'tazila's Non-Locus Volitions
Second objection: Mu'tazila's non-locus existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's objection that volitions, aversions, and annihilation exist without a locus, challenging the immanence/transcendence dichotomy.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's non-locus entities (volitions, aversions, annihilation) escape the dichotomy just like the philosophers' entities.

Imam Razi presents the second objection from the Mu'tazila, who affirm volitions, aversions, and annihilation as existents without a locus. These too cannot be described as immanent or transcendent with respect to direction. This objection further undermines the opponent's claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the entities the Mu'tazila affirm: volitions, aversions, annihilation.
2
Identify the key characteristic
These entities exist 'not in a locus' (la fi mahall). Understand what this means.
They are not located in a physical space.
3
Connect to the challenge
Since they are not in a locus, they cannot be immanent or transcendent with respect to direction. This challenges the dichotomy.
4
Reflect on the cumulative force
Consider how this objection, together with the first, puts pressure on the opponent's claim.

Source Text

?

?

Third Objection: Relations as Real Positive Attributes
Imam Razi proves that relations are real positive attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi proves that relations (al-idafat) like fatherhood are real, positive attributes distinct from the essence.

Core Idea
Relations are not mental constructs but real objective attributes.

In this lesson, Imam Razi begins his third objection by establishing that relations are real existents. He uses the example of fatherhood to show that it is a positive attribute separate from the essence of the father. This argument lays the groundwork for the main objection against the dichotomy of immanence and transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note that Imam Razi states that relations are existent particulars (accidents) and then provides evidence.
The claim is that relations are real, not just mental concepts.
2
Examine the first proof
The first proof: one can conceive of an essence without thinking of its relation (e.g., being a father). Therefore, the relation is different from the essence.
This shows that the relation is a distinct attribute.
3
Examine the second proof
The second proof: Jesus (peace be upon him) had a full essence but no fatherhood or sonship. Thus, the relation is separate from the essence.
This reinforces the distinction.
4
Understand why fatherhood is positive
The different thing must be either negative or positive. It cannot be negative because fatherhood removes the absence (non-fatherhood), and removal of non-existence is existence. Hence, fatherhood is a positive attribute.
This finalizes that relations are real and positive.

Source Text

الثالث : إنا نقيم الدلالة على أن الإضافات موجودات في الإعيان ، ثم تبين أنها يمتنع أن تكون محايثة للعالم، أو مباينة عنه بالجهة وذلك يبطل كلامكم . وإنما قلنا: إن الإضافات أعراض موجودات في الأعيان، لأن المعقول من كون الإنسان أبا لغيره ، مغاير لذاته المخصوصة. بدليل: أنه يمكن أن يعقل ذاته مع الذهول عن كونه أبا أو ابنا . والمعلوم غير ما هو معلوم . وأيضا : فإنه يمكن ثبوت ذات منفكة عن الأبوة والبنوة . مثل : عيسى عليه السلام . فإنه ما كان أبا لأحد ، ولا ابنا لأحد . والثابت غير ما هو غير ثابت. فمعنى كونه أبا وابنا ، مغاير لذاته المخصوصة . ثم هذا المغاير إما أن يكون وصفا سلبيا أو ثبوتيا . والأول باطل . لأن عدم الأبوة هو الوصف السلبي . والأبوة رافعة له، ورفع العدم وجود . فثبت : أن الأبوة وصف وجودي مغاير لذات الأب، إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنه مستحيل أن يقال : الأبوة محايثة لذات الأب . وإلا لزم أن يقال : إنه قام بنصف الأب نصف الأبوة، وبثلثه ثلث الأبوة : ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . ومحال أن يقال : إنها مباينة عن ذات الأب ، مباينة بالجهة والحيز . وإلا لزم كون الأبوة جوهراً قائما بذاته مباينا عن ذات الأب بالجهة . وذلك أيضا محال . فثبت بهذا الدليل وجود موجود لا يمكن أن يقال : إنه محايث للعالم، أو يقال إنه مباين عنه بالجهة . وإذا ثبت هذا ، بطل قولهم .
The third: We establish the proof that relations are existent particulars, then we show that it is impossible for them to be immanent in the world or transcendent from it with respect to direction, and that refutes your statement. And we only say that relations are accidents existing in particulars because the conceived meaning of a man being a father to another is different from his specific essence. By evidence: it is possible to conceive his essence while disregarding his being a father or a son. And the known is other than what is known. Also, it is possible to affirm an essence separate from fatherhood and sonship, such as Jesus, peace be upon him, for he was not a father to anyone nor a son to anyone. And the affirmed is other than what is not affirmed. So the meaning of being a father and a son is different from his specific essence. Then this different thing is either a negative or a positive attribute. The first is invalid because the absence of fatherhood is the negative attribute, and fatherhood removes it, and the removal of non-existence is existence. So it is established that fatherhood is a positive attribute different from the essence of the father. When this is established, we say: It is impossible to say that fatherhood is immanent in the essence of the father, otherwise it would be necessary to say that half of fatherhood subsists in half of the father, and a third in a third of the father, and it is known that that is false. And it is impossible to say that it is transcendent from the essence of the father, transcendent with respect to direction and space, otherwise it would entail that fatherhood is a substance subsisting by itself, transcendent from the essence of the father with respect to direction, and that is also impossible. So it is established by this proof that there exists an existent of which it cannot be said that it is immanent in the world nor that it is transcendent from it with respect to direction. And when this is established, their statement is refuted.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection, which is a detailed proof that relations (al-idafat) are real existents and that they cannot be described as either immanent or transcendent, thus refuting the opponent's dichotomy. The argument begins by asserting that relations are accidents (a'rad) existing in particulars (a'yan). The example of fatherhood (ubuwwa) is used. Fatherhood is conceived as different from the essence of the father; one can think of the essence without thinking of fatherhood. Also, Jesus had no father or son, showing that essence can exist without these relations. Therefore, fatherhood is a positive attribute (wajudi) because it removes the negative attribute of non-fatherhood, and the removal of non-existence is existence. Once established that fatherhood is a real positive attribute, the argument examines whether it is immanent or transcendent. If immanent, it would have to be divisible according to the parts of the father, which is false. If transcendent, it would be a separate substance, which is also false. Therefore, fatherhood is neither immanent nor transcendent. By analogy, there must exist something of which the same holds, refuting the opponent's claim that all existents are either immanent or transcendent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Relations are merely mental constructs with no external reality.
Correction: Imam Razi proves that relations are real attributes existing in particulars, not just mental concepts.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi argue that fatherhood is a positive attribute?
A) Because it is conceived independently of the essence
B) Because it removes the absence of fatherhood, which is negative
C) Because it is a substance existing by itself
D) Because it is immanent in the essence

Key Takeaway
Relations such as fatherhood are real, positive attributes distinct from the essence of the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you treat your relationships (like being a father, son, or friend) as mere labels or as real, positive attributes that define you? How would your interactions change if you truly believed they are real and will be accounted for?
?
Imam Razi proves that fatherhood is a positive attribute distinct from your essence. If that is true, then your role as a parent is not just a label but a real quality that Allah knows. Are you conscious that your relationships are real attributes that you will be questioned about? What is one concrete way you can live this truth today?
?
Jesus had no fatherhood or sonship to humans, yet he was a complete person. Does this make you reflect on your own identity being independent of relationships? Are you defined more by your relationships or by your essence in the sight of Allah? How can you balance both?
